Divine Decree
advanced
8 min
Imam al-Razi's Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Objection on Power and Independence
Imam al-Razi addresses an objection about the effect of power and shows it leads to denying Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi argues that acknowledging the necessity of the act upon the motive does not invalidate the effect of power, but rather negates independence, and how this leads to the collapse of Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Necessity of act on motive destroys Mu'tazilism, not just power's effect.

Imam al-Razi presents an objection from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argues that dependence on motive does not negate the effect of the servant's power—only independence. Al-Razi responds by reframing the debate: the issue is not effect but independence, and agreeing on that leads to the total rejection of Mu'tazilism. This passage is a masterclass in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. Abu al-Husayn claims that the act's dependence on the motive does not negate the power's effect, only its independence. He appeals to self-evident knowledge for the first point, not the second.
Note that the objection tries to preserve Mu'tazilism by separating effect from independence.
2
Al-Razi's redefinition of the issue
Al-Razi clarifies that his proof is not about effect but about negating independence, following the Master (al-Ash'ari) and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). This reframes the entire debate.
Al-Razi shifts the ground: the key issue is whether the servant is an independent agent.
3
Consequences if Abu al-Husayn agrees
If Abu al-Husayn agrees that independence is negated, then Mu'tazilism collapses. Al-Razi argues that there is no rational difference between commanding the servant to do God's act and commanding an act that necessarily follows from God's action.
In both cases, the servant cannot choose; thus Mu'tazilism's core tenets are undermined.
4
Three analogies to seal the argument
Al-Razi provides three analogies: (1) the servant's inability to do or leave, (2) punishment for what God originates versus punishment for what follows from God's origination, (3) the doer of evil versus the doer of what necessitates evil. All point to the same conclusion.
These analogies show that acknowledging necessity destroys any meaningful distinction.
5
Conclusion: Abu al-Husayn's deception
Al-Razi concludes that Abu al-Husayn was actually denying Mu'tazilism in this issue, and his claim of self-evident knowledge was a form of deception and obfuscation.
The final verdict exposes the opponent's inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الإمام لا يقال الاعتراف بتوقف صدور الفعل عن القادر على الداعي، ووجوب حصوله عند حصوله لا ينافي القول بأن قدرة العبد تؤثر في وجود الفعل، وإما ينافي استقلاله بالفاعلية، وهو إما ادعى العلم الضروري في الأول لا في الثاني . لأنا نقول نحن لا نستدل بالدليل المذكور لأجل بيان أن القدرة الحادثة غير مؤثرة، بل لبيان سلب الاستقلال كما هو مذهب الأستاذ وإمام الحرمين فإن كان أبو الحسين قد ساعدنا عليه فمرحباً بالوفاق، لكن يلزم منه فساد مذهب الاعتزال بالكلية لأنه لا فرق في العقل بين أن يأمر الله العبد بما يكون فعلاً الله تعالى وأن يأمره بفعل يجب حصوله عند فعل الله تعالى، ويمتنع حصوله عند عدمه، فإن المأمور على كلا التقديرين لا يكون متمكناً من الفعل والترك، ولا بين أن يعذب الله العبد على ما أوجده فيه، وأن بعذبه على ما يجب حصوله عند حصول ما أحدثه الله فيه ولا بين فاعل القبيح والظلم ، وفاعل ما يوجب القبيح والظلم فمن اعترف بوجوب حصول الفعل عند حصول الإرادة الحادثة، انسد عليه باب الاعتزال فظهر أن أبا الحسين كان المنكرين المذهب الاعتزال في هذه المسألة، وأن مبالغته في دعوى الضرورة فيها كانت على سبيل التغبية والتلبيس
Then Imam al-Razi said: It cannot be said that acknowledging the act's dependence on the motive and its necessity upon its occurrence does not negate the statement that the servant's power influences the existence of the act; rather it only negates his independence in agency. And he [Abu al-Husayn] claimed necessary knowledge in the first, not the second. To which we say: We do not use the mentioned proof to show that the originated power has no effect, but to negate independence, as is the school of the Master and Imam al-Haramayn. If Abu al-Husayn agrees with us on that, then welcome to agreement, but it entails the total corruption of Mu'tazilism because there is no difference in reason between God commanding the servant to do what is God's own act and commanding him to do an act that must occur when God does something and is impossible when He does not; in both cases the commanded one is not able to do or leave; nor between God punishing the servant for what He originated in him and punishing him for what must occur upon what He originates in him; nor between the doer of evil and injustice and the doer of what necessitates evil and injustice. So whoever acknowledges the necessary occurrence of the act upon the occurrence of originated will, the door of Mu'tazilism is closed upon him. So it becomes clear that Abu al-Husayn was among the deniers of the Mu'tazilite school in this issue, and that his exaggeration in claiming self-evidence was a form of deception and obfuscation.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection states that dependence on motive does not negate the effect of power, only independence. Imam al-Razi answers that the proof is not about effect but about independence. If Abu al-Husayn agrees, then Mu'tazilism collapses because there is no rational distinction between commanding God's own act and commanding an act that follows from God's action – in both, the servant is unable to choose. Therefore, acknowledging necessity of the act upon motive destroys Mu'tazilism, showing Abu al-Husayn's claim was deceptive.

Key Terms
استقلال
فاعلية
قدرة حادثة
إرادة حادثة
اعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully defends Mu'tazilism by showing that power can still be effective even if the act is necessary upon the motive.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the objection only works if one separates effect from independence, but that separation is untenable because it leads to the same consequences as direct divine action, thus collapsing Mu'tazilism.

Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Razi say is the consequence of acknowledging that the act must occur upon the occurrence of the originated will?
A) It proves the servant's power is effective
B) It closes the door of Mu'tazilism
C) It confirms Abu al-Husayn's position
D) It allows for free will

Key Takeaway
Acknowledging the necessity of the act upon the motive leads to the collapse of Mu'tazilism because it removes any meaningful distinction between God acting and the servant acting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your will is not independent—every motive you feel is created by God. When you sin, do you truly believe you could have chosen otherwise, or do you hide behind 'God made me do it'? What does your answer reveal about your view of accountability?
?
If there is no real difference between your act being directly God's act and your act being necessitated by God's creation of your will, how does that change the way you approach repentance? Do you repent as if you were the sole agent, or do you ask God to change your will?
?
This passage states that the door of Mu'tazilism is closed for anyone who admits the necessity of the act upon the motive. Does your heart admit that necessity? If so, how does that affect your trust in God's justice when you face trials that seem to result from your own choices?

Refuting the Later Mu'tazilite Interpretation of Necessity (Wujub)
Author refutes later Mu'tazilite interpretation of wujub at pure motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the later Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of necessity (wujub) as allowing possibility of leaving is self-defeating and leads either to jabr or to ignorance.

Core Idea
Necessity at pure motive either forces the act or is meaningless — there is no middle ground.

The later Mu'tazilites attempted to soften the doctrine of necessity (wujub) by claiming that even when motives are pure, the capable agent still retains the possibility of leaving the act. In this passage, the author critically examines this claim and exposes its logical flaw. He shows that either the act is necessitated with no possibility of leaving — which is precisely the jabr affirmed by Ahl al-Haqq — or the act is not necessitated, in which case the term 'necessity' loses its meaning and inference becomes impossible. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the Sunni position on divine action and human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Start by reading the passage where the author presents the later Mu'tazilite claim about wujub at pure motive. Notice the analogies they use (God's actions, Arab challenge).
They claim we can know a capable person acts while retaining possibility of leaving.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author then examines the claim by presenting two possibilities: either necessity forces the act (with no valid omission) or it does not. See how he handles each.
3
Understand the jabr implication
The first possibility leads to jabr as understood by Ahl al-Haqq, not the absolute jabr of the Mujabbira. Reflect on this distinction.
The decisive will being not the servant's will is the key.
4
See the inference structure
Finally, the author explains why the argument from non-production relies on necessity. Understand the logical inference from negation of consequent to negation of antecedent.
This shows how the Mu'tazilites' own reasoning undermines their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة أن معنى الوجوب عند خلوص الداعي، أنا نعلم أن القادر بفعله مع إمكان الترك، كما نعلم أن الله يثيب الأنبياء والأولياء بالجنة ويعاقب الكفار بالنار مع إمكان تركهما نعلم أن العرب لو قدروا على مثل القرآن مع توافر الدواعي وانتفاء الموانع لأتوا بمثله، ولا وجوب الإتيان بمثله بمعنى الذي ذكرنا لما عرفنا عجزه لجواز أن يقدروا ولا يأتوا به، وفيه نظر لأنه إما أن يلزم مع خلوص الداعي صدور الفعل من القادر . بحيث لا يصح منه الترك وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى أصل قدرته وإرادته فيتم ما ذكره الإمام من وجوب الفعل لظهور أن تلك الداعية والإرادة الجازمة اليستا بإرادة العبد، وهذا هو المعنى بالجبر، الذي يقول به أهل الحق ، ويلزم أبا الحسين لا الجبر المغلق الذي يقول به المجبرة. وبطلانه ضروري وإما أن لا يلزم فلا معنى لتسميته بالوجوب، ولا طريق إلى العلم بالصندور، بل هو رجم بالغيب، لأن المفروض تساوي الأمرين، وإما العلم فرع اعتقاد الوجوب، ألا يرى أنه إذا قيل من أين عرفت عجز المتحدين؟ قيل لأنه خلصت دواعيهم فلو قدروا لأتوا به، وهذا معني الوجوب لأنه استدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم، ولهذا يستدل بنفي الفعل عند تحقق القدرة على نفي الداعية وجزم الإرادة.
And some later Mu'tazilites claimed that the meaning of 'necessity' (wujub) at the purity of motive is that we know that the capable person does his act with the possibility of leaving, just as we know that God rewards prophets and saints with Paradise and punishes disbelievers with Hell with the possibility of leaving both; we know that if the Arabs were able to produce something like the Quran with abundant motives and absence of obstacles, they would have produced its like, and if there were no necessity of producing its like in the sense we mentioned, we would not know his inability because it is possible that they are able but do not produce it. There is examined: Because either the occurrence of the act from the capable follows from the purity of motive such that leaving is not valid although it is possible in itself and with respect to the origin of his power and will – then what Imam al-Razi mentioned about the necessity of the act is realized, because it appears that that motive and decisive will are not the servant's will, and that is the meaning of jabr that the People of Truth affirm, and it is binding on Abu al-Husayn, not the absolute jabr that the Mujabbira hold, the falsity of which is self-evident. Or it does not follow, then there is no meaning to calling it necessity, and no way to knowledge of the occurrence, but rather it is divination of the unseen, because the assumption is the equality of the two matters, but knowledge is a branch of belief in necessity. Do you not see that when it is said: 'How did you know the inability of the challengers?' It is said: 'Because their motives were pure, so if they were able, they would have produced it.' This is the meaning of necessity, because it is an inference from the negation of the consequent to the negation of the antecedent. For this reason, the negation of the act when power is verified is used to infer the negation of the motive and decisive will.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage refutes a later Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of wujub. The author presents their claim that necessity at pure motive is not absolute but allows possibility of leaving, using analogies like God's actions and the Arab challenge. Then the author examines: if necessity forces the act with no valid omission, then it is jabr as held by Ahl al-Haqq, and this is binding on Abu al-Husayn, not the absolute jabr. If it does not force, then calling it necessity is meaningless, and there is no way to know the act occurred except by guesswork. The author explains that the inference of inability from non-production relies on believing in necessity.

Key Terms
وجوب
داعي
جبر
إرادة جازمة
ضروري
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The later Mu'tazilites believed that wujub at pure motive allows the agent to both act and retain the possibility of not acting.
Correction: The author shows that either the act is forced (jabr) or the concept of wujub is meaningless and knowledge of the act is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the implication if necessity at pure motive does NOT force the act to occur?
A) It is true jabr
B) There is no meaning to necessity and no way to know the occurrence
C) It is the view of Ahl al-Haqq
D) It is the same as the Mujabbira

Key Takeaway
The later Mu'tazilite attempt to reconcile necessity with possibility of leaving fails because necessity at pure motive either compels the act (jabr) or provides no basis for knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself excusing a sin by thinking 'I couldn't help it,' are you aware that you are adopting the very notion of jabr that the author refutes? What will you do the next time such an excuse arises to take full responsibility?
?
The author argues that without necessity at pure motive, knowledge of an action becomes guesswork. In your life, do you rely on your own reasoning to decide moral matters without checking revelation? Where specifically are you trusting yourself more than the Quran?
?
If the Mu'tazilite position leads to ignorance, then relying solely on your intellect to understand divine decree is insufficient. What is one area of divine decree where you must submit to the clear texts even if your mind struggles? Make a concrete decision to study the texts on that issue.

The Mu'tazilite Proof from Moral Distinctions and the Ash'ari Response
Mu'tazilite argument from moral distinctions and Ash'ari refutation using kasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational argument for free will based on moral distinctions and the Ash'ari refutation using the concept of kasb.

Core Idea
Moral distinctions do not require human independence; acquisition (kasb) suffices.

This passage presents a classical theological debate: the Mu'tazilites argue that without human free will, moral and religious concepts lose meaning. The Ash'ari response counters that the concept of kasb (acquisition) preserves moral accountability without requiring full independence, and that the Mu'tazilite position itself faces a similar problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Read the first part of the passage, which lists many things that would be invalidated if the servant were not independent. Note the strong reliance on intuitive distinctions.
The argument is based on the idea that we intuitively perceive a difference between voluntary and involuntary actions.
2
Identify the list of invalidations
The passage enumerates praise and blame, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, prophethood, scriptures, distinction between faith and disbelief, good and evil, prophet's act vs devil's act, words of glorification vs delirium, and acts according to one's will vs another's will.
This list is comprehensive, covering religious, moral, and intuitive distinctions.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response via kasb
The response states that the Ash'ari position (which makes the servant's act related to his power and will through kasb) does not lead to invalidation. The act occurs by acquisition consequent upon the servant's resolve, preserving moral responsibility.
Kasb means the servant 'acquires' the act created by Allah, linking intention and action.
4
Notice the counter-argument
The response also points out that the Mu'tazilite position itself faces a similar problem because their system entails necessity or impossibility based on a determining factor and pre-eternal knowledge, which could also undermine moral distinctions.
This turns the objection back on the Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أنه لولا استقلال العبد لبطل المدح والذم والأمر والنهي والثواب والعقاب وفوائد الوعد والوعيد، وإرسال الرسل، وإنزال الكتب، والفرق بين الكفر والإيمان والإساءة والإحسان وفعل النبي والشيطان، وكلمات التسبيح والهذيان وكذا بين ما يقع بأعضاء العبد على وفق إرادته وإرادة غيره مع أن التفرقة مدركة بالوجدان. والجواب عن الأول أنه لا إشكال على من يجعل فعل العبد متعلقاً لقدرته وإرادته واقعاً بكسبه وعقيب عزمه، ولو لزم فعل المعتزلة أيضاً لوجوب الفعل أو امتناعه بناء على المرجح الموجب، والعلم الأزلي وجوداً وعدماً.
First: If the servant's independence (of action) did not exist, then praise and blame, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, benefits of promise and threat, sending of messengers, revelation of books, distinction between disbelief and faith, evil and good, the act of the prophet and the devil, words of glorification and delirium, and likewise between what occurs through the servant's limbs according to his will and the will of another would all be invalidated, while the distinction is perceived intuitively. And the answer to the first is that there is no problem for one who makes the servant's act related to his power and will, occurring by his acquisition (kasb) and consequent upon his resolve, even if it would entail the action of the Mu'tazilites as well due to the necessity or impossibility of the act based on the determining factor and pre-eternal knowledge of existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational proof of the Mu'tazilites for human free will, followed by an Ash'ari answer. The Mu'tazilite argument is that if humans do not independently create their actions, then all moral and religious distinctions (praise/blame, commands, rewards, etc.) become meaningless, and intuitive distinctions would be lost. The Ash'ari answer reframes the issue: the position that makes the servant's act occur by acquisition (kasb) after his resolve does not lead to these problems. It also points out that the Mu'tazilites themselves might face a similar issue because their own system entails necessity or impossibility based on a determining factor and pre-eternal knowledge, which could also undermine moral distinctions. Thus the objection is neutralized.

Key Terms
استقلال العبد
كسب
مدح وذم
وجوب الفعل
علم أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position on kasb denies any role for human will; it reduces all actions to divine compulsion.
Correction: The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb affirms a real connection between the servant's intention (resolve) and the act, such that the act is acquired by the servant and accordingly praise or blame apply.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument, what would be invalidated if the servant did not independently create his actions?
A) Only divine attributes
B) Praise and blame, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, etc.
C) Only prophethood
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof from moral distinctions is refuted by the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb, which maintains moral accountability without requiring human independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel proud of a good deed you have done, do you attribute that success to your own independent effort or to Allah's creation and your acquisition? How does your answer change your sense of gratitude and humility?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that without free will, praise and blame are meaningless. Yet the Ash'aris affirm praise and blame through kasb. In your own life, when you fail in a religious duty, do you blame yourself or feel that 'it was written'? How does the doctrine of kasb affect your repentance and hope for change?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take is acquired by you through Allah's creation, would that make you more humble in success and more hopeful in failure? Why or why not? What one thing can you do today to act on that belief?

Distinguishing Creation from Performance: The Ash'ari Response to the Problem of Evil
Mu'tazilite proof from divine wisdom and Ash'ari distinction between creation and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God cannot create evil acts and the Ash'ari counter-argument that distinguishes the creation of an act from its performance, thereby preserving divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Evil actions are ugly in performance, not in creation — God creates, servants perform.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God is perfectly wise, He cannot create acts that are inherently ugly, such as oppression or polytheism. The Ash'ari response concedes that these acts are rationally ugly but makes a subtle distinction: the ugliness lies in the performance of the act by the servant, not in God's creation of it. This distinction allows the Ash'ari theologian to affirm both God's creative power and His wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage has two distinct parts: the Mu'tazilite claim and the Ash'ari response.
The Arabic uses concise technical terms — pay attention to القبيح and the contrast between خلق and فعل.
2
Identify the proof and its assumption
Identify the Mu'tazilite proof: certain actions are ugly, and the Wise cannot create ugly things. Ask yourself: what assumption does this proof rely on? (It assumes that creating an ugly act makes the creator responsible for ugliness.)
3
Analyze the Ash'ari distinction
Focus on the phrase 'القبيح فعل القبيح لا خلقه'. The Ash'ari concedes rational ugliness but shifts the locus of ugliness from creation (khalq) to performance (fi'l). Think about how this saves God from being the agent of evil.
This distinction is a key tool in Ash'ari theology to solve the problem of evil.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction allows the Ash'ari to affirm both God's omnipotence (He creates all acts) and His justice (the moral quality belongs to the servant's performance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن من الأفعال قبائح يقبح من الحكيم خلقها كالظلم والشرك، وإثبات الولد ونحو ذلك . وعن الثاني : بعد تسليم القبح العقلي. أن القبيح فعل القبيح لا خلقه .
Second: Among actions are ugly things which it is ugly for the Wise to create, such as oppression, polytheism, attributing a son, and the like. And the answer to the second: After conceding rational ugliness, that the ugly is the doing of the ugly, not its creation.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite rational proof and its Ash'ari answer. The proof claims that certain actions (oppression, polytheism, etc.) are inherently ugly, and it would be ugly for the Wise God to create them. The Ash'ari answer first concedes that these actions are rationally ugly (qabīḥ ʿaqlī) but then makes a crucial distinction: the ugliness pertains to the performance (fiʿl) of the ugly act, not to its creation (khalq). God creates the act as an entity, but the servant performs it; the moral quality attaches to the performance, not the creation. Thus God is not tainted by the ugliness.

Key Terms
قبيح
حكيم
خلق
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position makes God responsible for evil because He creates all actions.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer shows that God creates the act as a neutral entity; the moral ugliness comes from the servant's performance, not from God's creation.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari answer distinguish between God's creation of an ugly act and the servant's performance of it?
A) God does not create ugly acts
B) The ugliness is in the creation, not the act
C) The ugliness is in the performance (fiʿl), not the creation (khalq)
D) All acts are good

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response preserves God's wisdom by locating ugliness in the servant's performance, not in God's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God created this action, so why is He punishing me?' How does this lesson force you to own your choices instead of shifting blame?
?
If the ugliness of an act lies entirely in your performance, not in God's creation, then what does that mean for how you approach repentance? Are you willing to admit that you are the sole source of the evil in your deeds?
?
Picture a moment tomorrow when you are tempted to commit a known sin. How will the distinction between God's creation and your performance change the way you pray for strength to resist?

intermediate

The Mu'tazilite Proof from Will-Act Necessity and the Ash'ari Answer
Mu'tazilite proof and Ash'ari answer on will-act correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the necessary correlation between will and act proves human agency, and the Ash'ari response that this correlation is maintained by Allah's habitual pattern (ʿāda).

Core Idea
The observed link between will and act reflects Allah's habit, not human causation.

This lesson examines a classic Mu'tazilite proof for human free will: if an act necessarily follows the servant's will, then God cannot be its sole creator. The argument seems strong, but the Ash'ari answer introduces a key concept—divine habit (ʿāda)—which preserves God's exclusive creative power while explaining the observed regularity. We will analyze both the proof and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Mu'tazilite premise: the servant's act necessarily occurs according to his will. If God were the sole creator, this correlation could be broken. Since it is not broken, the servant must be the agent.
Notice the logical flow: observed correlation → if God creates, chance of break → no break → servant creates.
2
Read the Ash'ari answer
The answer concedes the necessity of occurrence but reinterprets its cause: it is according to God's will which habitually agrees with the servant's will.
The key term is 'ʿādatan' (habitually).
3
Compare the two positions
Ask yourself what each view gains and loses. The Mu'tazilite secures human agency but risks limiting God's power. The Ash'ari preserves divine omnipotence but must explain regularity without necessitating it.
Think about the theological stakes.
4
Reflect on divine habit (ʿāda)
Consider how ʿāda differs from a law of nature. In Ash'ari theology, habits are regular but not necessary—God could break them, but He usually does not.
This concept is central to the Ash'ari response to objections about causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن فعل العبد واجب الوقوع على وفق إرادته، فلو كان بإيجاد الله لما كان كذلك بجواز أن لا يحدثه عند إرادته، ويحدثه عند كراهته . وعن الثالث: أنه لو سلم وجوب الوقوع فعلى وفق إرادة الله الموافقة لإرادة العبد عادة .
Third: That the servant's act necessarily occurs according to his will, so if it were by God's creation, it would not be so because it would be possible that He does not bring it about when the servant wills it, and brings it about when he dislikes it. And the answer to the third: That even if the necessity of occurrence is granted, it is according to God's will which habitually agrees with the servant's will.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazilite rational proof and its Ash'ari answer. The proof is based on the observed correlation between the servant's will and the occurrence of the act. If God were the sole creator, this correlation could be broken: God might not create the act when the servant wills it, or create it when the servant dislikes it. But since the correlation is observed, it must be that the servant is the agent. The Ash'ari answer concedes that the act necessarily occurs according to the servant's will, but explains that this necessity is due to God's will, which habitually (ʿādatan) agrees with the servant's will. In other words, God has established a customary pattern (ʿāda) of creating the act when the servant wills it, preserving the correlation while maintaining divine creation.

Key Terms
وجوب الوقوع
إرادة
عادة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view implies that God could break the correlation at any moment, making the world unpredictable.
Correction: The concept of divine habit (ʿāda) ensures regular correlation without necessitating it. God usually maintains the pattern, so the world is orderly, but the correlation is not necessary and could be broken by a miracle.

Checkpoint
What is the key concept in the Ash'ari response to the third argument?
A) Divine determinism
B) Habitual agreement (ʿāda)
C) The servant's absolute power
D) Miraculous intervention

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response preserves divine causality by appealing to Allah's habitual pattern, showing that observed regularities do not prove independent human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you will an action and it happens, do you feel as though you caused it, or do you recognize that Allah habitually creates it at that moment? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly believed the latter?
?
Consider a time you succeeded despite wanting to fail, or failed despite wanting to succeed. In light of this lesson, can you see the Ash'ari answer as explaining both outcomes as equally tied to Allah's habit? What does that do to your pride or despair?
?
If the link between your will and your act is only a divine habit, how does that affect your view of personal responsibility? Are you more motivated to improve your will, knowing that Allah's habit follows it, or does it make you feel passive? Examine your heart honestly.

The Mu'tazilite Proof from Divine Description and Its Dismissal
Claim that if God creates all actions, He would be described by sins, and the dismissive response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational proof that if God creates all actions He would be described by them, and the Ash'ari dismissal of this argument as foolish or insolent.

Core Idea
God's creation of an act does not mean He performs it.

This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite proof against the Ash'ari position that God creates all human actions. The proof argues that if God creates an action, He must be described by that action, leading to absurd conclusions about God. The Ash'ari response is not a detailed refutation but a blunt dismissal, indicating that the argument is considered too weak to merit serious engagement. Let's examine the proof and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement, an explanation, and a dismissive response.
The proof uses an extended list of attributes to emphasize the absurdity.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the argument: If God creates all actions, then He would be described by them because the agent is described by his act. Thus, God would be a disbeliever, sinner, etc. Identify the premise and conclusion.
The chain relies on the assumption that creating an act implies performing it.
3
Reflect on the dismissive response
Note that the Ash'ari answer does not refute the premise logically but calls it foolish or insolent. Consider why the author might choose such a response.
This implies the argument is seen as obviously flawed within the Ash'ari framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الله خالقاً لأفعال المخلوقين لصح اتصافه بها، إذ لا معنى للكافر إلا فاعل الكفر، فيكون كافراً ظالماً فاسقاً أكلاً شارباً قائماً قاعداً إلى ما لا يحصى . وعن الرابع : أنه حماقة أو وقاحة .
Fourth: If God were the creator of the actions of creatures, then it would be valid for Him to be described by them, since a disbeliever is only the doer of disbelief; then He would be a disbeliever, unjust, sinful, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, and so on ad infinitum. And the answer to the fourth: That it is foolishness or insolence.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite rational proof and its Ash'ari answer. The proof extends the idea: if God creates all acts, He must be described by those acts, since the agent is described by his action. Thus God would be a disbeliever, sinner, etc. The Ash'ari answer does not engage logically but dismisses the argument as foolishness (ḥamāqa) or insolence (waqāḥa). This implies that the argument is considered absurd because God's creation of an act does not entail that He performs it or is described by its moral quality—a point established in the earlier distinction between creation and performance. The dismissal suggests the argument is so weak it does not warrant serious refutation.

Key Terms
خالق
اتصاف
كافر
حماقة
وقاحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creation of an act makes Him morally responsible for that act.
Correction: The Ash'ari position distinguishes between creating an act and performing it; creation does not entail moral description.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari answer characterize the fourth argument?
A) As a valid logical proof
B) As foolishness or insolence
C) As based on scripture
D) As requiring elaboration

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response does not engage with the logic of the Mu'tazilite proof but dismisses it as foolish, reflecting a confidence that the premise is fundamentally flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God created your actions, why do you still feel shame when you sin? What does that shame reveal about the nature of responsibility?
?
Are you willing to accept absolute responsibility for your choices, even though every action is ultimately created by God? What is holding you back from that acceptance?

The First Mu'tazilite Proof: Praise and Blame Require Agency
The advanced Mu'tazilites' first rational proof for human agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof of the advanced Mu'tazilites that without independent human agency, praise and blame become meaningless.

Core Idea
If we are not agents, moral evaluation collapses.

The advanced Mu'tazilites built their case for human free will on five rational proofs. The first and strongest of these is based on the fact that we praise and blame people for their actions. This lesson presents that argument in its original form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The Mu'tazilites state that if humans are not independent originators, praise and blame are invalid.
2
Understand the reasoning
They argue that praise/blame apply only to what is under one's power and choice.
3
Note the context
This is the first of five proofs, considered their greatest pillar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقدمون من المعتزلة على أن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعاله نظري، فتمسكوا بوجوه عقلية ونقلية . أما العقليات فمرجعها إلى خمسة . الأول : وهو عمدتهم الكبرى، وعروتهم الوثقى، أنه لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعاله بالاستقلال لزم فسادات منها بطلان المدح والذم عليها . إذ لا معنى للمدح والذم على ما ليس بفعل له، ولا واقع بقدرته واختياره. ورد بالمنع بل ربما يمدح أو يذم على ما هو محل له كالحسن والقبح واعتدال القامة، وإفراط القصر. ومنها بطلان التكاليف من الأوامر والنواهي، إذ لا معنى للأمر بما لا يكون فعلاً للمأمور، ولا يدخل في قدرته بل ما لا يطيقه المرض ونحوه حتى أن العقلاء يتعجبون منه، وينسبون الأمر إلى الحمق والجنون بمنزلة من يطلب من المسان خلق الحيوان، والطيران إلى السماء بل من الجماد المشي على الأرض والصعود في الهواء، وكذا الثواب والعقاب إذ لا وجه للثواب والعقاب على ما هو بخلق المثيب والمعاقب حتى أن من يعاقب على ما يخلقه كان أشد ضرراً على العبد من الشيطان، وأحق منه بالذم إذ ليس منه إلا الوسوسة والتزيين ومنها بطلان فوائد الوعد والوعيد وإرسال
The advanced Mu'tazilites hold that knowledge of the servant being the originator of his actions is theoretical, so they cling to rational and transmitted proofs. As for rational proofs, they refer to five. First: Which is their greater pillar and firmest handle, that if the servant were not the independent originator of his actions, corruptions would follow, including the invalidation of praise and blame for them. For there is no meaning to praise and blame for what is not his act and does not occur by his power and choice. It was refuted by rejection: rather, one may be praised or blamed for what belongs to him, such as beauty and ugliness, moderate stature, and excessive shortness. Among them is the invalidation of obligations of commands and prohibitions, for there is no meaning to commanding what is not an act of the commanded and does not fall under his power, rather what he cannot bear such as illness and the like, so that rational people wonder at it and attribute the command to foolishness and madness, like one who asks an old man to create animals and fly to the sky, or asks a lifeless object to walk on earth and rise in the air. Likewise reward and punishment, for there is no basis for reward and punishment for what is the creation of the rewarder and punisher, so that he who punishes for what he creates is more harmful to the servant than Satan and more deserving of blame, for from Satan is only whispering and embellishment. Among them is the invalidation of benefits of promise and threat, and sending
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage introduces the rational proofs of the advanced Mu'tazilites and elaborates the first proof in greater detail. It begins by stating that the advanced Mu'tazilites consider knowledge of human agency to be theoretical, so they provide both rational and transmitted evidences. The rational proofs are said to be five, and this is the first. It repeats the earlier argument about the invalidation of praise and blame, commands, prohibitions, rewards, punishments, and benefits of promise and threat if humans do not independently create their actions. However, it adds new dimensions: it denies that praise and blame apply only to acts; they can apply to non-voluntary attributes like beauty or stature. It also emphasizes the absurdity of commanding what is impossible, using colorful examples. The refutation ('ورد بالمنع') is embedded within the presentation, indicating that the argument is being countered by pointing out that these moral concepts can apply without independent agency. The passage cuts off mid-sentence at 'وإرسال', suggesting a continuation onto the next page.

Key Terms
المتقدمون من المعتزلة
عمدتهم الكبرى
عروتهم الوثقى
مدح وذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite proof is unanswerable.
Correction: While it appears strong, it is refuted by showing that praise and blame can attach to non-voluntary attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the first Mu'tazilite proof?
A) Humans are not free
B) Praise and blame require independent agency
C) Actions are created by God
D) Knowledge of agency is innate

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that without human agency, moral evaluations like praise and blame lose their meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you blame someone for a mistake, do you first consider whether they had the power and choice to do otherwise? How does this lesson challenge your habit of blaming others, and what will you do the next time you are tempted to blame without reflection?

?
Think of a time you defended your own action by saying 'I had no choice.' According to this lesson, does that statement make moral evaluation meaningless? What will you do the next time you catch yourself excusing your behavior with that phrase?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Proof: Praise for Non-Voluntary Attributes
The refutation of the first Mu'tazilite proof using non-voluntary attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite proof from praise and blame is refuted by pointing out that praise can be directed at non-voluntary attributes.

Core Idea
One can be praised for beauty or blamed for ugliness, which are not acts.

The refutation of the first Mu'tazilite proof is both straightforward and profound. It shows that the link between agency and moral evaluation is not as tight as the Mu'tazilites assumed. This lesson examines the refutation and its implications for obligations, rewards, and punishments.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
The response denies the premise by giving counterexamples: praise/blame for beauty, stature, etc.
2
Consider the additional corruptions
The passage then lists other corruptions (commands, rewards) that would follow if the proof were valid.
3
Reflect on the method
This refutation is a 'man' (rejection) that challenges the initial claim directly.

The Mu'tazilite First Proof and Its Refutation
The author presents the first Mu'tazilite proof of five corruptions and then refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite first proof for human free will (based on five corruptions) and the Ash'ari refutation using acquisition (kash) and the distinction between locus and agency.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued that without independent human agency, moral and religious distinctions collapse; the Ash'ari response affirms acquisition while denying independent creation.

This passage presents the cornerstone Mu'tazilite argument for human free will. They claim that if humans are not independent creators of their actions, several absurdities follow. The Ash'ari response, however, shows that these absurdities only apply to the Jabariyya who deny any human role, not to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition, which preserves moral accountability while affirming divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the Mu'tazilite premise and the five listed corruptions.
The first corruption includes an immediate refutation in parentheses.
2
Identify the five corruptions
List each corruption: invalidation of praise/blame, obligations, promise/threat, distinction between good/evil acts, and distinction between voluntary/involuntary movements.
3
Analyze the immediate refutation of the first corruption
The refutation states that praise/blame can be based on being the locus (maḥall) rather than the agent.
Example: praising beauty or blaming ugliness.
4
Understand the general answer
The answer distinguishes between the Jabariyya (who deny all human power) and the Ash'ari position (which affirms acquisition and efficacy with a divine determinant).
Also note that some corruptions apply to the Mu'tazila themselves.
5
Reflect on the outcome
Consider how the Ash'ari view avoids the absurdities while maintaining divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقدمون من المعتزلة على أن العلم بكون العبد موجداً لأفعاله نظري، فتمسكوا بوجوه عقلية ونقلية .
أما العقليات فمرجعها إلى خمسة .
الأول : وهو عمدتهم الكبرى، وعروتهم الوثقى، أنه لو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعاله بالاستقلال لزم فسادات منها بطلان المدح والذم عليها . (إذ لا معنى للمدح والذم على ما ليس بفعل له ، ولا واقع بقدرته واختياره) . ورد بالمنع بل ربما يمدح أو يذم على ما هو محل له كالحسن والقبح واعتدال القامة، وإفراط القصر. ومنها بطلان التكاليف من الأوامر والنواهي، إذ لا معنى للأمر بما لا يكون فعلاً للمأمور، ولا يدخل في قدرته بل ما لا يطيقه المرض ونحوه حتى أن العقلاء يتعجبون منه، وينسبون الأمر إلى الحمق والجنون بمنزلة من يطلب من المسان خلق الحيوان، والطيران إلى السماء بل من الجماد المشي على الأرض والصعود في الهواء، وكذا الثواب والعقاب إذ لا وجه للثواب والعقاب على ما هو بخلق المثيب والمعاقب حتى أن من يعاقب على ما يخلقه كان أشد ضرراً على العبد من الشيطان، وأحق منه بالذم إذ ليس منه إلا الوسوسة والتزيين ومنها بطلان فوائد الوعد والوعيد وإرسال الرسل، وبعثة الأنبياء، وإنزال الكتب من السماء، إذ لا يظهر للترغيب والترهيب، والحث على تحصيل الكمالات، وإزالة الرذائل ونحو ذلك فائدة ، إلا إذا كان بقدرة العبد وإرادته تأثير في أفعاله ويتولى مباشرتها باستقلاله ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الأفعال التي تطابق العقل والشرع على استحسانها واستحقاقها المدح في العاجل والثواب في الآجل، والتي ليست كذلك كالكفر والإيمان وكالإساءة إلى الفقراء والإحسان، وكفعل النبي من الهداية ، والإرشاد وتمهيد قواعد الخيرات، وفعل إبليس من الإضلال والإغواء، وتزيين الشرور والشهوات وكالتكلم بالتسبيحات والدعوات المترتب عليها الثواب باستجابة والتكلم بالهذيانات والفحش والهجاء التي لا تورث إلا اللوم والعقاب ، لأن الكل بخلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للعبد، ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الحركات التي تظهر من أعضاء العبد بقدرته وإرادته، والتي تظهر منها بقدرة الغير وإرادته كما إذا حرك زيد يد عمرو مثلاً مع أن كل أحد يفرق بينهما بالضرورة .
والجواب عن الكل، أنه إنما يرد على المجبرة النافين لقدرة العبد واختياره، لا على من يجعل فعله متعلقاً بقدرته وإرادته واقعاً بكسبه وعقيب عزمه، وإن كان بخلق الله تعالى عز وجل، ولا على من يجعل قدرته مؤثرة لكن لا بالاستقلال بل بمرجح هو بمحض خلق الله تعالى، على أن من الفسادات ما يلزم المعتزلة أيضاً، كبطلان استقلال العبد بناء على وجوب الفعل وامتناعه لوجود المرجح أو عدمه، وتعلق علم الله بوقوعه أو لا وقوعه، ومنها ما يندفع بطريق آخر، فإن المدح والذم قد يكون باعتبار المحلية دون الفاعلية كالمدح والذم بالحسن والقبح وسائر الغرائز، وأن
The early Mu'tazila hold that knowledge that the servant is the creator of his actions is theoretical, so they adhered to intellectual and transmitted proofs. As for the intellectual proofs, they reduce to five. The first: and it is their major pillar and their strongest handle, that if the servant were not the independent creator of his actions, then corruptions would follow, among them: the invalidation of praise and blame upon them. (For there is no meaning to praise or blame for what is not his action, and does not occur by his power and choice.) And it is refuted by denial; rather, one may be praised or blamed for that of which he is the locus, like beauty and ugliness, moderate stature, and excessive shortness. And among them: the invalidation of the obligations of commands and prohibitions, for there is no meaning to commanding what is not an action of the commanded and does not enter under his power, but rather what he cannot bear, like illness and the like, so that rational people wonder at it and ascribe the command to foolishness and madness, like one who demands from an old man the creation of an animal, or flying to heaven, or from an inanimate object walking on earth and ascending in the air. And likewise reward and punishment, for there is no basis for reward or punishment for what is the creation of the rewarder or punisher, such that one who punishes for what he himself created is more harmful to the servant than Satan, and more deserving of blame, since nothing comes from Satan except whispering and embellishment. And among them: the invalidation of the benefits of promise and threat, sending messengers, dispatching prophets, and sending down books from heaven, for there appears no benefit in encouragement and deterrence, urging towards acquiring perfections, removing vices and the like, unless the servant has influence in his actions by his power and will, and undertakes their direct performance independently. And among them: the invalidation of the distinction between actions that intellect and law agree upon approving and deserving praise in the immediate and reward in the hereafter, and those that are not such, like disbelief and faith, like harming the poor and doing good, like the action of the prophet in guidance, direction, and laying down the foundations of good deeds, and the action of Iblis in misleading, leading astray, embellishing evils and desires, and like speaking the words of glorification and supplication on which reward follows by response, and speaking nonsense, obscenity, and satire which only bring blame and punishment, because all is by the creation of God Most High without any influence of the servant. And among them: the invalidation of the distinction between movements that appear from the servant's limbs by his power and will, and those that appear from them by the power and will of another, as when Zayd moves Amr's hand, for example, even though everyone necessarily distinguishes between them. And the answer to all: that it only applies to the Jabariyya who deny the servant's power and choice, not to those who make his action related to his power and will, occurring by his acquisition and consequent to his resolution, even if it is by the creation of God Most High, nor to those who make his power effective but not independently, rather by a determinant which is purely by the creation of God Most High. Moreover, some of the corruptions also apply to the Mu'tazila themselves, such as the invalidation of the servant's independence based on the necessitation and impossibility of the act due to the presence or absence of the determinant, and God's knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence. And some of them are repelled by another way: for praise and blame may be based on the locus rather than agency, like praise and blame for beauty and ugliness and other innate qualities, and that
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the first intellectual proof of the Mu'tazila regarding human free will and then provides the Ash'ari refutation. The Mu'tazila begin by asserting that knowledge of the servant being the creator (mūjid) of his actions is theoretical (naẓarī), so they rely on rational arguments. They claim there are five intellectual proofs, but here they present only the first, which is their strongest: if the servant were not the independent creator (bi-l-istiqlāl) of his actions, then several corruptions (fasādāt) would necessarily follow. Five corruptions are enumerated: (1) Invalidation of praise and blame, since one cannot be praised or blamed for what is not his own act. (2) Invalidation of religious obligations (takālīf), as commanding what is not within one's power is absurd and similar to demanding the impossible from the elderly or inanimate objects; similarly, reward and punishment would be meaningless if God Himself created the acts. (3) Invalidation of the benefits of promise and threat, sending prophets, and revelation, since these would have no purpose if human actions were not under human power. (4) Invalidation of the distinction between good and evil acts (e.g., faith vs. disbelief, prophecy vs. Iblis's acts) because all would be equally God's creation without human contribution. (5) Invalidation of the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements (e.g., one's own hand movement vs. being moved by another), though everyone intuitively distinguishes them. Immediately after the first corruption, the author inserts a quick refutation: praise and blame can be based on being the locus (maḥall) rather than the agent, as in praising beauty or blaming ugliness. Then the comprehensive answer (al-jawāb ʿan al-kull) is given: this proof only applies to the Jabariyya who deny all human power and choice, not to the Ash'ari position that affirms acquisition (kash) and efficacy (taʾthīr) of human power with a divine determinant (murjīḥ). Moreover, some of the corruptions also apply to the Mu'tazila themselves, such as the problem of determinism (wujūb al-fiʿl wa-imtināʿuh) given God's knowledge and the necessity of the determinant. Finally, the author notes that praise and blame can be based on the locus (maḥalliyya) rather than agency (fāʿiliyya), as with innate qualities. The passage ends mid-sentence at 'and that' (wa-an), indicating continuation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first corruption listed by the Mu'tazila, and how is it refuted?
A) Invalidation of obligations; refuted by saying obligations apply only to the able.
B) Invalidation of praise and blame; refuted by saying praise/blame can be based on the locus rather than agency.
C) Invalidation of promise and threat; refuted by saying God's actions are purposeful.
D) Invalidation of voluntary vs. involuntary movement; refuted by saying distinctions are by divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite first proof argues that without independent human agency, five corruptions follow; the Ash'ari response affirms human acquisition (kash) while denying independence, thus preserving moral responsibility and divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you committed that you still blame yourself for. Did you truly believe you could have done otherwise, or did you feel compelled? How does the Ash'ari understanding of acquisition—that God creates your action while you acquire it—affect your sense of responsibility? Will you truly repent, relying on God's mercy, or will you continue to excuse yourself?
?
Have you ever felt that a command of Allah was too difficult, as if commanding the impossible? How does this lesson help you see that your ability to obey is from Allah, yet you are still accountable for your intention and effort? What is one command you have been neglecting, and what step will you take today to act on it, seeking Allah's help?
?
Reflect on a situation where you felt your action was forced by circumstances—like being pressured to lie or cheat. Did you still feel morally responsible? After this lesson, will you examine your intentions more carefully, knowing that your acquisition (kash) involves your will even while God creates? What will you do the next time you face such pressure?

The Three Objections Against Ash'ari Kasb
Lists three objections to Ash'ari kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections raised against the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari view on human action appears to collapse moral and prophetic distinctions.

This passage lists the key objections that opponents (especially Mu'tazilites) raise against the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions while humans acquire them. Before examining the answer, it is crucial to grasp the force of these objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of objections
Read the Arabic passage from 'الرسل' to 'بالضرورة'. Notice the three distinct arguments introduced by 'منها'.
The objections are presented as necessary consequences (فساد) of denying human power.
2
Identify each objection
Identify the three objections: (1) futility of prophethood and reward/punishment, (2) collapse of moral distinction between good and evil, (3) inability to distinguish voluntary from coerced actions.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why each objection is considered a problem for the Ash'ari view. Think about the role of human power in moral responsibility.
The objections assume that if God creates all actions, human choice is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرسل، وبعثة الأنبياء، وإنزال الكتب من السماء، إذ لا يظهر للترغيب والترهيب، والحث على تحصيل الكمالات، وإزالة الرذائل ونحو ذلك فائدة، إلا إذا كان بقدرة العبد وإرادته تأثير في أفعاله ويتولى مباشرتها باستقلاله ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الأفعال التي تطابق العقل والشرع على استحسانها واستحقاقها المدح في العاجل والثواب في الآجل، والتي ليست كذلك كالكفر والإيمان وكالإساءة إلى الفقراء والإحسان، وكفعل النبي من الهداية والإرشاد وتمهيد قواعد الخيرات، وفعل إبليس من الإضلال والإغواء، وتزيين الشرور والشهوات وكالتكلم بالتسبيحات والدعوات المترتب عليها الثواب باستجابة والتكلم بالهذيانات والفحش والهجاء التي لا تورث إلا اللوم والعقاب، لأن الكل بخلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للعبد، ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الحركات التي تظهر من أعضاء العبد بقدرته وإرادته، والتي تظهر منها بقدرة الغير وإرادته كما إذا حرك زيد يد عمرو مثلاً مع أن كل أحد يفرق بينهما بالضرورة. والجواب عن الكل، أنه إنما يرد على المجبرة النافين لقدرة العبد واختياره، لا على من يجعل فعله متعلقاً بقدرته وإرادته واقعاً بكسبه وعقيب عزمه، وإن كان بخلق الله تعالى عز وجل، ولا على من يجعل قدرته مؤثرة لكن لا بالاستقلال بل بمرجح هو بمحض خلق الله تعالى، على أن من الفسادات ما يلزم المعتزلة أيضاً، كبطلان استقلال العبد بناء على وجوب الفعل وامتناعه لوجود المرجح أو عدمه، وتعلق علم الله بوقوعه أو لا وقوعه، ومنها ما يندفع بطريق آخر، فإن المدح والذم قد يكون باعتبار المحلية دون الفاعلية كالمدح والذم بالحسن والقبح وسائر الغرائز، وأن الثواب والعقاب أيضاً، لما فعل الله وتصرفاً فيها هو حقه لم يتوجه سؤال لميته كما لا يقال لم خلق الإحراق عقيب مس النار، وأن التكليف والبعثة والتهديد والوعيد والوعد، ونحو ذلك، قد يكون دواعي إلى الفعل أو الترك فيخلقه الله تعالى، وأن عدم افتراق الفعلين في المخلوقية الله تعالى لا ينافي افتراقها بوجوه أخر.
The messengers, sending of prophets, and revelation of books from heaven, because there does not appear for encouragement and deterrence, urging the acquisition of perfections and removal of vices and the like any benefit, except if the servant's power and will have an effect in his actions and he undertakes their direct execution independently. And among them is the invalidity of the distinction between actions that agree with reason and revelation as to their approval and their deserving praise in the present and reward in the future, and those that are not so, such as disbelief and faith, and such as injury to the poor and beneficence, and such as the act of the Prophet in guidance and direction and laying down the foundations of good deeds, and the act of Iblis in misguidance and seduction and adorning evils and desires, and such as speaking with glorifications and supplications upon which reward is contingent through response, and speaking with nonsense, obscenity, and satire which yield nothing but blame and punishment, because all are by the creation of God Most High without any effect of the servant. And among them is the invalidity of the distinction between movements that appear from the servant's limbs by his power and will, and those that appear from his limbs by the power and will of another, such as when Zayd moves 'Amr's hand, for example, while everyone necessarily distinguishes between them. And the answer to all is that it applies only to the Jabrites who deny the servant's power and choice, not to one who makes his action attached to his power and will, occurring by his kasb and subsequent to his resolve, even though it is by the creation of God Most High, and not to one who makes his power effective but not independently, rather by a preponderant which is by sheer creation of God Most High. Moreover, among the corruptions [that follow from this] are those that also follow upon the Mu'tazilites, such as the invalidity of the servant's independence based on the necessity or impossibility of the act due to the existence or non-existence of the preponderant, and the connection of God's knowledge to its occurrence or non-occurrence. And among them are those that are repelled by another way, for praise and blame may be with respect to the locus rather than the agent, like praise and blame for beauty and ugliness and other natural traits. And reward and punishment also, when God does and disposes in it as His right, no question of its 'why' is directed, just as it is not said why He created burning following contact with fire. And that obligation, sending [messengers], threats, warnings, promises, and the like may be motives to action or abstention, and God creates them. And that the non-differentiation of two acts in being created by God Most High does not negate their differentiation in other respects.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections that, it is claimed, follow from the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions are created by God alone, and then gives a general response. The objections are: (1) the futility of sending prophets and rewarding/punishing if humans have no real power; (2) the collapse of moral distinction between good and evil actions; (3) the inability to distinguish between voluntary and coerced movements. The answer clarifies that these objections only apply to pure Jabrites who deny human power and choice entirely, not to the Ash'ari position which affirms that actions occur by the servant's kasb (acquisition) and follow his intention, even though ultimately created by God. The answer also notes that some consequences (like determinism) also affect the Mu'tazilite position, and that praise/blame can be based on the locus rather than the agent. Finally, it argues that God's creation of acts does not prevent differentiation in other respects like motive or moral quality.

إرادة
كسب
مدح وذم
ثواب وعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections apply to all Muslims who believe God creates actions.
Correction: The passage shows that these objections are specifically meant to refute the Ash'ari view, but the response will clarify that they only apply to pure Jabrites who deny human choice entirely.

Key Takeaway
The three objections show that denying real human power seems to undermine prophethood, moral responsibility, and basic human intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the distinction you naturally feel between moving your hand voluntarily and having it moved by someone else. If you truly believed that both movements are directly created by God, would that change how you view your own choices and responsibilities? What would that mean for your daily prayers and your efforts to avoid sin?
?
When you hear about the Prophet's guidance and Iblis's misguidance, do you find it unsettling that the Ash'ari view treats both as equally created by God? How does this challenge your intuitive sense of justice and your motivation to follow good and avoid evil?
?
If encouragement and deterrence (promises of Paradise and threats of Hell) have no effect because you have no real power, then what is the point of your striving? Does this thought make you rely more on God's mercy, or does it discourage you from action? Be honest with yourself.

The General Response: Ash'aris vs. Jabrites
Provides the general answer distinguishing Ash'aris from Jabrites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response deflects the objections by distinguishing their position from that of the Jabrites.

Core Idea
The objections only hit pure Jabrites who deny human power and choice.

After listing the objections, Sanusi provides a general answer that clarifies the Ash'ari position. He argues that the objections only apply to the Jabrites (المجبرة) who completely negate human power and choice, not to the Ash'aris who affirm kasb (acquisition) and effect by a preponderant from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
Read the Arabic from 'والجواب عن الكل' to 'بمحض خلق الله تعالى'. Note the distinction between three groups: Jabrites, Ash'aris who affirm kasb, and those who posit a non-independent effect.
2
Identify the key distinction
The answer says the objections only apply to 'المجبرة النافين لقدرة العبد واختياره' (Jabrites who deny the servant's power and choice). Ash'aris have two ways: (1) actions occur by kasb and after intent, (2) power has effect but not independently, by a preponderant from God.
Kasb means the servant's action occurs posterior to his resolve and is attributed to him, even though God creates it.
3
Understand the 'preponderant' clause
The second version says power is effective but not independently (لا بالاستقلال) rather by a preponderant (مرجح) which is sheer creation of God. This preserves human responsibility while affirming divine causality.
This nuance distinguishes Ash'aris from Mu'tazilites who claim independent human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرسل، وبعثة الأنبياء، وإنزال الكتب من السماء، إذ لا يظهر للترغيب والترهيب، والحث على تحصيل الكمالات، وإزالة الرذائل ونحو ذلك فائدة، إلا إذا كان بقدرة العبد وإرادته تأثير في أفعاله ويتولى مباشرتها باستقلاله ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الأفعال التي تطابق العقل والشرع على استحسانها واستحقاقها المدح في العاجل والثواب في الآجل، والتي ليست كذلك كالكفر والإيمان وكالإساءة إلى الفقراء والإحسان، وكفعل النبي من الهداية والإرشاد وتمهيد قواعد الخيرات، وفعل إبليس من الإضلال والإغواء، وتزيين الشرور والشهوات وكالتكلم بالتسبيحات والدعوات المترتب عليها الثواب باستجابة والتكلم بالهذيانات والفحش والهجاء التي لا تورث إلا اللوم والعقاب، لأن الكل بخلق الله تعالى من غير تأثير للعبد، ومنها بطلان الفرق بين الحركات التي تظهر من أعضاء العبد بقدرته وإرادته، والتي تظهر منها بقدرة الغير وإرادته كما إذا حرك زيد يد عمرو مثلاً مع أن كل أحد يفرق بينهما بالضرورة. والجواب عن الكل، أنه إنما يرد على المجبرة النافين لقدرة العبد واختياره، لا على من يجعل فعله متعلقاً بقدرته وإرادته واقعاً بكسبه وعقيب عزمه، وإن كان بخلق الله تعالى عز وجل، ولا على من يجعل قدرته مؤثرة لكن لا بالاستقلال بل بمرجح هو بمحض خلق الله تعالى، على أن من الفسادات ما يلزم المعتزلة أيضاً، كبطلان استقلال العبد بناء على وجوب الفعل وامتناعه لوجود المرجح أو عدمه، وتعلق علم الله بوقوعه أو لا وقوعه، ومنها ما يندفع بطريق آخر، فإن المدح والذم قد يكون باعتبار المحلية دون الفاعلية كالمدح والذم بالحسن والقبح وسائر الغرائز، وأن الثواب والعقاب أيضاً، لما فعل الله وتصرفاً فيها هو حقه لم يتوجه سؤال لميته كما لا يقال لم خلق الإحراق عقيب مس النار، وأن التكليف والبعثة والتهديد والوعيد والوعد، ونحو ذلك، قد يكون دواعي إلى الفعل أو الترك فيخلقه الله تعالى، وأن عدم افتراق الفعلين في المخلوقية الله تعالى لا ينافي افتراقها بوجوه أخر.
The messengers, sending of prophets, and revelation of books from heaven, because there does not appear for encouragement and deterrence, urging the acquisition of perfections and removal of vices and the like any benefit, except if the servant's power and will have an effect in his actions and he undertakes their direct execution independently. And among them is the invalidity of the distinction between actions that agree with reason and revelation as to their approval and their deserving praise in the present and reward in the future, and those that are not so, such as disbelief and faith, and such as injury to the poor and beneficence, and such as the act of the Prophet in guidance and direction and laying down the foundations of good deeds, and the act of Iblis in misguidance and seduction and adorning evils and desires, and such as speaking with glorifications and supplications upon which reward is contingent through response, and speaking with nonsense, obscenity, and satire which yield nothing but blame and punishment, because all are by the creation of God Most High without any effect of the servant. And among them is the invalidity of the distinction between movements that appear from the servant's limbs by his power and will, and those that appear from his limbs by the power and will of another, such as when Zayd moves 'Amr's hand, for example, while everyone necessarily distinguishes between them. And the answer to all is that it applies only to the Jabrites who deny the servant's power and choice, not to one who makes his action attached to his power and will, occurring by his kasb and subsequent to his resolve, even though it is by the creation of God Most High, and not to one who makes his power effective but not independently, rather by a preponderant which is by sheer creation of God Most High. Moreover, among the corruptions [that follow from this] are those that also follow upon the Mu'tazilites, such as the invalidity of the servant's independence based on the necessity or impossibility of the act due to the existence or non-existence of the preponderant, and the connection of God's knowledge to its occurrence or non-occurrence. And among them are those that are repelled by another way, for praise and blame may be with respect to the locus rather than the agent, like praise and blame for beauty and ugliness and other natural traits. And reward and punishment also, when God does and disposes in it as His right, no question of its 'why' is directed, just as it is not said why He created burning following contact with fire. And that obligation, sending [messengers], threats, warnings, promises, and the like may be motives to action or abstention, and God creates them. And that the non-differentiation of two acts in being created by God Most High does not negate their differentiation in other respects.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections that, it is claimed, follow from the Ash'ari doctrine that all actions are created by God alone, and then gives a general response. The objections are: (1) the futility of sending prophets and rewarding/punishing if humans have no real power; (2) the collapse of moral distinction between good and evil actions; (3) the inability to distinguish between voluntary and coerced movements. The answer clarifies that these objections only apply to pure Jabrites who deny human power and choice entirely, not to the Ash'ari position which affirms that actions occur by the servant's kasb (acquisition) and follow his intention, even though ultimately created by God. The answer also notes that some consequences (like determinism) also affect the Mu'tazilite position, and that praise/blame can be based on the locus rather than the agent. Finally, it argues that God's creation of acts does not prevent differentiation in other respects like motive or moral quality.

Key Terms
المجبرة
كسب
مرجح
استقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari and Jabrite positions are the same because both say God creates all actions.
Correction: The Ash'ari position affirms real human power and choice (kasb), whereas the Jabrites deny any human power. The response shows the objections only apply to Jabrites.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, to whom do the objections actually apply?
A) The Ash'aris
B) The Jabrites who deny human power and choice
C) The Mu'tazilites
D) All Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response saves human responsibility by affirming kasb or non-independent effect, thereby deflecting the Jabrite accusation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself blaming it on 'what was written' instead of your own choice? How does the Ash'ari teaching on kasb confront that tendency and call you to own your actions before Allah?
?
The Ash'aris say your power is real but not independent—it depends entirely on Allah's creation. Does this humble you or frustrate you? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived knowing that every good deed you do is from Allah's sheer grace, not your own independence?
?
The Jabrites deny human power altogether, making them passive in worship. But are there areas of your deen where you act like a Jabrite—feeling that you have no real ability to change or improve? What is one specific struggle you can start actively fighting today, trusting that Allah has given you real (though dependent) power to overcome it?

Additional Deflections: Locus vs. Agent and Other Responses
Provides additional deflections beyond the general answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand additional responses that address the objections regarding moral distinction, reward/punishment, and divine creation.

Core Idea
Praise and blame can be based on the locus (action) rather than the agent (God).

Shifting from the general answer, Sanusi provides further arguments to counter remaining concerns. He notes that some corruptions also affect the Mu'tazilites, and then explains that praise/blame can relate to the action itself (its inherent qualities) rather than the creator. He also discusses reward/punishment as God's right, and that divine creation does not negate other distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional responses
Read the Arabic from 'على أن من الفسادات' to the end. Identify the different points: (1) some corruptions also apply to Mu'tazilites, (2) praise/blame can be by locus, (3) reward/punishment is God's right, (4) motives are created by God, (5) non-differentiation in creation does not negate other differences.
2
Focus on the 'locus vs. agent' argument
The passage says 'المدح والذم قد يكون باعتبار المحلية دون الفاعلية' (praise and blame may be with respect to the locus rather than the agent). This means we can praise an action for its beauty or blame it for its ugliness without attributing the action to God as the moral agent.
This is a key Ash'ari move: actions have inherent moral qualities (حسن وقبح) that are grounds for praise/blame regardless of who creates them.
3
Understand the final point
The passage concludes that two acts (e.g., good and evil) not differing in being created by God does not prevent them from differing in other aspects (e.g., moral value, motive).
This ties back to the second objection about indistinguishability.

?

?

Is your heart at peace with that, or does it resist? What does that resistance reveal about your trust in His ultimate justice?
?
The lesson says commands and threats from God are motives He creates in us. When you feel unmotivated to obey, do you see this as a created motive? How can you actively seek those motives from Him, rather than relying on your own self-generated will?
