Al-Razi's Refutation of Jabr from Every Verse
Al-Taftazani reports al-Razi's claim that every verse refutes jabr and begins a new enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi demonstrated that every Quranic verse refutes jabr (divine compulsion) and his classification of Mu'tazilite scriptural proofs into types, beginning with verses that attribute actions to servants.

Core Idea
Every Quranic verse refutes jabr, as al-Razi showed, starting with Surah al-Fatiha.

This passage reports a bold Mu'tazilite claim that every verse of the Quran contains evidence against jabr. Remarkably, the Ash'ari scholar Imam al-Razi took up this challenge and demonstrated it in his commentary on Surah al-Fatiha, achieving what al-Taftazani calls 'the farthest limit in exposition and refutation.' After this, al-Razi classified the Mu'tazilite scriptural proofs into several types, beginning with the most abundant category: verses that explicitly attribute actions to their human doers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the claim, then al-Razi's response, then the classification.
The passage ends mid-sentence; al-Taftazani is referring to a previous point.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find the sentence that states the Mu'tazilite position. What do they claim about every verse?
The phrase 'حتى زعموا أنه ما من آية إلا وفيها دلالة على بطلان الجبر' is the key.
3
Understand al-Razi's role
Al-Razi, an Ash'ari theologian, is said to have demonstrated this claim in Surah al-Fatiha. Why would an Ash'ari do this? He was engaging with the Mu'tazilite argument and using their own framework.
This shows the depth of scholarly engagement.
4
Recognize the classification
The passage states that al-Razi classified scriptural proofs into several types. The first type is verses that attribute actions to servants. Note the Quranic examples given.
The category is vast, from al-Baqarah to al-Nas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى زعموا أنه ما من آية إلا وفيها دلالة على بطلان الجبر، وقد بينه الإمام الرازي رحمه الله في سورة الفاتحة (1) ليقاس عليه الباقي، وبلغ الأمد الأقصى في التقرير والمعارضة من جانب أهل الحق ، ثم ضبط دلائلهم السمعية على كثرتها في عدة أنواع . الأول: الآيات الدالة على إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد، بإسناد الفعل إلى فاعله، وهو أكثر من أن يحصى فليبدأ من قوله تعالى الذين يؤمنون بالغيب ويقيمون الصلاة إلى قوله تعالى الذي يوسوس في صدور الناس وقد عرفت أن هذا
Until they claimed that there is not a single verse except that in it is evidence for the invalidity of jabr (compulsion), and Imam al-Razi (may God have mercy on him) demonstrated this in Surah al-Fatiha (1) for the rest to be measured by it, and he reached the farthest limit in exposition and refutation from the side of the people of truth. Then he classified their scriptural proofs, despite their abundance, into several types. First: The verses indicating the attribution of actions to servants, by attributing the act to its doer, which is more than can be counted, so let him start from His saying, the Exalted, 'Who believe in the unseen and establish prayer' to His saying, the Exalted, 'Who whispers in the hearts of people.' And you have known that this...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage reports a claim by the Mu'tazilites that every verse of the Quran contains evidence against jabr (divine compulsion), and then states that Imam al-Razi (the Ash'ari scholar) demonstrated this in his commentary on Surah al-Fatiha, achieving the utmost in exposition and counterargument from the perspective of the people of truth (i.e., the Ash'aris). After this demonstration, al-Razi classified the Mu'tazilite scriptural proofs into several types. The passage begins to list these types: the first type is verses that attribute actions to servants by directly attributing the act to its doer. This is a vast category; al-Taftazani gives examples from the Quran: from the beginning of Surah al-Baqarah ('Who believe in the unseen and establish prayer') to the end of Surah al-Nas ('Who whispers in the hearts of people'). The passage ends abruptly with 'And you have known that this...', indicating that the author is referring to a previous established point. This lesson presents al-Razi's approach as one that even the Mu'tazilites would accept as proving their case, but al-Taftazani uses it to show the extent of the debate.

Key Terms
بطلان الجبر
الإمام الرازي
إسناد الأفعال
دلائل سمعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this passage affirms the Mu'tazilite view.
Correction: The passage is actually reporting the Mu'tazilite claim and al-Razi's engagement with it from an Ash'ari perspective; al-Razi is using their own argument framework to show the extent of the debate.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what did the Mu'tazilites claim about the Quranic verses?
A) Only a few verses support free will.
B) Every verse contains evidence for the invalidity of jabr.
C) All verses support jabr.
D) The verses are ambiguous on the issue.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi demonstrated that every Quranic verse refutes jabr, and he classified the Mu'tazilite proofs, starting with verses that directly attribute actions to human servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself blaming your sins on God's decree, feeling that you had no choice? How does the claim that 'every verse refutes jabr' strip away that excuse, and what will you do the next time you feel that temptation?
?
Al-Razi used Surah al-Fatiha as a model to show that even the first surah refutes compulsion. Read al-Fatiha now: which verse(s) in it directly demand personal accountability from you, and what action will you take today because of that demand?
?
The passage says al-Razi 'reached the farthest limit in exposition and refutation.' What is one area of your life where you have been avoiding taking responsibility, claiming circumstances forced you? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to own that action.

The Fifth Objection and Its Rebuttal
Taftazani presents the fifth objection against determinism and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objection that human actions are independent of God's will by affirming that human will is subordinate to divine will.

Core Idea
Human will is not independent; it functions within God's overarching will.

This passage addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of divine determinism: that Quranic verses appear to grant humans independent choice. Taftazani presents both the objection and the rebuttal, showing that the verses affirming human will do not negate God's ultimate control. The rebuttal relies on a complementary verse that subordinates human will to God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the Quranic verses cited to argue for human independence: 'So whoever wills let him believe and whoever wills let him disbelieve' (Q18:29) and 'Do whatever you will' (Q41:40).
These verses seem to grant humans free will.
2
Read the rebuttal
Notice how Taftazani responds by affirming human will but qualifying it: 'Yes, but their will is by His will.'
The rebuttal does not deny human will but places it under divine will.
3
Connect the verses
See how the rebuttal verse ('And you do not will except that Allah wills' — Q76:30) resolves the tension by showing that human will is not independent.
This verse is the key to the Ash'ari position.
4
Reflect on the principle
Understand that in Sunni theology, secondary causes (human will) are real but encompassed by primary causality (divine will).
This reconciles divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: تعليق أفعال العباد بمشيئتهم دون مشيئته فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر، اعملوا ما شئتم، قلنا: نعم لكن مشيئتهم بمشيئته وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله
The fifth: Attributing the actions of servants to their own will without His will, 'So whoever wills let him believe and whoever wills let him disbelieve', 'Do whatever you will'. We said: Yes, but their will is by His will, 'And you do not will except that Allah wills'.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection against the doctrine of determinism (jabr). The opponents argue that Quranic verses such as 'So whoever wills let him believe and whoever wills let him disbelieve' (Q18:29) and 'Do whatever you will' (Q41:40) attribute human actions entirely to human choice, independent of God's will. The author responds ('قلنا') by affirming that while humans do have will, their will is itself created and determined by God's will, citing the verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' (Q76:30). Thus, the apparent independence of human will is subordinated to divine omnipotence.

Misconception: Affirming human will negates divine determinism.
Correction: Human will is real but created and encompassed by God's will.

B) By affirming that human will is created by God and subject to His will
C) By reinterpreting the verses metaphorically
D) By claiming the verses are abrogated

Key Takeaway
Human will is real but created and determined by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The verse 'You do not will except that Allah wills' confronts your deepest assumptions about autonomy. Are you willing to let go of the pride that comes from thinking your will is self-sufficient? What practical step can you take today to live in the reality that your will is borrowed from Allah?

Three Types of Scriptural Evidence Used by Opponents of Determinism
Taftazani categorizes opponents' scriptural arguments into three types and gives the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of scriptural evidence opponents of determinism use and how the Ash‘ari response reconciles them with divine decree.

Core Idea
Opponents' scriptural proofs fall into three distinct categories, each answered by the same key: God creates the power-motive complex.

Taftazani, citing Imam al-Razi, shows that opponents of determinism (i.e., those arguing for human free will) cite numerous Quranic verses. Rather than address each verse individually, he classifies them into three types, each presenting a different angle: direct attribution of acts to humans, commands and moral language, and verbs of creation applied to humans. For each, the Ash‘ari response is that the reality of human action lies in the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da‘i), and that combination is created by God. Thus the verses are valid without implying that humans are independent creators.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read Taftazani's Arabic classification and its English translation. Notice how he structures the three types and references Imam al-Razi's approach.
The passage begins by noting that opponents claim every verse proves their case, then summarizes their proofs into types.
2
Identify the first type: attribution of acts
The first type are verses that attribute actions directly to human agents, like 'those who believe...' Identify why Taftazani says this is not really disputed and how the response uses the power-motive combination.
3
Identify the second type: commands and moral language
The second type includes verses of command, prohibition, praise, blame, reward, punishment, and historical warnings. Consider why these would only be meaningful if humans have genuine choice.
Taftazani simply says 'you have known the answer' – referring to the same power-motive response.
4
Identify the third type: verbs of creation
The third type lists specific Arabic verbs that imply bringing something into existence (عمل, فعل, صنع, كسب, جعل) as applied to humans in the Quran. Each is illustrated with examples. Notice the incomplete ending.
This third type reinforces that human 'creation' is secondary, dependent on God's creation of the power and motive.
5
Reflect on the unifying response
Consider how all three types are answered by the same principle: human acts have a real aspect (the combination of power and motive) but that combination is created by God, so the ultimate attribution is to God, yet the human is truly acting.
This balances divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

Until they claimed that there is no verse except that it contains evidence of the invalidity of determinism, and Imam al-Razi, may God have mercy on him, explained this in Surat al-Fatiha so that the rest could be analogized to it, and he reached the utmost limit in exposition and objection on behalf of the people of truth. Then he summarized their scriptural evidences, despite their abundance, into several types. First: The verses indicating the attribution of actions to servants, by attributing the act to its doer, which are more than can be counted, beginning from His saying 'Those who believe in the unseen and establish prayer' to His saying 'Who whispers in the breasts of people'. And you have known that this is not part of the disputed matter at all, and the Imam claimed that there is no escape from them except by affirming that the combination of power and motive is effective in the act, and the creator of that combination is God Almighty. By this consideration, the attribution is valid and the contradiction disappears between them and the definitive proofs that everything is by God's decree and predestination. Second: The verses concerning commanding servants to some actions, forbidding them from some, praising them for faith and acts of obedience, blaming them for disbelief and sins, promising them reward for obedience and punishment for disobedience, and in the stories of past nations as a warning that what befell them might befall the listeners for admonition and consideration of their conditions. All of this is valid only if the servant has power and choice in producing actions, and you have known the answer. Third: The explicit verses attributing words placed for bringing into existence to the servants, namely: 'عمل' (action) as in His saying 'Whoever does righteous deed, it is for himself' and 'that He may recompense those who do evil for what they did' and 'Indeed those who believe and do righteous deeds' and 'Whoever does an evil deed will be recompensed only with its like' - and this is very abundant. And 'فعل' (act) as in His saying 'And whatever good you do, indeed Allah is knowing of it' and 'Do good'. And 'صنع' (making) as in His saying 'And how evil was what they used to make'. And 'كسب' (earning) as in His saying 'And every soul will be paid in full for what it earned' and 'Every person is held in pledge for what he earned' and 'Today every soul will be recompensed for what it earned'. And 'جعل' (making) as in His saying 'They make...'

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the scriptural evidences used by opponents of determinism. The author notes that opponents claim every verse supports their view, and Imam al-Razi thoroughly addressed this. The author then summarizes their evidences into three types. The first type consists of verses that attribute actions directly to human agents, like the opening of Sura al-Baqarah. The response, according to Imam al-Razi, is that the effective cause of action is the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i), and this combination is created by God. Thus the attribution is correct without contradicting divine decree. The second type includes verses of command, prohibition, praise, blame, reward, punishment, and historical stories meant as warnings. These would be valid only if humans have genuine power and choice. The author simply says 'you have known the answer' referring to the earlier response. The third type lists explicit verbs used for creation (e.g., عمل, فعل, صنع, كسب, جعل) attributed to humans in the Quran. Each is illustrated with multiple verses. The response is the same: human acts are real but secondary, as God creates the power and the act.

The Imam's Resolution: Conjunction of Power and Motive
Imam al-Razi's claim on the conjunction of power and motive as the created cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi resolves the apparent contradiction between divine decree and human action by positing that the conjunction of power and motive is created by God.

Core Idea
The conjunction of power and motive, created by God, is the true cause of action.

This passage presents a key theological solution from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He argues that to avoid a deadlock, we must accept that the combination of human power and motive is what brings about an action, yet that combination itself is divinely created. This subtle distinction preserves both human responsibility and divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the flow: dispute, Imam's claim, conclusion.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the three components
Identify 'power' (القدرة), 'motive' (الداعي), and their 'conjunction' (مجموع القدرة والداعي). Understand that the Imam claims this conjunction is the effective factor.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how stating that God creates the conjunction resolves the contradiction between ascribing actions to humans and the decisive proofs that everything is by God's decree.
This is the key insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس من المتنازع فيه في شيء، وزعم الإمام أنه لا محيص عنها إلا بالتزام أن مجموع القدرة والداعي مؤثر في الفعل، وخالق ذلك المجموع هو الله تعالى. بهذا الاعتبار صح الإسناد وزال التناقض بينها وبين الأدلة القاطعة على أن الكل بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره.
It is not disputed at all. The Imam claimed that there is no escape from it except by affirming that the conjunction of power and motive is effective in the action, and the Creator of that conjunction is God Almighty. With this attribution, the ascription becomes correct and the contradiction disappears between it and the definitive proofs that everything is by the decree and predestination of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a key claim by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. It begins by stating that there is no dispute about the matter. Then it attributes to the Imam the view that one cannot avoid the conclusion that the conjunction (مجموع) of power (القدرة) and motive (الداعي) is what effectively produces the action. However, that conjunction itself is created by God. With this understanding, it becomes correct to ascribe actions to humans (صح الإسناد) without contradicting the decisive proofs that everything occurs by God's decree and predestination. The idea is that while the conjunction of power and motive is the immediate cause, its existence comes from God, thus reconciling human agency with divine predestination.

Misconception: Some think the Imam denies human agency entirely, attributing all actions directly to God without any human contribution.
Correction: In fact, the Imam affirms human power and motive as real components, but their conjunction—which is the effective cause—is created by God. This preserves human agency within a framework of divine causation.

Verses Rebuking Disbelievers: An Objection to Determinism
Author lists verses rebuking disbelievers as if they could have believed, implying free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Qur'anic verses that rebuke disbelievers are used as an objection against theological determinism, and why they imply human free will.

Core Idea
Qur'anic rebuke assumes ability; without it, rebuke would be unjust.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful objection against the view that all human actions are directly compelled by God. The objection draws on several Qur'anic verses that rebuke disbelievers and sinners, implying that they had the capacity to believe and obey but chose otherwise. These verses seem to challenge any deterministic theology that denies human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the pattern: each verse is presented as evidence for the objection.
The verses are quoted in a condensed form; refer to the full context if needed.
2
Identify the two key concepts: مانع and ملجيء
The passage states there is no preventer (مانع) from faith and obedience, and no compeller (ملجيء) to disbelief and disobedience. These terms frame the objection.
These concepts are central to the free will vs. determinism debate.
3
Examine each cited verse
Look at each verse and consider how it implies that the disbelievers could have done otherwise. What does the rebuke assume about their ability?
Verses 17:94, 2:28, 38:75, 84:20, 74:49, 3:71.
4
Reflect on the force of the objection
Ask yourself: If God had predetermined disbelief, would rebuking people be fair? This is the crux of the objection.
The author will later respond; for now, understand the objection's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الآيات الدالة على توبيخ الكفار والعصاة، وأنه لا مانع من الإيمان والطاعة ولا ملجيء إلى الكفر والمعصية كقوله تعالى (وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا) و(كيف تكفرون بالله) و(وما منعك أن تسجد) و(فما لهم لا يؤمنون) و(فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين) و(لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل وتكتمون الحق).

Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against theological determinism (jabr). The objection is based on Qur'anic verses that rebuke disbelievers and sinners as if they had the ability to do otherwise. The verses imply that there was no external preventer (مانع) from faith and obedience, and no compulsion (ملجئ) to disbelief and disobedience. The author cites several verses: 'What prevented the people from believing?' (Quran 17:94), 'How can you disbelieve in God?' (Quran 2:28), 'What prevented you from prostrating?' (Quran 38:75), 'So what is the matter with them that they do not believe?' (Quran 84:20), 'So what is the matter with them that they turn away from the reminder?' (Quran 74:49), and 'Why do you mix truth with falsehood and conceal the truth?' (Quran 3:71). The implication is that if God had predetermined disbelief and sin, rebuking people for what they cannot avoid would be unjust. The passage ends here; the answer is presumably given in the following section.

Answering the Objection: Quranic 'Creation' by Humans as Metaphorical
Al-Taftazani interprets verses using 'creation' for humans as metaphorical causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani reconciles Quranic verses attributing creation to humans with the Ash'ari doctrine of divine decree.

Core Idea
Verses attributing creation to humans are metaphors for customary causation, not literal creation.

The Mu'tazila often cite Quranic verses where humans are described as 'creating' or 'innovating' to argue that humans have independent creative power. How can an Ash'ari respond? In this passage, al-Taftazani provides a clear hermeneutical strategy: these expressions are metaphorical, indicating that humans are customary causes, while true creation belongs to Allah alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the verses enumerated by the opponent. They include 'creation' (khalq), 'innovation' (ihdāth), and 'invention' (ibtidāʿ) attributed to humans.
These verses are the basis of the Mu'tazili argument for human creative power.
2
Understand the answer's foundation
Al-Taftazani first reaffirms that everything is by divine decree and predestination, established by prior proofs. This is the non-negotiable starting point.
The answer does not question the verses but reinterprets them in light of established doctrine.
3
Learn the metaphorical interpretation
He argues that these expressions must be taken as metaphors for 'customary causation' (al-tasabbub al-ʿādī), meaning the human becomes a customary cause for the action, similar to saying 'the prince built the city' when he only ordered it.
This preserves the literal meaning of other terms like 'kasb' (acquisition), which remains literal.
4
Clarify specific terms
Al-Taftazani explains that 'creation' (khalq) in these contexts means 'estimation' (taqdīr), and 'making' (jaʿl) means 'rendering' (taṣyīr), not bringing something into existence from nothing. He gives examples like 'Allah made the dirham in the purse'.
This shows that the verb 'jaʿla' does not always imply creation ex nihilo.
5
Explore the alternative Ash'ari view
He presents the view of the Imam (likely al-Ash'ari or al-Juwayni) that the combined power (qudra) and motive (dāʿiya) are the effective cause of the act, and this combination is created by Allah without the servant's choice. On this view, no metaphor is needed.
This view eliminates any independence for the servant, thus refuting Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصابعهم في آذانهم من الصواعق وجعلوا الله شركاء الجن والخلق كقوله تعالى فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين وأخلق لكم من الطين وإذ تخلق من الطين كهيئة الطير والإحداث كقوله حكاية عن الخضر حتى أحدث لك ذكراً والابتداع كقوله تعالى ورهبانية ابتدعوها منه . والجواب : أنه لما ثبت بالدلائل السالفة أن الكل بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره . وجب جعل هذه الألفاظ مجازات عن التسبب العادي أي من صار سبباً عادياً للأعمال الصالحة، وعلى هذا القياس، أو جعل هذه الإسنادات مجازات لكون العبد سبباً لهذه الأفعال كما في بنى الأمير المدينة هذا في غير لفظ الكسب، فإنه يصح على حقيقته. والخلق فإنه بمعنى التقدير والجعل بمعنى التصيير، وهو لا يستلزم إيجاد أمر محقق مثل جعل الله الدرهم في الكيس، وجعل لزيد شريكاً. وأما على رأي الإمام وهو أن مجموع القدرة والداعية مؤثرة في الفعل، وذلك المجموع بخلق الله تعالى من غير اختيار العبد فلا مجاز ولا إشكال ولا استقلال للعبد فلا اعتزال .
Their fingers in their ears from the thunderbolts, and they made the jinn partners of Allah, and creation as His saying 'So blessed be Allah, the best of creators', and 'I create for you from clay' and 'when you create from clay like the form of a bird', and innovation as His saying narrating from al-Khiḍr 'until I innovate for you a mention', and invention as His saying 'and monasticism they invented'. And the answer: Since it has been established by the previous proofs that everything is by the decree and predestination of Allah, it is necessary to make these expressions metaphors for customary causation, i.e., whoever becomes a customary cause for righteous deeds, and similarly, or to make these attributions metaphors for the servant being a cause for these actions, as in 'the prince built the city' – this is except for the term acquisition (kasb), for it is valid in its literal sense. And 'creation' (khalq) means estimation, and 'making' (jaʿl) means rendering, and it does not necessitate bringing into existence a realized thing, like 'Allah made the dirham in the purse' and 'made Zayd a partner'. As for the view of the Imām, which is that the combined power (qudra) and motive (dāʿiya) are effective in the act, and that combined entity is created by Allah without the servant's choice, then there is no metaphor, no problem, no independence for the servant, hence no Muʿtazilism.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objection cites Quranic verses where verbs like 'create' (khalq), 'innovate' (ihdāth), and 'invent' (ibtidāʿ) are attributed to humans, which the Muʿtazila use to argue for human creative power. The author (al-Taftazani) responds by first reaffirming that all things are by divine decree and predestination, established by prior proofs. Therefore, these expressions must be interpreted metaphorically. He explains that they indicate 'customary causation' (al-tasabbub al-ʿādī), meaning the human becomes a customary cause for actions, just as we say 'the prince built the city' when he only ordered it. The term 'acquisition' (kasb) is excepted because it is valid literally. Then he clarifies the meanings: 'creation' (khalq) here means estimation (taqdīr), and 'making' (jaʿl) means rendering (taṣyīr), not bringing something into existence from nothing. He gives examples like 'Allah made the dirham in the purse' which does not imply the dirham was non-existent before. Finally, he presents an alternative view attributed to 'the Imam' (likely al-Ashʿari or al-Juwayni): that the combined power (qudra) and motive (dāʿiya) are the effective cause of the act, and this combination is created by Allah without the servant's choice. On this view, there is no need for metaphorical interpretation, no difficulty, and no human independence, thus refuting Muʿtazilism.

Key Terms
خلق
جعل
كسب
مجاز
تسبب عادي
قدرة
داعية
اعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ashʿari position on human acts contradicts Quranic verses that attribute creation to humans.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows these verses are metaphorical, indicating customary causation, not literal creation. Moreover, on the Imām's view, there is no metaphor needed because the effective cause is the created combination of power and motive.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when the Quran says that humans 'create' or 'innovate', how should these terms be understood?
A) They are literal, proving that humans are independent creators.
B) They are metaphorical, indicating customary causation or estimation.
C) They are only applicable to prophets.
D) They are errors in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verbs of 'creation' attributed to humans are interpreted metaphorically as customary causation, preserving Allah's sole creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in something—a project, a skill, a relationship—do you ever catch yourself feeling like the 'creator' of that outcome, as if your effort alone brought it into being? How would al-Taftazani’s concept of customary causation change the way you receive praise?
?
You read verses like 'You create from clay a form like a bird' (Quran 5:110) and feel a tension: is that really 'creation'? After this lesson, can you honestly say your heart submits to the idea that you are never an independent originator—even in your most intimate acts of will? If not, what stands in the way of that submission?
?
Al-Taftazani offers two ways to understand human agency: metaphorical causation or the view that even your power and motive are created by Allah. Which one makes you more accountable for your actions, and which one makes you more reliant on Allah? Which one do you actually live by—and which one would your Lord hold you to?

The Fourth Objection: Quranic Rebukes and Predestination
Al-Taftazani rebuts the criticism that predestination makes Quranic rebukes pointless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that predestination makes Quranic rebukes contradictory, and how al-Taftazani's answer distinguishes between apparent and rational obstacles.

Core Idea
The Quranic rebukes target apparent obstacles, not the hidden decree of God.

This passage presents the fourth objection against the Ashʿari doctrine of predestination: if God decrees everything, why does He rebuke disbelievers as if they had a choice? Al-Taftazani first states the objection, then cites a lengthy rhetorical challenge from the Muʿtazilī scholar al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād, and finally gives a concise answer based on the distinction between apparent and rational obstacles. By working through this lesson, you will see how the Ashʿari response preserves both divine decree and Quranic rebuke.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Read the opening verses cited by al-Taftazani that rebuke disbelievers, and understand the objection: these verses seem to imply that disbelief is freely chosen, contradicting predestination.
Pay attention to the list of Quranic verses.
2
Examine the hypothetical argument from the mujbira
The passage presents a hypothetical argument from those who believe in compulsion (mujbira): if God creates disbelief, wills it, etc., then the Quran becomes a proof for the disbeliever, not against him.
This argument is the core of the objection.
3
Analyze al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād's rhetorical questions
Read the long quote from al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād, where he poses a series of pointed questions highlighting the apparent inconsistency between God's decree and His rebuke.
These questions are meant to force a contradiction.
4
Identify the answer and its key distinction
Al-Taftazani answers by distinguishing two types of obstacles: apparent obstacles (like social pressure) known to ignorant disbelievers, and hidden rational obstacles (like divine decree) known only to scholars.
This distinction resolves the objection: the verses refer to apparent obstacles, not the hidden decree.
5
Reflect on the refutation of the Qadariyya
Consider how this answer refutes the Qadariyya (who overemphasize free will) by showing that the hidden rational obstacles are beyond ordinary comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الآيات الدالة على توبيخ الكفار والعصاة ، وأنه لا مانع من الإيمان والطاعة ولا ملجيء إلى الكفر والمعصية كقوله تعالى وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا كيف تكفرون بالله وما منعك أن تسجد فما لهم لا يؤمنون فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل ولم تصدور عن سبيل الله وأمثال ذلك. وعلى مذهب المجبرة لهم أن يجادلوا ويقولوا إنك خلقت فينا الكفر، وعلمته، وأردته، وأخبرت به، وخلقت قدرة وداعية، يجب معهما الكفر، وكل هذه موانع من الإيمان، فيكون القرآن حجة للكافر، وقد أنزل ليكون حجة عليه، وإلى هذا أشار الصاحب بن عباد وكان غالياً في الرفض والاعتزال ساعياً في تربية أبي هاشم الجبائي ورفع قدره، وإعلاء ذكره حيث قال : كيف يأمر بالإيمان ولم يرده وينهى عن الكفر وأراده، ويعاقب على الباطل ويقدره، وكيف يصرف عن الإيمان ثم يقول أنى يصرفون ويخلق فيهم الإفك ثم يقول أنى يؤفكون وأنشأ فيهم الكفر ثم يقول كيف تكفرون وخلق فيهم لبس الحق بالباطل ثم قال لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل وصدهم عن السبيل ثم يقول لم تصدون عن سبيل الله وحال بينهم وبين الإيمان ثم يقول وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا وأذهب عن الرشد ثم قال فأين تذهبون وأضلهم عن الدين حتى أعرضوا ثم قال فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين . والجواب : أن المراد الموانع الظاهرة التي يعلمها جهال الكفرة، وهذه موانع عقلية خفيت على علماء القدرية .
Fourth: The verses indicating the rebuke of disbelievers and sinners, and that there is no preventer from faith and obedience and no compel[l]er to disbelief and disobedience, such as His saying 'And what prevented the people from believing', 'How can you disbelieve in Allah', 'And what prevented you from prostrating', 'So what is [the matter] with them that they do not believe', 'So what is [the matter] with them that they turn away from the reminder', 'Why do you clothe truth with falsehood', 'And why do you avert [people] from the way of Allah', and the like. And according to the doctrine of the compellers (al-mujbira), they can argue and say: You created in us disbelief, and you knew it, and you willed it, and you informed of it, and you created power and motive which necessitate disbelief, and all these are preventers from faith, so the Quran would be an argument for the disbeliever, whereas it was revealed to be an argument against him. And to this al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād pointed—he was extreme in Rafḍ and Muʿtazilism, striving to raise Abū Hāshim al-Jubbāʾī and elevate his rank and make his mention high—where he said: How can He command faith while not willing it, forbid disbelief while willing it, punish falsehood while decreeing it? How can He turn away from faith then say 'Where are they being turned away'? And He creates falsehood in them then says 'Where are they being deluded'? And He originates disbelief in them then says 'How can you disbelieve'? And He creates in them the clothing of truth with falsehood then says 'Why do you clothe truth with falsehood'? And He bars them from the way then says 'Why do you bar from the way of Allah'? And He interposes between them and faith then says 'And what [would it have harmed] them if they had believed'? And He takes away guidance then says 'Then where are you going'? And He leads them astray from religion until they turn away then says 'So what is [the matter] with them that they turn away from the reminder'? And the answer: That what is meant are the apparent obstacles which the ignorant among the disbelievers know, and these are rational obstacles hidden from the scholars of the Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection and its answer. The objection begins by citing Quranic verses that rebuke disbelievers and imply that faith was possible and that no force compelled them to disbelief. The author then presents a hypothetical argument from the 'compellers' (al-mujbira)—a term used here for those who argue that if God predestines everything, then the disbeliever can claim that God created disbelief in them, willed it, etc., making the Quranic rebuke a proof for the disbeliever rather than against them. This argument is then amplified by a long quote from al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād, a Muʿtazilī who composed a series of rhetorical questions pointing out the apparent contradiction between God's predestination and His rebuke. The answer is concise: the obstacles referred to in these verses are 'apparent obstacles' (al-mawāniʿ al-ẓāhira) that ignorant disbelievers are aware of, such as social pressure or lack of external means—not the 'rational obstacles' (mawāniʿ ʿaqliyya) of divine predestination, which are hidden. Thus the objection is based on confusing two types of obstacles.

Misconception: That the Ashʿari position on predestination makes Quranic rebukes of disbelievers incoherent.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explains that the rebukes refer to apparent obstacles (like social pressure), not the hidden rational obstacles of divine predestination.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿari answer preserves Quranic rebukes by distinguishing between apparent obstacles and the hidden decree of God.

Answering Scriptural Objections on Free Will
Author answers objection from Quranic verses on human will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians reconcile Quranic verses that appear to condition actions on human will with divine predestination.

Core Idea
Quranic verses conditioning actions on human will are subordinate to God's will.

This passage addresses a key objection raised by proponents of free will: Quranic verses like 'let him who wills believe' seem to grant humans independent will. The author, drawing on Ash'ari theology, explains that while actions are conditioned on human will, that will itself is created by God. He then presents rational proofs that ultimately resolve the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses and objection
Start by reading the Quranic verses listed and understand the objection they pose to predestination. Notice how they seem to put the choice in human hands.
These verses are often cited by those who advocate free will.
2
Understand the Ash'ari response
The author affirms that action is conditioned on human will, but immediately qualifies that human will is dependent on God's will. This is the key dialectical move.
The response uses the Quran itself (verse 'you do not will except that God wills') to support this subordination.
3
Examine the rational proofs
The author mentions two rational proofs that are decisive: the proof of the necessitating motive and the proof of eternal knowledge. These are the foundation for resolving the scriptural contradiction.
The Mu'tazilites themselves reportedly acknowledged these proofs as 'the two enemies of Mu'tazilism'.
4
Reflect on the anecdote
The story of 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and the Magian illustrates the logical problem of positing multiple independent wills. It shows the absurdity of the free-will position.
The anecdote reinforces the argument that only one will can prevail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر اعملوا ما شئتم (١) اعملوا ماشئتم (٢) ولمن شاء منكم أن يتقدم أو يتأخر (٣) و فمن شاء ذكره (٤) و فمن شاء اتخذ إلى ربه سبيلاً (٥) والجواب: أن التعليق بمشيئة العبد مذهبنا لكن مشيئته بمشيئة الله تعالى. وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله. وفي تعداد هذه الأنواع إفرادها إطالة، وقد فصلها الإمام في كتبه سيما المطالب العالية، وأورد أيضاً أحاديث كثيرة توافق أنواع الآيات. واقتصر في الجواب على أن الأدلة السمعية متعارضة، فالتمويل على العقليات وعمدته في ذلك دليل الداعي الموجب، ودليل العلم الأزلي، ولذا نقل عن بعض أذكياء المعتزلة أنه كان يقول «هما العدوان للاعتزال وإلا فقدتم الدست». وأما دليل الإرادة فقد أورده الموافق في عدادهما فلا معول عليه عندهم، لتجويزهم وقوع خلاف مراداً الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. ولهذا ألزم المجوسي عمرو بن عبيد حين قال له: «لم لا تسلم» فقال: «لأن الله لم يرد إسلامي». فقال: «إن الله يريد إسلامك لكن الشياطين لا يتركونك». فقال المجوسي: «فأنا أكون مع الشريك الغالب».
So let him who wills believe, and let him who wills disbelieve (1), do whatever you will (2), and for whoever among you wishes to advance or delay (3), and whoever wills remembers it (4), and whoever wills takes to his Lord a way (5). The answer: that conditioning on the will of the slave is our doctrine, but his will is by the will of God Most High. And you do not will except that God wills. And enumerating these types individually is lengthy, and the Imam has detailed them in his books, especially al-Matalib al-‘Aliya, and also adduced many hadiths that agree with the types of verses. And he restricted himself in the answer to saying that the scriptural evidences are contradictory, so reliance is on the rational proofs, and his mainstay in that is the proof of the necessitating motive and the proof of eternal knowledge. Hence it is reported from some clever Mu‘tazilites that he used to say: 'They are the two enemies of Mu‘tazilism, otherwise you would have lost the constitution.' As for the proof of will, the opponent has adduced it among their [arguments], so it is not relied upon according to them, because they allow the occurrence of contrary to what God wills – exalted is He above that greatly. And for this reason the Magian forced ‘Amr ibn ‘Ubayd when he said to him: 'Why do you not submit?' He replied: 'Because God did not will my Islam.' He said: 'God wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you.' The Magian said: 'Then I will be with the prevailing partner.'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The author first lists Quranic verses that appear to condition actions on human will (e.g., 'let him who wills believe', 'do whatever you will'), which proponents of free will might use as evidence. He then provides the answer: the Ash‘ari doctrine affirms the conditioning of actions on the human will, but this human will is itself subordinate to God’s will, as indicated by the verse 'you do not will except that God wills'. He explains that enumerating all the types of such verses would be lengthy; Imam al-Razi has detailed them in his works like al-Matalib al-‘Aliya, and also adduced many hadiths that correspond to these verse types. His concise answer is that the scriptural evidences are contradictory on this issue, so one must rely on rational proofs. The key rational proofs are the proof of the necessitating motive (al-dā‘ī al-mūjib) and the proof of eternal knowledge (al-‘ilm al-azalī). The author reports that some clever Mu‘tazilites said these two proofs are the two enemies of Mu‘tazilism, or else you would have lost the constitution (i.e., the foundation of your doctrine). As for the proof from God’s will (irāda), the opponents have used it in their counting, but it is not relied upon because they allow that something contrary to God’s will may occur – a position the author considers impossible. The passage ends with an anecdote: the Mu‘tazilite ‘Amr ibn ‘Ubayd questioned a Magian about why he did not submit to Islam. The Magian replied that God did not will his Islam. ‘Amr said God does will his Islam but the devils prevent him. The Magian cleverly retorted that then he will side with the prevailing partner (i.e., the stronger of the two wills, which is the devils according to ‘Amr’s logic). This illustrates the logical problem of positing multiple independent wills.

Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that these verses condition actions on human will, but that human will itself is created by God and subordinate to His will, as per the very next words: 'you do not will except that God wills'.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural evidence on free will is contradictory, so rational proofs are necessary to establish that human will is subordinate to God's will.

The Middle Path Between Jabr and Tafwid
Taftazani states the conclusion of the dispute affirming the middle path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari middle path between pure compulsion (jabr) and absolute delegation (tafwid), and the distinction between proximate and remote principles of action.

Core Idea
The human is compelled in the form of the free chooser.

This lesson presents the core resolution of the long-standing debate on human agency. Imam al-Taftazani states the Ash'ari position that neither pure jabr nor tafwid is correct, but rather a matter between two matters. He explains this by distinguishing the proximate principles (which are voluntary) from the remote principles (which are necessitating), concluding that the human is compelled in the form of the free chooser.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding statement
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the opening phrase 'خاتمة في فصل النزاع' (conclusion on settling the dispute). Identify the key summarizing phrases.
The passage is the author's own concluding section.
2
Understand the two extremes
The passage first lists the consequences of jabr (compulsion) and qadr (decree). Notice how each extreme leads to theological problems.
These are presented as unacceptable extremes.
3
Grasp the middle path
Focus on the sentence 'فالحق أنه لا جبر ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين'. This is the central thesis. Then see how the distinction between proximate and remote principles explains this middle path.
The phrase 'مضطر في صورة المختار' captures the paradox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة في فصل النزاع العبد مضطر في صورة المختار فصل (خاتمة : امتناع الترجح بلا مرجح وعدم العلم بتفاصيل الأفعال يعود إلى الجبر وحسن المدح والذم والأمر والنهي، وكون الأفعال تابعة لقصد العبد وداعية إلى المقدور، وكون العبد منبع النقصان يليق بالجبر وكثرة السفه، والعبث والقبح في الأفعال بالقدر والآيات والآثار متكاثرة في الجانبين. فالحق أنه لا جبر ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين إذ المبادىء القريبة على الاختيار والبعيدة على الاضطرارية فالإنسان مضطر في صورة المختار) . يشير إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن حال هذه المسألة عجيبة. فإن الناس كانوا مختلفين فيها أبداً بسبب أن ما يمكن الرجوع إليها فيها متعارضة متدافعة فمعول الجبرية على أنه لا بد لترجح الفعل على الترك من مرجح، ليس من العبد، ومعول القدرية على أن العبد لو لم يكن قادراً على فعله لما حسن المدح والذم والأمر والنهي وهما مقدمتان بديهيتان، ثم من الدلائل العقلية اعتماد الجبرية على أن تفاصيل أحوال الأفعال غير معلومة للعبد، واعتماد القدرية على أن أفعال العباد واقعة على وقوع مقصودهم ودواعيهم وهما متعارضان ومن الإلزامات الخطابية أن القدرة على الإيجاد صفة كمال لا تليق بالعبد الذي هو منبع النقصان، وأن أفعال العباد تكون سفهاً وعبثاً فلا تليق بالمتعالي عن النقصان، وأما الدلائل السمعية، فالقرآن مملوء بما يوهم الأمرين، وكذا الآثار فإن أمة من الأمم لم تكن خالية من الفرقتين، وكذا الأوضاع والحكايات متدافعة من الجانبين حتى قيل : إن وضع النرد على الجبر، ووضع الشطرنج على القدر. إلا أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب أن القدح في -
Conclusion on settling the dispute: The slave is compelled in the form of the free chooser. Section (Conclusion: The impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator and the absence of knowledge of the details of actions lead back to jabr (compulsion); and the goodness of praise and blame, command and prohibition, and that actions follow the intention of the slave and are incitements to the predetermined, and that the slave is an origin of deficiency befits jabr; and the abundance of foolishness, futility, and ugliness in actions are by qadr (decree); and the verses and traditions are abundant on both sides. The truth is that there is neither jabr nor tafwid (delegation), but a matter between two matters. For the proximate principles are voluntary, and the remote ones are necessitating. So the human is compelled in the form of the free chooser.) He refers to what Imam al-Razi mentioned, that the state of this issue is wondrous. For people have always differed over it because the sources to which one can refer in it are contradictory and mutually repelling. The reliance of the Jabrites is on the necessity of a preponderator for the preponderance of action over inaction, which is not from the slave; and the reliance of the Qadarites is that if the slave were not capable of his action, praise, blame, command, and prohibition would not be good – both are self-evident premises. Then from the rational proofs: the Jabrites rely on the fact that the details of the states of actions are not known to the slave; the Qadarites rely on the fact that the actions of servants occur upon the occurrence of their intention and motives – these two are contradictory. And from the rhetorical implications: that the power to bring into existence is a perfection attribute unfitting for the slave, who is an origin of deficiency; and that the actions of servants would be foolishness and futility, unfitting for the One exalted above deficiency. As for the scriptural proofs, the Quran is filled with what suggests both sides, and likewise the traditions: no community has ever been devoid of the two sects. Likewise the customs and stories are mutually repelling from both sides, until it was said: 'The invention of backgammon is on jabr, and the invention of chess is on qadr.' However, our school is stronger because the censure of -
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument (khātima) that resolves the long-standing dispute between predestination (jabr) and free will (qadar). It begins by stating the two poles of the debate: the Jabrites rely on the principle that a preponderator is necessary for any action to occur over inaction, and that this preponderator is not from the slave; the Qadarites rely on the self-evident premise that praise, blame, command, and prohibition only make sense if the slave has genuine capability. The author then presents rational arguments from both sides: the Jabrites point out that the slave lacks knowledge of the full details of his actions, while the Qadarites insist that actions result from the slave's intentions and motives. Both sets of premises are self-evident yet contradictory. He also notes rhetorical implications: ascribing the power to create actions to the slave would be an act of foolishness and futility unbecoming of God, yet denying all human agency makes God the author of evil. The scriptural evidences (Quran and hadith) are abundant on both sides, as are historical anecdotes. A striking saying is that backgammon (dice game) was introduced by Jabrites (emphasizing fate) and chess by Qadarites (emphasizing human choice). Despite this, the author asserts that the Ash‘ari school is strongest, although the text breaks off mid-sentence. The fundamental resolution is expressed in the maxim 'no jabr and no tafwid, but a matter between two matters': the proximate principles of an action (the slave's intention and choice) are truly voluntary, but the remote principles (the creation of the act and the preponderating factor) are from God. Thus the human is 'compelled in the form of the free chooser' (mujbur fī ṣūrat al-mukhtār).

مضطر في صورة المختار

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is pure jabr (compulsion).
Correction: The passage explicitly denies both pure jabr and pure tafwid, affirming a middle path where the proximate principles are voluntary and the remote principles are necessitating.

Checkpoint

B) Voluntary (اختيارية)

Key Takeaway
The human is neither purely compelled nor purely free; rather, the proximate causes are from his choice while the remote causes are from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you often find yourself making excuses that 'it was decreed' or do you take full responsibility? How does this lesson's distinction between proximate and remote principles challenge your default reaction? What concrete change will you make in how you repent tonight?
?
Think of a moment when you felt genuine pride in a good deed. Did you attribute it to yourself or to God? How does the statement 'the slave is an origin of deficiency' (from the translation) reshape your understanding of your own goodness? Are you willing to see every good in you as God's grace?
?
If you truly believed that your choices are genuinely yours yet entirely dependent on God, how would that affect the way you pray for help in making a difficult decision? Will you now ask God to 'preponderate' the right choice, as the remote principles suggest, while still actively choosing? What specific step will you take to live this truth tomorrow?

The Conflicting Evidences and the Ash'ari Resolution
Taftazani presents the conflicting proofs between Jabrites and Qadarites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conflicting rational and scriptural evidences between the Jabrites and Qadarites, and why the Ash'ari school is considered strongest.

Core Idea
Both sides have self-evident premises yet contradict—only the middle path resolves.

This lesson continues the discussion by presenting the conflicting proofs that have made this issue so contentious. Al-Taftazani explains how the Jabrites and Qadarites each rely on self-evident premises that seem to contradict, and how rational arguments and scriptural evidences are abundant on both sides. He then asserts that despite this, the Ash'ari school is strongest, though the text cuts off mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides' core premises
The passage states the reliance of the Jabrites on the necessity of a preponderator, and the Qadarites on the goodness of praise/blame. Notice that both are presented as self-evident (بديهيتان).
2
Examine the rational proofs
The author lists two rational proofs: one from lack of knowledge (Jabrites) and one from intention/motives (Qadarites). Note how they are contradictory.
3
Consider the rhetorical and scriptural evidences
The rhetorical implications (power being perfection, etc.) and scriptural evidences (Quran and hadith) are presented as abundant on both sides. The anecdote about backgammon and chess illustrates the deep division.
4
Understand the Ash'ari claim of strength
The passage ends with 'إلا أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب أن القدح في -' indicating the Ash'ari school is strongest, but the reason is cut off. Reflect on how the middle path resolves the contradictions.
The missing reason is likely the distinction between kasb and creation.

The Middle Position Between Compulsion and Free Will
Al-Taftazani presents the Ash'ari resolution of the jabr versus qadar dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine of 'amr bayn amrayn' and how it resolves the tension between divine compulsion and human free will by distinguishing near and distant principles.

Core Idea
Human actions are neither fully compelled nor fully free; they are an affair between the two.

Al-Taftazani concludes the long-standing dispute between Jabriyya and Qadariyya by presenting the Ash'ari resolution known as 'amr bayn amrayn'. This teaching reconciles the apparent contradictions in revelation and reason by distinguishing between the immediate human choice and ultimate divine determination. In this lesson, you will explore the arguments of both sides and the precise middle path that preserves both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opposing positions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting how al-Taftazani summarizes the reliance of the Jabriyya (on the need for a preponderant) and the Qadariyya (on the validity of praise and blame).
Pay attention to the self-evident premises (مقدمتان بديهيتان) used by both sides.
2
Understand the problem of contradictory evidences
Al-Taftazani shows that rational, scriptural, and rhetorical proofs are contradictory on this issue. Recognize why this leads to confusion and the need for a resolution.
The Qur'an and traditions are filled with evidence for both sides, which is why the dispute persists.
3
Grasp the resolution: amr bayn amrayn and the analogy
Focus on the key phrase 'لا جبر ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين' and the distinction between near and distant principles. Understand the analogy of the pen and the peg as an illustration of human action.
Man is like a pen in the hand of the writer: the pen's movement is determined by the writer, but the pen itself is not the real agent.

خاتمة في فصل النزاع
العبد مضطر في صورة المختار
فصل
(خاتمة : امتناع الترجح بلا مرجح وعدم العلم بتفاصيل الأفعال يعود إلى الجبر وحسن المدح والذم والأمر والنهي، وكون الأفعال تابعة لقصد العبد وداعية إلى المقدور، وكون العبد منبع النقصان يليق بالجبر وكثرة السفه، والعبث والقبح في الأفعال بالقدر والآيات والآثار متكاثرة في الجانبين. فالحق أنه لا جبر ولا تفويض ولكن أمر بين أمرين إذ المبادىء القريبة على الاختيار والبعيدة على الاضطرارية فالإنسان مضطر في صورة المختار) .
يشير إلى ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن حال هذه المسألة عجيبة. فإن الناس كانوا مختلفين فيها أبداً بسبب أن ما يمكن الرجوع إليها فيها متعارضة متدافعة فمعول الجبرية على أنه لا بد لترجح الفعل على الترك من مرجح، ليس من العبد، ومعول القدرية على أن العبد لو لم يكن قادراً على فعله لما حسن المدح والذم والأمر والنهي وهما مقدمتان بديهيتان، ثم من الدلائل العقلية اعتماد الجبرية على أن تفاصيل أحوال الأفعال غير معلومة للعبد، واعتماد القدرية على أن أفعال العباد واقعة على وقوع مقصودهم ودواعيهم وهما متعارضان ومن الإلزامات الخطابية أن القدرة على الإيجاد صفة كمال لا تليق بالعبد الذي هو منبع النقصان، وأن أفعال العباد تكون سفهاً وعبثاً فلا تليق بالمتعالي عن النقصان، وأما الدلائل السمعية، فالقرآن مملوء بما يوهم الأمرين، وكذا الآثار فإن أمة من الأمم لم تكن خالية من الفرقتين، وكذا الأوضاع والحكايات متدافعة من الجانبين حتى قيل : إن وضع النرد على الجبر، ووضع الشطرنج على القدر. إلا أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب أن القدح في قلوبنا لا يترجح ، لكن الأمر لا يترجح لممكن إلا بموجح يوجب السداد، باب إثبات الصانع ونحن نقول الحق ما قال بعض أئمة الدين : انه لا ولا تفويض، ولكن أمر بين أمرين، وذلك لأن من المبادىء القريبة لأفعال العباد على قدرته واختياره ، والمبادىء البعيدة على عجزه واضطراره. فإن الإنسان مضطر في صورة مختار، كالقلم في يد الكاتب والوتد في شق الحائطو في كلام العقلاء ، قال الحائط للوتد لم تشقني
فقال سل من يدقني .
Conclusion on settling the dispute. The servant is compelled in the form of the free. Section (Conclusion: The impossibility of preference without a preponderant, and lack of knowledge of the details of actions leads to compulsion; and the validity of praise and blame, command and prohibition; and actions being consequent upon the servant's intention and motive to the predetermined; and the servant being a source of deficiency befits compulsion; and the abundance of folly, absurdity, and ugliness in actions is by decree. Verses and traditions are abundant on both sides. So the truth is that there is neither compulsion nor delegation, but an affair between two affairs, because the near principles are by choice and the distant ones are by necessity. Thus man is compelled in the form of the free.) [The author] refers to what Imam al-Razi mentioned, that the state of this issue is wondrous. For people have always differed over it because what can be referred to in it is contradictory and conflicting. The reliance of the Jabriyya is that there must be a preponderant for the preference of action over inaction, and that preponderant is not from the servant. The reliance of the Qadariyya is that if the servant were not capable of his action, then praise and blame, command and prohibition would not be valid. Both are self-evident premises. Then from rational proofs: the Jabriyya rely on the fact that the details of the states of actions are unknown to the servant; the Qadariyya rely on the fact that the actions of servants occur according to the occurrence of their intent and motives. These are contradictory. And from rhetorical objections: that the power to create is a perfection attribute not befitting the servant who is a source of deficiency, and that the actions of servants would be foolish and absurd, thus not befitting the One exalted above deficiency. As for scriptural proofs, the Qur'an is filled with what suggests both matters, and likewise the traditions: no community has been free of the two groups. Likewise, customs and stories are conflicting from both sides, until it was said: The placement of backgammon [is attributed] to compulsion, and the placement of chess to free will. However, our school is stronger because the suspicion in our hearts does not preponderate, but the matter does not preponderate for a possible except by a preponderant that necessitates correctness. The chapter on establishing the Maker. And we say the truth is what some leaders of religion said: that it is neither compulsion nor delegation, but an affair between two affairs. That is because from the near principles of the actions of servants, they are under his power and choice, and from the distant principles, they are under his incapacity and necessity. For man is compelled in the form of the free, like the pen in the hand of the writer and the peg in the crack of the wall. And in the speech of the wise: The wall said to the peg, "Why have you split me?" So it said, "Ask the one who drives me."
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a concluding discussion on the theological dispute between compulsion (jabr) and free will (qadar), presenting the Ash'ari resolution known as 'amr bayn amrayn' (an affair between two affairs). The author begins by summarizing the core issue: the impossibility of preference without a preponderant and the lack of knowledge of action details lead to jabr, while the validity of praise and blame, and actions being based on intention, support qadar. Both sides have abundant scriptural evidence. The truth, however, is neither pure jabr nor pure tafwid (delegation), but a middle position: the near principles (immediate causes) of actions are within human choice, while the distant principles (ultimately, divine creation) are by necessity. The author then references Imam al-Razi's observation that the issue is perplexing because the evidences are contradictory. He outlines the two positions: the Jabriyya rely on the need for a preponderant from outside the servant, and the Qadariyya rely on the necessity of capability for moral responsibility. Both premises are self-evident yet contradictory. Rational proofs include the Jabriyya's argument that the details of action states are unknown to the servant, and the Qadariyya's argument that actions occur according to the agent's intent. Rhetorical objections highlight that the power to create is a perfection not befitting the deficient servant, and that attributing independent causality to humans would imply folly. Scriptural proofs are equally ambiguous. The author then affirms the Ash'ari school as stronger, explaining that truth is the middle position: near principles are by human power and choice, distant principles by divine necessity. Man is like a pen in the writer's hand or a peg in a wall, a famous analogy illustrating compulsion in the form of choice.

Key Terms
جبر
تفويض
أمر بين أمرين
مرجح
اختيار
اضطرار
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'ari position of 'amr bayn amrayn' is a mere compromise or that it collapses into jabr.
Correction: This passage clarifies that it is a precise doctrine distinguishing near and distant principles: humans have real choice in immediate causes, while the ultimate creation of actions is from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between the near and distant principles of human actions?
A) Near principles are by divine decree, distant principles are by human choice.
B) Near principles are by human power and choice, distant principles are by divine necessity.
C) Both are entirely by human choice.
D) Both are entirely by divine compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of 'amr bayn amrayn' affirms human responsibility for near causes while acknowledging divine sovereignty over distant causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you sinned and felt that you had no control. In light of this lesson, how do your near principles (your choice) and distant principles (God's decree) interact in that sin? Will you now take full responsibility for your choice while trusting Allah's forgiveness? What concrete step can you take tonight to own that choice before Him?
?
The peg in the wall says, 'Ask the one who drives me.' When you face a hardship, do you see yourself as the peg blaming God, or do you accept your role as the apparent actor? How does this metaphor transform your complaint into submission? Write down one trial you are currently facing and reframe it: 'I am the peg, and I choose to trust the Driver.'
?
This lesson reveals that your actions are both yours and not yours. Are there areas in your life where you have fallen into either extreme: fatalism (jabr) or self-reliance (tafwid)? Today, identify one habit where you can practice 'amr bayn amrayn' by acting with full effort while praying for divine help. How will you remind yourself of this balance in your next decision?

Definitions of Qada and Qadar Among Schools
Lists definitions of qada and qadar from three schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Sunna, Mu'tazila, and philosophers define qada and qadar, and the distinction between decree and decreed.

Core Idea
Qada and qadar are defined differently, but all affirm events are by God's decree.

This lesson introduces the technical meanings of qada and qadar as used by major theological schools. You will see how each group defines these terms and the implications for divine action and human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying each school's definition
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the definitions given by Ahl al-Sunna, Mu'tazila, and philosophers.
The passage is structured as a series of definitions.
2
Compare the definitions
Compare the Ahl al-Sunna view (creation and predestination) with the Mu'tazila and philosophers' views. Notice how each school emphasizes different aspects.
The Ahl al-Sunna view is the most comprehensive.
3
Reflect on acceptance of the decree
Consider the statement that acceptance (rida) is due only to the decree itself, not the decreed. This is a key theological nuance.
This distinguishes God's act from the created event.

قال وأفعاله بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره بمعنى خلقه، وتقديره ابتداء أو بوسط موجب والرضا إنما يجب بالقضاء دون المقضي، وعند المعتزلة لا يصلح إلا بمعنى الإعلام والكتابة أو بمعنى الإلزام في الواجبات خاصة، وقالت الفلاسفة : القضاء وجود الكائنات في العالم العقلي مجملة والقدر وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة . ودخول الشر في القضاء بالتبعية. قد اشتهر من بين أكثر الملل أن الحوادث بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره، وهذا يتناول أفعال العباد، وأمره ظاهر عند أهل الحق لما تبين أنه الخالق لها نفسها، أو الخالق للقدرة والداعية الموجبتين لها، فمعنى القضاء والقدر الخلق والتقدير كما في قوله تعالى فقضاهن سبع سموات (سورة فصلت: 12) وقوله تعالى وقدر فيها أقواتها (سورة فصلت: 10) ولا يستقيم هذا عند القدرية، وقد يكون القضاء والقدر معنى الإيجاب والإلزام كما في قوله تعالى وقضى ربك ألا تعبدوا إلا إياه (سورة الإسراء: 23) وقوله تعالى نحن قدرنا بينكم الموت (سورة الواقعة: 60) فيكون الواجبات بالقضاء والقدر دون البواقي، وقد يراد بهما الإعلام والتبيين لقوله تعالى وقضينا إلى بني إسرائيل في الكتاب لتفسدن في الأرض (سورة الإسراء: 4) الآية وقوله تعالى إلا امرأته قدرناها من الغابرين (سورة النمل: 57) أي أعلمنا بذلك وكتبناه في اللوح فعلى هذا جميع الأفعال بالقضاء والقدر.
He said: And His acts are by the decree (qada) of God Most High and His predestination (qadar), meaning His creation and His determining from the beginning or through an intermediate cause. And acceptance (al-rida) is only required with respect to the decree, not the decreed. According to the Mu'tazila, it is only valid in the sense of informing and writing, or in the sense of obligation specifically for duties. The philosophers said: Al-qada is the existence of beings in the intellectual world in summary, and al-qadar is their existence in their external matters in detail. And the entry of evil into qada is by way of secondary inclusion. It is well-known among most religions that events are by God's decree and predestination, and this includes the acts of servants. And its matter is apparent to the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) since it has been made clear that He is the creator of those acts themselves, or the creator of the power and the motive that necessitate them. So the meaning of qada and qadar is creation and predestination, as in His statement 'Then He decreed them (faqadahunna) as seven heavens' (Quran 41:12) and His statement 'And He determined (qaddara) therein its provisions' (Quran 41:10). And this is not valid according to the Qadariyya. And sometimes qada and qadar mean obligation and enforcement, as in His statement 'And your Lord has decreed (qada) that you worship none but Him' (Quran 17:23) and His statement 'We have decreed (qadarna) death among you' (Quran 56:60). So the obligations are by decree and predestination, not the rest. And sometimes they are intended to mean information and clarification, as in His statement 'And We decreed (qadayna) to the Children of Israel in the Book: You will surely cause corruption on the earth' (Quran 17:4) — the verse, and His statement 'except his wife: We determined (qadarna) her to be among those who remained behind' (Quran 27:57), meaning We informed of that and wrote it in the Tablet. So on this basis, all acts are by decree and predestination.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the terms qada and qadar (divine decree and predestination). It begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Sunna: acts are by God's decree and predestination, meaning His creation and determination either directly or through an intermediate cause. Acceptance (al-rida) is due only to the decree itself, not to the decreed object (so one accepts God's decree even if the decreed event is disliked). Then it contrasts the Mu'tazila, who restrict the meaning to informing/writing or to obligation for duties only. Next, it presents the philosophers' view: qada is the universal existence of things in the intellectual world, and qadar is their detailed existence in matter; evil enters into qada only secondarily (as a necessary consequence). The passage then notes that it is widely accepted among most religious groups that events are by qada and qadar, including human acts. For Ahl al-Haqq, this is evident because God creates the acts themselves or creates the power and motive that necessitate them. The core meaning of qada and qadar is creation and predestination, supported by Quran 41:12 ('faqadahunna') and 41:10 ('qaddara'). This meaning is rejected by the Qadariyya. The passage then acknowledges other valid meanings: obligation/enforcement (as in Quran 17:23 and 56:60), which applies only to obligatory acts; and information/clarification (as in Quran 17:4 and 27:57), meaning God informed and wrote it in the Tablet. The conclusion is that on the basis of the information meaning, all acts (obligatory and non-obligatory) are by qada and qadar, because God's knowledge encompasses everything.

Key Terms
قضاء
قدر
خلق
تقدير
معتزلة
فلاسفة
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qada and qadar always mean blind fate or coercion of human actions.
Correction: The passage shows they have multiple meanings including creation, obligation, and information; the Ahl al-Sunna view emphasizes God's creation of acts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ahl al-Sunna's primary meaning of qada and qadar?
A) Creation and predestination
B) Inform and write
C) Obligation for duties
D) Summary and detailed existence

Key Takeaway
The meaning of qada and qadar varies by school, but the Ahl al-Sunna define them as God's creation and predestination, with acceptance of the decree itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a painful event occurs, do you find yourself questioning God's wisdom or quietly resenting His decree? The passage teaches that you must accept the decree itself even if you dislike what was decreed. Will you commit today to saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala kulli hal' even in your hardest moments, separating your feelings about the event from your acceptance of God's choice?
?
Have you ever acted as though God's decree is merely a set of rules (like the Mu'tazila view) rather than His direct, moment-by-moment creation? How would your day change if you saw every breath—and every trial—as a fresh act of divine creation? What one thought can you carry to remind yourself of this truth?
?
The philosophers reduce qada and qadar to a necessary emanation, almost mechanical. Do you ever feel that life runs on automatic—cause and effect—without a personal God actively deciding? This lesson calls you back to the Sunni view: every event is by God's creation and predestination. Will you pause before each action today to say, 'This is by God's decree' and mean it from your heart?

Ahl al-Sunna Position and Quranic Meanings
Explains Ahl al-Sunna position and additional Quranic meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq position that all events are by God's decree, and the Quranic evidence for different meanings (creation, obligation, information).

Core Idea
Quranic usage shows qada and qadar can mean creation, obligation, or information, all pointing to God's absolute control.

This lesson continues the discussion, focusing on the Ahl al-Sunna's proof that God creates all acts, and then explores additional meanings of qada and qadar found in the Quran, concluding that all acts are encompassed by God's decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on universal acceptance of qada and qadar
Read the passage that states most religions accept that events are by God's decree, including human acts. Note the Ahl al-Haqq's evidence.
This establishes the broad consensus.
2
Identify the primary meaning: creation
Find the Quranic verses used to support the creation meaning (41:12, 41:10). Understand why this is rejected by the Qadariyya.
3
Identify the other meanings: obligation and information
Look at the verses for obligation (17:23, 56:60) and information (17:4, 27:57). Note how each meaning applies to different types of acts.
The obligation meaning applies only to duties; the information meaning applies to all acts.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that based on the information meaning, all acts are by qada and qadar. Reflect on why this is significant.
God's knowledge encompasses everything.
