Philosophers' Definition of al-Qaḍā' and al-Qadar
The philosophers define al-qaḍā' and al-qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers distinguish between al-qaḍā' (divine decree as unified pre-existence) and al-qadar (predestination as detailed temporal execution) based on their cosmology of emanation and matter.

Core Idea
Qaḍā' is the blueprint; qadar is the execution in time.

The philosophers build their definition of divine decree and predestination on a specific metaphysical framework involving an intellectual world where all forms exist collectively. This lesson unpacks that framework and the distinction between the two terms, supported by a Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument.
2
Understand the metaphysical background
The passage explains that all forms of beings exist in the intellectual world through origination (ibdā') of the First Necessary Being. Grasp this concept of emanation.
3
Distinguish qaḍā' and qadar
Notice that qaḍā' is defined as the collective, summarized existence in the intellectual world, while qadar is the detailed existence in external matter one after another over time.
4
Connect to the Qur'anic verse
The verse about treasuries and a known measure (15:21) is cited as support. Reflect on how it aligns with the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة لما (١) كانت جميع صور الموجدات الكلية والجزئية حاصلة حيث هي معقولة في العالم العقلي بإبداع الأول الواجب إياها، وكان إيجاد ما يتعلق منها بالمادة في المادة مختلفاً على سبيل الإبداع ممتنعاً إذ هي غير متأتية لقبول صورتين معاً فضلاً عن أكثر، وكان الجود الإلهي مقتضياً لتكميل المادة بإبداع تلك الصور فيها، وإخراج ما فيها بالقوة من قبول تلك الصور إلى العقل قدر بلطيف حكمته زماناً يخرج فيه تلك الأمور من القوة إلى الفعل، فالقضاء عبارة من وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة ومجملة على سبيل الإبداع، والقدرة عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد، كما قال عز من قائل وإن من شيء إلا عندنا خزائنه وما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم (٢) قالوا ودخول الشر في القضاء الإلهي على سبيل التبع، فإن الموجود إما خير محض كالعقول والأفلاك، أو الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم، فإن المرض وإن كثر فالصحة أكثر منه، ولما امتنع عقلاً إيجادها (۳) في هذا العالم مبرأ عن الشرور بالكلية، فإن المطر المخصب للبلاد يخرب بعض الدور بالضرورة وجب في الحكمة إيجاده لأن ترك الخير الكثير، لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير، فدخل الشر في القضاء، وإن كان مكروهاً غير مرضى
The philosophers said: When all forms of beings, universal and particular, are present as they are intelligible in the intellectual world through origination (ibdā') of the First Necessary Being (al-wājib), and the bringing into existence of what pertains to matter in matter in a different way through origination is impossible since matter is not capable of receiving two forms together, let alone more, and the divine generosity requires perfecting matter by originating those forms in it, and bringing out what is potential in it from accepting those forms to actuality by a measure, through the subtlety of His wisdom, a time in which those things emerge from potentiality to actuality, then al-qaḍā' (decree) is the expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world, gathered and summarized, by way of origination, and al-qadar (predestination) is the expression for their existence in their external matters in detail, one after another, as the Mighty and Majestic said: 'There is not a thing but with Us are its treasures, and We do not send it down except in a known measure' (Qur'an 15:21). They said: And the entry of evil into the divine decree is by way of secondary [consideration]. For the existent is either pure good, like the intellects and spheres, or good predominates over it as in this world. For illness, even if abundant, health is more abundant. And since it is rationally impossible to bring them into existence in this world free from evils entirely—indeed, rain that fertilizes lands inevitably destroys some houses—it was necessary in wisdom to bring it into existence, because abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil. So evil entered into the decree, even though it is disliked and not approved.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' definition of divine decree (al-qaḍā') and predestination (al-qadar) along with their theodicy for the presence of evil. It is an argumentative definition that proceeds in several steps. First, it establishes that all forms of beings exist in the intellectual world as objects of knowledge through the origination (ibdā') of the First Necessary Being. Then it explains that matter cannot receive multiple forms simultaneously, so the divine generosity requires that forms be originated in matter gradually over time. Thus, al-qaḍā' is defined as the unified, collective existence of all things in the intellectual world by way of origination, while al-qadar is their detailed existence in external matters one after another, supported by the Qur'anic verse about the treasuries and the known measure. Second, the passage addresses the entry of evil. It argues that existence is either pure good (like intellects and spheres) or predominantly good (like this world). Since it is rationally impossible to create this world entirely free from evils—rain that fertilizes land inevitably destroys some houses—wisdom requires bringing it into existence; abandoning much good for a little evil would be a greater evil. Thus, evil enters the decree as a necessary byproduct, even though it is disliked. This passage clearly explains the philosophers' deterministic cosmology and their justification for the presence of evil as a secondary consequence of a greater good.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qaḍā')
القدر (al-qadar)
الإبداع (al-ibdā')
العالم العقلي (al-'ālam al-'aqlī)
المادة (al-mādda)
الجود الإلهي (al-jūd al-ilāhī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: al-qaḍā' and al-qadar are synonyms.
Correction: They are distinct: qaḍā' is unified pre-existence in the intellectual world, qadar is detailed temporal execution in matter.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is al-qaḍā'?
A) The detailed existence of things in matter one by one
B) The unified collective existence of all things in the intellectual world by way of ibdā'
C) The divine decree that includes only good
D) The measure of rain

Key Takeaway
Philosophers define qaḍā' as the collective existence of all things in the intellectual world and qadar as their detailed sequential existence in matter, with the Qur'anic verse as support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire existence—every joy, trial, and moment—has existed as a unified blueprint in the intellectual world before time began, how does that reshape your perspective on the difficult events you face today? Will you trust the divine timing of their emergence, or will you fight against the qadar that unfolds them slowly?
?
The philosophers argue that divine generosity requires matter to be perfected gradually. Where in your life have you demanded instant perfection from yourself or others, denying the patient process that divine wisdom has decreed? How can you embrace the qadar of gradual growth, even when it feels slow?
?
You cannot receive two forms at once; your nature has limits. What are the competing 'forms' you are trying to hold simultaneously—ambitions, relationships, distractions—that contradict the reality of your own capacity? Will you accept the qadar of your finitude and let go of what you were never meant to carry at once?

Philosophers' Theodicy: Evil as Secondary in Divine Decree
The philosophers explain the entry of evil into divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that evil enters the divine decree only as a necessary byproduct of a greater good, and why they consider this rational.

Core Idea
Evil is a necessary accompaniment to a world that is predominantly good.

Having defined qaḍā' and qadar, the philosophers turn to the problem of evil. They argue that existence is either pure good or predominantly good. Since a world free from all evil is impossible, wisdom requires creating this world despite its evils, because the greater good outweighs the lesser evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the theodicy argument.
2
Understand the classification of beings
Notice that existence is divided into pure good (intellects, spheres) and predominantly good (this world). The example of health and illness illustrates predominance.
3
Grasp the necessity argument
Evil is inevitable in a material world; rain benefits but destroys houses. Since avoiding evil entirely would mean abandoning much good, wisdom demands creating this world despite evil.

وقالت الفلاسفة لما (١) كانت جميع صور الموجدات الكلية والجزئية حاصلة حيث هي معقولة في العالم العقلي بإبداع الأول الواجب إياها، وكان إيجاد ما يتعلق منها بالمادة في المادة مختلفاً على سبيل الإبداع ممتنعاً إذ هي غير متأتية لقبول صورتين معاً فضلاً عن أكثر، وكان الجود الإلهي مقتضياً لتكميل المادة بإبداع تلك الصور فيها، وإخراج ما فيها بالقوة من قبول تلك الصور إلى العقل قدر بلطيف حكمته زماناً يخرج فيه تلك الأمور من القوة إلى الفعل، فالقضاء عبارة من وجود جميع الموجودات في العالم العقلي مجتمعة ومجملة على سبيل الإبداع، والقدرة عبارة عن وجودها في موادها الخارجية مفصلة واحداً بعد واحد، كما قال عز من قائل وإن من شيء إلا عندنا خزائنه وما ننزله إلا بقدر معلوم (٢) قالوا ودخول الشر في القضاء الإلهي على سبيل التبع، فإن الموجود إما خير محض كالعقول والأفلاك، أو الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم، فإن المرض وإن كثر فالصحة أكثر منه، ولما امتنع عقلاً إيجادها (۳) في هذا العالم مبرأ عن الشرور بالكلية، فإن المطر المخصب للبلاد يخرب بعض الدور بالضرورة وجب في الحكمة إيجاده لأن ترك الخير الكثير، لأجل الشر القليل شر كثير، فدخل الشر في القضاء، وإن كان مكروهاً غير مرضى
The philosophers said: When all forms of beings, universal and particular, are present as they are intelligible in the intellectual world through origination (ibdā') of the First Necessary Being (al-wājib), and the bringing into existence of what pertains to matter in matter in a different way through origination is impossible since matter is not capable of receiving two forms together, let alone more, and the divine generosity requires perfecting matter by originating those forms in it, and bringing out what is potential in it from accepting those forms to actuality by a measure, through the subtlety of His wisdom, a time in which those things emerge from potentiality to actuality, then al-qaḍā' (decree) is the expression for the existence of all existents in the intellectual world, gathered and summarized, by way of origination, and al-qadar (predestination) is the expression for their existence in their external matters in detail, one after another, as the Mighty and Majestic said: 'There is not a thing but with Us are its treasures, and We do not send it down except in a known measure' (Qur'an 15:21). They said: And the entry of evil into the divine decree is by way of secondary [consideration]. For the existent is either pure good, like the intellects and spheres, or good predominates over it as in this world. For illness, even if abundant, health is more abundant. And since it is rationally impossible to bring them into existence in this world free from evils entirely—indeed, rain that fertilizes lands inevitably destroys some houses—it was necessary in wisdom to bring it into existence, because abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil. So evil entered into the decree, even though it is disliked and not approved.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' definition of divine decree (al-qaḍā') and predestination (al-qadar) along with their theodicy for the presence of evil. It is an argumentative definition that proceeds in several steps. First, it establishes that all forms of beings exist in the intellectual world as objects of knowledge through the origination (ibdā') of the First Necessary Being. Then it explains that matter cannot receive multiple forms simultaneously, so the divine generosity requires that forms be originated in matter gradually over time. Thus, al-qaḍā' is defined as the unified, collective existence of all things in the intellectual world by way of origination, while al-qadar is their detailed existence in external matters one after another, supported by the Qur'anic verse about the treasuries and the known measure. Second, the passage addresses the entry of evil. It argues that existence is either pure good (like intellects and spheres) or predominantly good (like this world). Since it is rationally impossible to create this world entirely free from evils—rain that fertilizes land inevitably destroys some houses—wisdom requires bringing it into existence; abandoning much good for a little evil would be a greater evil. Thus, evil enters the decree as a necessary byproduct, even though it is disliked. This passage clearly explains the philosophers' deterministic cosmology and their justification for the presence of evil as a secondary consequence of a greater good.

Key Terms
الخير والشر (al-khayr wa al-sharr)
الجود الإلهي (al-jūd al-ilāhī)
العقول (al-'uqūl)
الأفلاك (al-aflāk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil is directly willed by God.
Correction: Evil enters the decree only as a secondary, necessary byproduct of a greater good.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why does evil enter the divine decree?
A) Because God wills evil directly
B) As a secondary consequence of creating a predominantly good world
C) Because evil is an illusion
D) Because matter is evil

Key Takeaway
Evil is not intended for itself but is an unavoidable side effect of a world where good predominates, justified by the principle that abandoning much good for little evil is a greater evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial like illness or loss, do you truly believe that it is a secondary consequence of a greater good, or do you secretly feel that God could have prevented it without losing the good? What concrete step can you take today to align your heart with the wisdom that the good outweighs the evil?
?
Think of a time when you received a great blessing that came with unavoidable pain—like rain that fertilizes but also damages. Can you honestly say that the good outweighed the evil? If not, how does this passage challenge you to re-evaluate your perspective, and how might that change your gratitude?
?
The philosophers argue that abandoning much good for a small evil is itself a great evil. Where in your life are you holding back from doing something good—like helping someone, speaking truth, or pursuing righteousness—because you fear a minor inconvenience or risk? What is one step you will take this week to act on that good, trusting that God's wisdom permits small harms for greater good?

Qada and Qadar: Sunni Doctrine and Refutation of Qadariyya
Refutation of Qadariyya through definitions and Imam Ali's statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Sunni doctrine of qada and qadar, and how it refutes the Qadariyya claim that belief in destiny nullifies moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Qadar does not negate choice; it affirms God's command and judgment.

This lesson addresses a central misunderstanding about predestination: that it removes human accountability. Through a dialogue and citations from Imam Ali, the author defines qada and qadar as God's command and judgment, and refutes the Qadariyya who argue that God's decree justifies sin. The passage also warns against both fatalism and absolute free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing how the author argues against the Qadari objection that necessary qada and qadar nullify reward and punishment.
Pay attention to the structure: dialogue, definition, refutation, then citations.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find where qada and qadar are defined as 'amr' (command) and 'hukm' (judgment). See how this differs from the Qadari misunderstanding of inevitability.
The definition is given in response to the sheikh's question.
3
Analyze the refutation and Ali's legacy
Notice how the author refutes the Qadariyya as 'Magians' and then cites Imam Ali's three statements to establish the Sunni middle path: belief in qadar without fatalism, action within qadar, and rejection of partnership with God.
These citations are essential for understanding the balanced Sunni position.

لائمة الشيخ كيف والقضاء القدر ساقنا، فقال ويحك لعلك ظننت قضاء لازماً، وقدراً حتماً لو كان كذلك لبطل الثواب والعقاب والوعد والوعيد والأمر والنهي، ولم تأت من الله لمذنب، ولا محمدة لمحسن، ولم يكن المحسن أولى بالمدح من المسيء ولا المسيء أولى بالذم من المحسن تلك مقالة عبدة الأوثان، وجنود الشياطين وشهود الزور، وأهل العمي عن الصواب، وهم قدرية هذه الأمة ومجوسها. إن الله أمر تخييراً ونهى تحذيراً، وكلف يسيراً، لم يعص مغلوباً، ولم يطع مستكرهاً، ولم يرسل الرسل إلى خلقه عبثاً، ولم يخلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا فويل للذين كفروا من النار . فقال الشيخ وما القضاء والقدر اللذان ما سرنا إلا بهما ؟ قال : هو الأمر من الله والحكم ثم تلا قوله تعالى وقضى ربك ألا تعبدوا إلا إياه وعن الحسن بعث الله تعالى محمداً إلى العرب وهم قدرية يحملون ذنوبهم إلى الله وتصديقه قوله تعالى و إذا فعلوا فاحشة قالوا وجدنا عليها أباءنا والله أمرنا بها عنهم . وقد صح عنه أنه قال قلنا ما ذكر لا يدل إلا على أن القول بأن فعل العبد إذا كان بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره وخلقه وإرادته يجوز للعبد الإقدام عليه ويبطل اختياره فيه، واستحقاقه للثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم عليه قول المجوس فلينظر أن هذا قول المعتزلة أم المجبرة ولكن من لم يجعل الله له نوراً فما له من نور. ومن وقاحتهم أنهم يروجون باطلهم بنسبته إلى مثل أمير المؤمنين علي وأولاده رضي الله خطب الناس على منبر الكوفة ، فقال ليس منا من لم يؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره وأنه حين أراد حرب الشام قال شمرت عن ثوبي ودعوت قنبراً : قدم لوائي لا تؤخر حذراً لن يرفع الحذار ما قد قدرا وأنه قال لمن قال إني أملك الخير والشر والطاعة والمعصية تملكها مع الله أو تملكها بدون الله ، فإن قلت أملكها مع الله فقد ادعيت أنك شريك الله ، وإن قلت أملكها بدون الله فقد ادعيت أنك أنت الله» . فتاب الرجل على يده.
The sheikh blamed? how qada and qadar brought us, and said: Woe to you, perhaps you thought qada is necessary and qadar is inevitable; if it were so, reward, punishment, promise, threat, command, and prohibition would be nullified, and no sin would come from God, no praise for a good doer, and the good doer would not be more deserving of praise than the evil doer, nor the evil doer more deserving of blame than the good doer. That is the saying of idol worshippers, the soldiers of Satan, the false witnesses, and the people blind to truth, and they are the Qadariyya of this nation and its Magians. Indeed, God commanded as a choice and forbade as a warning, and charged lightly; He is not disobeyed by force, nor obeyed under compulsion, and He did not send messengers to His creation in vain, and He did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in vain; that is the assumption of those who disbelieve, so woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire. Then the sheikh said: What are qada and qadar by which we were only driven? He said: They are the command and judgment from God. Then he recited the saying of the Most High: And your Lord has commanded that you worship none but Him (17:23). And from al-Hasan: God sent Muhammad to the Arabs while they were Qadariyya, carrying their sins to God, and confirming His saying: And when they commit an immorality, they say: We found our fathers doing it, and Allah has commanded us to do it (7:28) from them. And it was authentically reported from him that he said: We said: What was mentioned only indicates that the saying that if the servant's action is by God's decree, predestination, creation, and will, then the servant is permitted to undertake it and his choice is nullified, and his deserving reward, punishment, praise, and blame for it is the saying of the Magians. So let it be considered whether this is the saying of the Mu'tazila or the Mujbira, but whoever God has not granted light has no light. And from their impudence is that they promote their falsehood by attributing it to the likes of the Commander of the Faithful Ali and his sons, may God be pleased with them. He addressed the people on the pulpit of Kufa and said: He is not from us who does not believe in qadar, its good and its evil. And when he intended the war of Syria, he said: I rolled up my sleeves and called Qanbar: bring my banner forward, do not delay out of caution; caution will not raise what has been decreed. And he said to one who said: I possess good and evil, obedience and disobedience: Do you possess it with God or without God? If you say I possess it with God, you claim to be God's partner; if you say I possess it without God, you claim to be God Himself. So the man repented at his hand.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a composite refutation of Qadariyya, beginning with a dialogue about qada and qadar and ending with a citation from Imam Ali. The first part presents a Qadari objection that if qada and qadar are necessary, then reward, punishment, etc., are nullified. The author attributes this view to idol worshippers and calls the Qadariyya the 'Magians of this nation'. He then affirms that God's command and prohibition are based on choice, not compulsion. The sheikh defines qada and qadar as God's command and judgment, citing Quran 17:23. Further, he quotes al-Hasan that the Arabs were Qadariyya, attributing their sins to God, citing Quran 7:28. The author then argues that the claim that God's decree permits sin and nullifies choice is the saying of the Magians, and notes that this error is shared by Mu'tazila and Mujbira. Finally, he cites Imam Ali's sermons: one affirming belief in qadar, one showing activism within qadar, and a third refuting the idea that humans possess power alongside God, thus establishing the Sunni middle path.

Correction: The passage shows that correct belief in qadar affirms that God's command and judgment are based on choice, and humans are accountable. Imam Ali's statements demonstrate both reliance on qadar and active effort.

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Refutation of a Qadari Using Surah al-Fatiha
A story of Ja'far al-Sadiq refuting a Qadari using Surah al-Fatiha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq used a simple Quranic verse to refute the Qadari belief in independent human action.

Core Idea
Seeking help from God implies dependence on Him for all actions.

The Qadariyya sect claimed that humans create their own actions independently of God's will. Ja'far al-Sadiq, a renowned scholar, confronted a Qadari by asking him to recite Surah al-Fatiha. When the Qadari reached the verse 'Iyyaka na'budu wa iyyaka nasta'in' (You alone we worship, and You alone we seek help), Ja'far posed a simple yet profound question that exposed the inconsistency in the Qadari's belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the story.
Notice the structure: recitation, question, silence.
2
Understand the Qadari position
The Qadari believed that human actions are independent of God's power and will. Identify this in the story.
The Qadari's view is implied in Ja'far's question.
3
Analyze Ja'far's question
Why does Ja'far ask 'From whom do you seek help?' Consider the implications of the verse 'Iyyaka nasta'in'.
The verse itself asserts dependence on God.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The Qadari is silenced because his belief contradicts the Quranic prayer for help. Write a short explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن جعفر الصادق (۱) ، قال لقدري : اقرأ الفاتحة فقرأ . فلما بلغ قوله ( إياك نعبد وإياك نستعين (۲) قال له جعفر على ماذا تستعين بالله وعندك أن الفعل منك وجميع ما يتعلق بالأقـدار والتمكين والألطاف قد حصلت وتمت ، فانقطع القدري والحمد لله رب العالمين. (۱) هو جعفر بن محمد الباقر بن علي زين العابدين بن الحسين السبط الهاشمي القرشي أبو عبد الله الملقب بالصادق. سادس الأئمة الاثني عشر عند الإمامية. كان من أجلاء التابعين وله منزلة رفيعة في العلم ، أخذ عنه جماعة منهم الإمامان أبو حنيفة ومالك ولقب بالصادق لأنه لم يعرف عنه الكذب قط له أخبار مع الخلفاء وكان جريتاً عليهم صداعاً بالحق له رسائل مجموعة في كتاب ورد ذكرها في كشف الظنون يقال إن جابر بن حيان قام بجمعها توفي سنة ١٤٨ هـ . راجع وفيات الأعيان ۱ : ۱۰۵ وصفة الصفوة ٢ : ٩٤ . (۲) سورة الفاتحة آية ٥ .
And that Ja'far al-Sadiq (1) said to a Qadari: Recite al-Fatiha. So he recited. When he reached His saying: You alone we worship and You alone we seek help (2), Ja'far said to him: From whom do you seek help from God when according to you the action is from you and all that pertains to decrees, empowerment, and graces have already been obtained and completed? So the Qadari was silenced, and praise be to God, Lord of the worlds. (1) He is Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Baqir ibn Ali Zayn al-Abidin ibn al-Husayn al-Sibt al-Hashimi al-Qurashi, Abu Abd Allah, nicknamed al-Sadiq. The sixth of the twelve Imams according to the Imamiyya. He was among the eminent followers (tabi'un) and had a high rank in knowledge. A group took from him, including the two Imams Abu Hanifa and Malik. He was nicknamed al-Sadiq because he was never known to lie. He had interactions with caliphs and was bold with them, proclaiming the truth. He had collected epistles mentioned in Kashf al-Zunun; it is said Jabir ibn Hayyan compiled them. He died in 148 AH. Refer to Wafayat al-A'yan 1:105 and Sifat al-Safwa 2:94. (2) Surah al-Fatiha, verse 5.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a narrative refutation of Qadariyya using a direct example from Ja'far al-Sadiq. The Qadari believed that human actions are independent of God's power and will. Ja'far al-Sadiq asks him to recite Surah al-Fatiha. When the Qadari reaches verse 5, 'You alone we worship and You alone we seek help (ista'in),' Ja'far poses a dilemma: whom are you seeking help from? If you believe action is entirely from you and that divine decrees, empowerment, and graces are already complete, then the prayer for help is meaningless. The Qadari is silenced, unable to reconcile his belief with the Quranic verse. The attached footnote provides a brief biography of Ja'far al-Sadiq, noting his scholarly status, his teachers including Abu Hanifa and Malik, and his epithet 'al-Sadiq' (the truthful).

Ja'far al-Sadiq's Refutation of the Qadari
Anecdote of Ja'far al-Sadiq using the Fatiha to refute a Qadari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far al-Sadiq used the Fatiha to logically refute the Qadari belief in independent human action.

Core Idea
The Fatiha itself refutes the Qadari: if you ask God for help, you admit dependence.

This lesson presents a powerful anecdote from early Islamic history where Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq engages a Qadari—someone who denies that God creates human actions—in debate. By simply asking the Qadari to recite the Fatiha and pausing at a particular verse, Ja'far exposes an internal contradiction in the Qadari's own worship. The story illustrates a fundamental principle of Ahl al-Sunnah: the necessity of relying on God's will in all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key elements: the Qadari is asked to recite, the verse, and Ja'far's question.
2
Identify the contradiction
Reflect on Ja'far's question: if the Qadari believes his actions are independent of God, why does he ask God for help? The act of seeking help implies that help comes from God, contradicting the Qadari's position.
3
Understand the refutation
Recognize that the refutation is not merely rhetorical but uses the Qadari's own scripture against him. This shows the importance of the Qur'an in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأن جعفر الصادق، قال لقدري: اقرأ الفاتحة فقرأ. فلما بلغ قوله (إياك نعبد وإياك نستعين) قال له جعفر: على ماذا تستعين بالله وعندك أن الفعل منك وجميع ما يتعلق بالأقدار والتمكين والألطاف قد حصلت وتمت، فانقطع القدري والحمد لله رب العالمين.
And that Ja'far al-Sadiq said to a Qadari: 'Recite the Fatiha.' So he recited. When he reached His saying 'You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help,' Ja'far said to him: 'How can you ask God for help while according to you the action is from you, and all that pertains to predestinations, enablement, and graces have been obtained and completed?' So the Qadari was silenced, and praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage recounts an anecdote where Ja'far al-Sadiq refutes a Qadari (one who denies divine predestination of human actions) by using the very words of the Fatiha. The Qadari is asked to recite the Fatiha. When he reaches the verse 'You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help,' Ja'far points out the contradiction: if the Qadari believes that his actions are independent of God, then why does he ask God for help? The verse implies that all help comes from God, and the act of seeking help acknowledges that one depends on God. The Qadari is left without an answer.

Key Terms
Qadari
Fatiha
istia'na (seeking help)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadaris believe that human actions are entirely independent of God's will and that God has no control over them.
Correction: The Fatiha verse 'You alone we ask for help' shows that even the Qadari acknowledges dependence on God. The act of seeking help contradicts the belief in independent action.

Checkpoint
What verse from the Fatiha did Ja'far al-Sadiq use to refute the Qadari?
A) 'Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds'
B) 'You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help'
C) 'Guide us to the straight path'
D) 'The path of those whom You have favored'

Key Takeaway
The Fatiha's verse 'You alone we ask for help' is a clear Qur'anic refutation of the Qadari belief in human autonomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you recite 'You alone we ask for help' in your prayers while still relying on your own plans and efforts? How often do you consciously realize that your ability to act is from Allah, not from yourself? Commit to reciting this verse with full presence of mind, acknowledging your need for His help in every action.
?
If a Qadari was silenced by this verse, what does it say about your own heart when you feel self-sufficient? When have you felt that your success is due to your own effort, forgetting the One who gave you the ability? Repent and renew your reliance on Allah today.
?
Why do you recite 'Iyyaka nasta'in' if you still believe deep down that your choices are independent of Allah's will? Ask yourself: Is my daily life consistent with my belief in divine decree? If not, what specific changes can I make starting tonight?

The Polytheist Objection as Mockery
Refutation of polytheist claim as mockery followed by two verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the polytheist claim 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners' is invalid because it was made in mockery, and how the divine response in two verses exposes this.

Core Idea
The polytheists' claim was mockery, not theology.

The polytheists used a seemingly theological argument to justify their shirk, but the Quran exposes their real intent: mockery. This lesson unpacks that refutation, showing how Allah's response in two verses treats them as deniers and affirms His absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author starts by identifying the motive behind the polytheist statement.
Pay attention to the word 'استهزاء' (mockery) – it is key.
2
Identify the core claim and its refutation
The polytheists said 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners.' The author says this was for mockery, not a sincere question. Then he quotes two verses: 6:148 and 6:149.
Verse 6:148 declares them deniers; verse 6:149 states that if Allah willed, He would have guided all.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does the Quran respond this way? Because the objection was not genuine. This shows that some theological objections are insincere and should be addressed by exposing intent rather than giving a detailed logical answer.
This connects to broader principles in responding to doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرد على الذين قالوا لو شاء الله ما أشركنا ولا آباؤنا فلقصدهم الاستهزاء ولذلك قال الله تعالى كذلك كذب الذين من قبلهم حتى ذاقوا بأسنا فجعلهم مكذبين. وصرح آخر بأنه لو شاء لهداكم أجمعين. وقد يتمسك بقوله تعالى وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون وقوله تعالى كل ذلك كان سيئه عند ربك مكروها. ورد الأول بعد تسليم العموم بأن المعنى لأمرهم بالعبادة أو ليتذللوا أو ليكونوا عباداً لي. والثاني : بعد تسليم كون الإشارة إلى ما وقع بأن المعنى مكروهاً بين الناس وفي مجاري العادات.
As for the response to those who said, 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners, nor our forefathers' — it was for the purpose of mockery, and therefore Allah said, 'Thus did those before them deny until they tasted Our might' (6:148), so He made them deniers. And another [verse] explicitly states that if He had willed, He would have guided you all (6:149). And they may also cling to His saying, 'And I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' (51:56), and His saying, 'All of that — its evil is hateful in the sight of your Lord' (17:38). The first is refuted, after conceding its generality, by interpreting it as meaning 'I commanded them to worship' or 'to humble themselves' or 'to be servants to Me.' The second, after conceding that the reference is to what occurred, is interpreted as meaning 'hateful among people and in the course of customary practices.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection raised by polytheists: if Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners. The author points out that this objection was made in mockery (istihzā'), not as a genuine argument. Allah's response in verse 6:148 is to declare them deniers, like those before them. Another verse (6:149) states that if He willed, He would have guided all. Then the author considers two verses that might be misused: (51:56) 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me,' and (17:38) 'All of that is hateful in the sight of your Lord.' The author refutes them: the first, even if taken generally, means 'I commanded them to worship' or 'to be disposed to worship' — not that they necessarily do. The second means 'hateful' in the estimation of people and customary norms, not that Allah hates it absolutely. Thus, these verses do not contradict the generality of Allah's will.

Key Terms
استهزاء
مكذبين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The polytheist claim is a genuine logical dilemma about predestination and human responsibility.
Correction: It was a mockery, not a sincere question; the divine response exposes their insincerity rather than providing a direct logical answer.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the purpose behind the polytheist statement 'If Allah had willed, we would not have associated partners'?
A) A sincere theological doubt
B) Mockery
C) A request for guidance
D) Denial of prophecy

Key Takeaway
The polytheist objection is invalid because it was made in mockery, and the Quran exposes this rather than engaging with it as a sincere argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself using 'Allah willed it' to excuse a sin or failure, is your heart truly submitting or is there a trace of mockery? Think of a specific recent example and decide what sincere repentance would look like today.
?
The polytheists hid behind divine decree to avoid responsibility. In what area of your life do you most often use fate or circumstance to avoid making a difficult choice? Commit to one concrete step of action instead of attributing it to qadar.
?
If Allah were to expose the insincerity of your questions about His wisdom, what would He find? Take a moment to lay your doubts before Him honestly and ask for purification of your intentions.

Interpreting 'Except to Worship Me'
Refutation of misuse of verse 51:56.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' (51:56) is correctly interpreted to mean that Allah commanded worship or created humans with the capacity to worship, not that all actually worship.

Core Idea
The verse states the purpose of creation, not its actual outcome.

Some may misuse verse 51:56 to argue that all humans must worship Allah, but the proper interpretation shows it refers to the divine command or innate disposition. This lesson explains the refutation offered by the author.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the verse and the refutation. Notice that the author first concedes the generality of the verse.
The word 'تسليم العموم' means conceding its general application.
2
Understand the three interpretations
The author offers three ways to interpret the verse: (1) 'I commanded them to worship,' (2) 'to humble themselves,' (3) 'to be servants to Me.' All three avoid the conclusion that all jinn and humans actually worship.
These interpretations shift the meaning from actual worship to purpose or capacity.
3
Connect to the broader argument
This refutation preserves the generality of Allah's will by showing that the verse does not prove universal actual worship. It aligns with the principle that Allah's will is not contradicted by human disbelief.
This is a key point in the debate on predestination.

Source Text

A) I commanded them to worship
B) To humble themselves
C) To be servants to Me
D) To worship Me willingly

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the purpose of your existence is worshipping Allah, how will that change the way you approach your daily tasks? Will you now see every action as an act of servitude?
?
Have you ever used 'it is Allah's decree' to excuse your own negligence? The lesson shows that purpose and decree are distinct—how will you take responsibility for your choices while trusting His will?

Interpreting 'All of That is Hateful'
Refutation of misuse of verse 17:38.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse 'All of that — its evil is hateful in the sight of your Lord' (17:38) is interpreted as meaning hateful according to human custom and societal norms, not as an absolute divine decree of hatred.

Core Idea
'Hateful' in this verse is relative to human standards, not Allah's will.

Some may use verse 17:38 to claim that certain actions are absolutely hateful to Allah, but the author clarifies that the verse describes what people consider evil. This lesson explains the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage that presents the verse and the refutation. Notice that the author concedes the reference is to actual events.
The phrase 'بعد تسليم كون الإشارة إلى ما وقع' means after conceding that the verse refers to what happened.
2
Understand the interpretation
The author interprets 'makrūh' (hateful) as hateful among people and according to customary practices, not an absolute divine attribute. This avoids contradiction with the generality of Allah's will.
In other words, the verse describes human moral evaluation, not Allah's eternal decree.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
This interpretation maintains that all actions occur by Allah's will, but some are considered evil according to human norms. This is a key distinction in Islamic theology.
It resolves the apparent conflict between divine will and moral evil.

?

Refuting al-Allaf: Divine Compulsion and Human Agency
Taftazani refutes al-Allaf's interpretation of divine will as compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Allaf's claim that God creates faith in people without their choice, and why Taftazani refutes it by arguing that such a believer would be God, not the servant.

Core Idea
If God forces belief, the believer becomes identical to the Creator.

The Mu'tazila attempted to reconcile divine will with human responsibility by reinterpreting Quranic verses. Their first representative, al-Allaf, proposed that when God wills faith, He simply creates it in the person without any human participation. Taftazani exposes the absurdity of this view: if faith is created without any human act, then the believer would be indistinguishable from God, collapsing the distinction between Creator and creature.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Allaf's interpretation
Identify what al-Allaf claims: God creates faith and guidance in people without their choice. Notice how this reduces human agency to zero.
Al-Allaf's view is a form of extreme predestination.
2
Understand the refutation
Taftazani argues that if faith is created solely by God without any human act, then the believer would be God, not a servant. This follows from the Mu'tazili own principle that God is the sole creator.
The refutation uses the Mu'tazili's own logic against them.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is devastating: it shows al-Allaf's view leads to a logical absurdity, thereby protecting the clear distinction between God and His servants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمعتزلة فيها تأويلات فاسدة، وتعسفات باردة يتعجب منها الناظر، ويتحقق أنهم فيها محجوجون، وبوهقها مخنوقون ، ولظهور الحق في هذه المسألة يكاد عامتهم به يعترفون ويجري على ألسنتهم أن ما لم يشأ الله لا يكون. ثم العمدة القصوى لهم في الجواب عن أكثر الآيات حمل المشيئة على مشيئة القسر والإلجاء، وحين سئلوا معناها تحيروا . فقال العلاف (۱) معناها خلق الإيمان والهداية فيهم بلا اختيار عنهم . ورد بأن المؤمن حينئذ يكون هو الله لا العبد على ما زعمتم في إلزامنا (٢) حين قلنا بأن الخالق هو الله تبارك وتعالى وعز وجل مع قدرتنا واختيارنا وكسبنا، فكيف بدون ذلك ؟ فقال الجبائي : معناها خلق العلم الضروري بصحة الإيمان، وإقامة الدلائل المثبتة لذلك العلم الضروري. ورد بأن هذا لا يكون إيماناً والكلام فيه على أن في بعض الآيات دلالة على أنهم لو رأوا كل آية ودليل لا يؤمنون البتة فقال ابنه أبو هاشم معناها : أن خلق لهم العلم الضروري بأنهم لو لم (۳) يؤمنوا لعذبوا عذاباً شديداً وهذا أيضاً فاسد، (٤)، لأن كثيراً من الكفار كانوا يعلمون ذلك، وكذا إبليس ولا يؤمنون على أنه قال تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ولكن حق القول منى لأملأن جهنم من الجنة والناس أجمعين (٥). يشهد بفساد تأويلاتهم لدلالته على أنه إنما لم يهد الكل لسبق الحكم بملء جهنم ولاخفاء في أن الإيمان والهداية بطريق الجبر لا يخرجهم عن استحقاق جهنم سيما عند المعتزلة. وتمام تفصيل هذا المقام وتزييف تأويلاتهم في المطولات وكتب التأويلات
The Mu'tazilites have corrupt interpretations regarding it, and cold contortions at which the observer marvels, and he realizes that they are refuted in them, and strangled by their proof. And because of the appearance of truth in this matter, most of them almost admit it, and it runs on their tongues that what God does not will cannot be. Then their utmost reliance in answering most of the verses is to interpret 'will' as the will of compulsion and coercion, and when asked its meaning they became perplexed. Al-Allaf said: its meaning is creating faith and guidance in them without choice from them. It was refuted by saying that the believer at that time would be God, not the servant, based on what you claimed in your objection when we said that the Creator is God Almighty with our power, choice, and acquisition; how then without that? Al-Jubba'i said: its meaning is creating necessary knowledge of the soundness of faith and establishing proofs that confirm that necessary knowledge. It was refuted by saying that this would not be faith, and the discussion of it is that some verses indicate that even if they saw every sign and proof, they would never believe. Then his son Abu Hashim said: its meaning is that He creates for them the necessary knowledge that if they do not believe they will be severely punished, and this too is corrupt, because many of the disbelievers knew that, and likewise Iblis, and they do not believe, especially since He said: 'And if We willed, We could have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me will come true: I will fill Hell with jinn and humans all together' (Quran 32:13). This testifies to the corruption of their interpretations because it indicates that He did not guide all due to the prior decree to fill Hell, and there is no hiding that faith and guidance by way of compulsion do not remove them from deserving Hell, especially according to the Mu'tazilites. And the full detail of this topic and the refutation of their interpretations are in the lengthy works and books of interpretations.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazili interpretations of divine will. The author notes that the Mu'tazila have corrupt and cold contortions, but even most of them admit that nothing occurs without God's will. Their main strategy is to interpret verses about will as referring to the will of compulsion (qasr) and coercion (ilja'). Then three specific interpretations are presented: (1) Al-Allaf's view that God creates faith and guidance in them without choice; refuted by saying that then the believer would be God, not the servant. (2) Al-Jubba'i's view that God creates necessary knowledge of faith's soundness; refuted because this would not be true faith, and the Quran says they would not believe even with signs. (3) Abu Hashim's view that God creates necessary knowledge of punishment; refuted because many disbelievers knew this and still disbelieved. The Quranic verse (32:13) is cited to show that God did not guide all because He decreed to fill Hell. The passage concludes that these interpretations are false.

Key Terms
تأويلات
المشيئة
القسر
الإلجاء
العلاف
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Allaf's view that God creates faith in people without any human choice is a valid interpretation of divine will.
Correction: This view leads to the logical absurdity that the believer would be the creator of faith, i.e., God, not a servant. It contradicts the clear distinction between Creator and creature.

Checkpoint
What is al-Allaf's interpretation of divine will in the context of faith?
A) God creates faith in people without their choice.
B) God gives people the ability to choose faith.
C) God shows signs and leaves people free.
D) God forces people to disbelieve.

Key Takeaway
Al-Allaf's view that God creates faith without human choice collapses the distinction between God and servant, making it self-refuting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own faith, do you see it as a gift from God that you actively embraced, or as something that just happened to you? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for your belief?
?
If you believe that God's will determines everything, do you ever use that as an excuse to neglect your own efforts? What would change in your life if you truly lived out the balance this passage teaches?
?
Al-Allaf's view removes human choice from faith. In your own journey, where have you seen the danger of relying too much on divine decree while neglecting your own struggle? What step can you take today to actively strengthen your iman, knowing that both God's will and your effort matter?

Refuting al-Jubba'i: Necessary Knowledge Does Not Constitute Faith
Taftazani refutes al-Jubba'i's interpretation of divine will as creating necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's claim that divine will means creating necessary knowledge of faith, and why Taftazani refutes it by arguing that such compelled knowledge is not genuine faith.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge is not faith; true faith requires free choice.

Al-Jubba'i, another Mu'tazili scholar, proposed a more sophisticated interpretation: when God wills faith, He creates necessary knowledge of the truth of Islam and provides proofs that confirm it. Taftazani counters that this does not constitute faith, because faith is a voluntary act of assent, not a forced cognition. Moreover, the Quran states that even if disbelievers saw every sign, they would not believe, showing that knowledge alone is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubba'i's interpretation
Al-Jubba'i says: will means creating necessary knowledge of faith's soundness and establishing proofs. Notice that he tries to avoid compulsion by involving knowledge, but still it is forced.
Necessary knowledge is immediate and undeniable.
2
Understand the refutation
Taftazani says this is not faith because faith requires voluntary acceptance. He also cites Quranic evidence that disbelievers would not believe even after seeing every sign.
The refutation uses both logical and scriptural arguments.
3
Reflect on the difference
Consider why knowledge alone is not faith: Iblis knew God but disbelieved out of arrogance. Faith involves submission of the heart, not just intellectual assent.

Source Text

وللمعتزلة فيها تأويلات فاسدة، وتعسفات باردة يتعجب منها الناظر، ويتحقق أنهم فيها محجوجون، وبوهقها مخنوقون ، ولظهور الحق في هذه المسألة يكاد عامتهم به يعترفون ويجري على ألسنتهم أن ما لم يشأ الله لا يكون. ثم العمدة القصوى لهم في الجواب عن أكثر الآيات حمل المشيئة على مشيئة القسر والإلجاء، وحين سئلوا معناها تحيروا . فقال العلاف (۱) معناها خلق الإيمان والهداية فيهم بلا اختيار عنهم . ورد بأن المؤمن حينئذ يكون هو الله لا العبد على ما زعمتم في إلزامنا (٢) حين قلنا بأن الخالق هو الله تبارك وتعالى وعز وجل مع قدرتنا واختيارنا وكسبنا، فكيف بدون ذلك ؟ فقال الجبائي : معناها خلق العلم الضروري بصحة الإيمان، وإقامة الدلائل المثبتة لذلك العلم الضروري. ورد بأن هذا لا يكون إيماناً والكلام فيه على أن في بعض الآيات دلالة على أنهم لو رأوا كل آية ودليل لا يؤمنون البتة فقال ابنه أبو هاشم معناها : أن خلق لهم العلم الضروري بأنهم لو لم (۳) يؤمنوا لعذبوا عذاباً شديداً وهذا أيضاً فاسد، (٤)، لأن كثيراً من الكفار كانوا يعلمون ذلك، وكذا إبليس ولا يؤمنون على أنه قال تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ولكن حق القول منى لأملأن جهنم من الجنة والناس أجمعين (٥). يشهد بفساد تأويلاتهم لدلالته على أنه إنما لم يهد الكل لسبق الحكم بملء جهنم ولاخفاء في أن الإيمان والهداية بطريق الجبر لا يخرجهم عن استحقاق جهنم سيما عند المعتزلة. وتمام تفصيل هذا المقام وتزييف تأويلاتهم في المطولات وكتب التأويلات
The Mu'tazilites have corrupt interpretations regarding it, and cold contortions at which the observer marvels, and he realizes that they are refuted in them, and strangled by their proof. And because of the appearance of truth in this matter, most of them almost admit it, and it runs on their tongues that what God does not will cannot be. Then their utmost reliance in answering most of the verses is to interpret 'will' as the will of compulsion and coercion, and when asked its meaning they became perplexed. Al-Allaf said: its meaning is creating faith and guidance in them without choice from them. It was refuted by saying that the believer at that time would be God, not the servant, based on what you claimed in your objection when we said that the Creator is God Almighty with our power, choice, and acquisition; how then without that? Al-Jubba'i said: its meaning is creating necessary knowledge of the soundness of faith and establishing proofs that confirm that necessary knowledge. It was refuted by saying that this would not be faith, and the discussion of it is that some verses indicate that even if they saw every sign and proof, they would never believe. Then his son Abu Hashim said: its meaning is that He creates for them the necessary knowledge that if they do not believe they will be severely punished, and this too is corrupt, because many of the disbelievers knew that, and likewise Iblis, and they do not believe, especially since He said: 'And if We willed, We could have given every soul its guidance, but the word from Me will come true: I will fill Hell with jinn and humans all together' (Quran 32:13). This testifies to the corruption of their interpretations because it indicates that He did not guide all due to the prior decree to fill Hell, and there is no hiding that faith and guidance by way of compulsion do not remove them from deserving Hell, especially according to the Mu'tazilites. And the full detail of this topic and the refutation of their interpretations are in the lengthy works and books of interpretations.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Mu'tazili interpretations of divine will. The author notes that the Mu'tazila have corrupt and cold contortions, but even most of them admit that nothing occurs without God's will. Their main strategy is to interpret verses about will as referring to the will of compulsion (qasr) and coercion (ilja'). Then three specific interpretations are presented: (1) Al-Allaf's view that God creates faith and guidance in them without choice; refuted by saying that then the believer would be God, not the servant. (2) Al-Jubba'i's view that God creates necessary knowledge of faith's soundness; refuted because this would not be true faith, and the Quran says they would not believe even with signs. (3) Abu Hashim's view that God creates necessary knowledge of punishment; refuted because many disbelievers knew this and still disbelieved. The Quranic verse (32:13) is cited to show that God did not guide all because He decreed to fill Hell. The passage concludes that these interpretations are false.

Key Terms
الجبائي
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Jubba'i's view that God creating necessary knowledge of faith is equivalent to giving faith is correct.
Correction: Necessary knowledge is not faith; it is involuntary, while faith requires voluntary submission. The Quran confirms that knowledge alone does not guarantee belief.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani reject al-Jubba'i's interpretation that divine will means creating necessary knowledge of faith?
A) Because necessary knowledge is not faith.
B) Because necessary knowledge cannot be created.
C) Because it contradicts human free will entirely.
D) Because al-Jubba'i was not a reliable scholar.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's interpretation fails because necessary knowledge is not the same as voluntary faith, and the Quran shows that knowledge alone does not compel belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'If only I saw a clear miracle, I would have perfect faith'? This lesson reveals that even the greatest signs do not compel belief. What does that reveal about the hidden resistance in your own heart? Today, will you choose to respond to the signs already present in your life, or will you wait for a proof you think would force you—but which this passage shows never does?
?
Al-Jubba'i assumed that necessary knowledge would be enough for faith. But you know many true things about Allah that you do not act on. Where in your life are you treating your knowledge as an excuse for inaction? What is one truth you claim to believe but have not submitted to, and what concrete step will you take before the day ends to make that belief active faith?
?
The Quran says that some people would not believe even if they saw every sign. Is there an area of your life where you are stubbornly refusing to accept a truth that is already clear? Perhaps about your prayer, your character, or a relationship. What would it take for you to let go of that resistance and submit? Describe one specific change you will make now, not hypothetically.

Refuting Abu Hashim: The Futility of Threat-Based Compulsion
Taftazani refutes Abu Hashim's interpretation of divine will as creating knowledge of punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's claim that divine will creates necessary knowledge of punishment for disbelief, and why Taftazani refutes it by showing that even disbelievers like Iblis knew of punishment yet persisted in disbelief.

Core Idea
Threatening knowledge does not force faith; even Iblis knew he would be punished.

Abu Hashim, the son of al-Jubba'i, proposed a third interpretation: when God wills faith, He creates the necessary knowledge that if they do not believe, they will be severely punished. Taftazani refutes this by noting that many disbelievers, including Iblis, had such knowledge and still disbelieved. Furthermore, the Quranic verse (32:13) explicitly states that God did not guide all because He decreed to fill Hell, indicating that the withholding of guidance is due to a prior decree, not a failure of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's interpretation
Abu Hashim says God creates necessary knowledge of the punishment for disbelief. This knowledge is meant to compel belief through fear.
This is a psychological compulsion rather than physical.
2
Understand the refutation
Taftazani points out that many disbelievers, including Iblis, knew they would be punished yet did not believe. So the knowledge does not force faith.
The refutation uses examples from the Quran.
3
Consider the Quranic verse
Verse 32:13 says God did not guide all because He decreed to fill Hell. This shows that the absence of guidance is due to divine decree, not lack of knowledge.
This verse directly undermines all Mu'tazili interpretations.

First Mu'tazili Argument: God Cannot Will Evil
Taftazani presents and refutes the first Mu'tazili argument against God willing evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument against God willing evil and Taftazani's refutation based on divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Nothing is evil from God; we simply do not see the goodness.

Taftazani begins his refutation of the Mu'tazila by presenting their first argument. They claim that willing evil is evil, so God, being free from all evil, cannot will evil. But Taftazani quickly dismantles this by asserting that nothing from God is truly evil — any apparent evil is due to our limited understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument and refutation.
Notice the concise style of Taftazani.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili premise
The Mu'tazila claim that willing evil is evil, so God cannot will evil.
3
Understand the refutation
Taftazani says nothing from God is evil; we just don't see the goodness.
This is a key Ash'ari principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة تمسكوا في دعواهم بوجوه : الأول : أن إرادة القبيح قبيحة، والله منزه عن القبائح. ورد بأنه لا قبيح منه غاية الأمر أنه يخفي علينا وجه حسنه
And the Mu'tazilites clung to their claim by (the following) arguments: First: that willing evil is evil, and God is free from evils. It was refuted by saying that nothing is evil from Him; the most that can be said is that He hides from us the aspect of its goodness.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five arguments by the Mu'tazila to deny that God wills evil. The argument is that willing evil is itself evil, and since God is free from all evil, He cannot will evil. The refutation is that nothing that God does is evil; the attribution of 'evil' is based on our limited knowledge. God may hide the aspect of goodness in His actions from us, but that does not mean the action is inherently evil. Thus, the premise that willing evil is evil does not apply to God.

Key Terms
إرادة القبيح
قبيح
منزه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God wills something that appears evil, He must be evil.
Correction: The author explains that God's actions are not subject to human moral standards; nothing from God is truly evil.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani refute the claim that God cannot will evil?
A) He says that nothing is evil from God; we are just ignorant of the wisdom.
B) He says that God does will evil, but it is not actually evil.
C) He says the argument is based on a false premise.
D) He says the Mu'tazila are wrong because they agree with the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazili argument by asserting that nothing from God is evil; any apparent evil is due to our ignorance of its underlying goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels unjust or harmful, do you instinctively blame God? How would your heart respond if you truly believed that nothing from Him is evil, and the goodness is simply hidden from you right now? What one step can you take this week to train yourself to look for that hidden goodness?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that God's actions fit their sense of justice. Are there areas in your life where you are secretly holding God to your own standards? What would it cost you to surrender that judgment and say, 'You are good, even when I don't see it'? Will you do it today?
?
If you really accepted that every difficulty in your life has a hidden aspect of goodness from God, what would change about how you face your current biggest trial? What specific complaint about your life are you willing to let go of right now, as an act of trust in God's hidden wisdom?

God's Absolute Ownership as a Refutation of Injustice
Taftazani presents and refutes the second Mu'tazili argument about punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazili argument that punishing what God wills is unjust, and Taftazani's refutation based on God's absolute ownership.

Core Idea
God's absolute ownership renders His actions beyond accusations of injustice.

The Mu'tazila argue that if God punishes something He Himself willed, that would be unjust. Taftazani counters by invoking God's absolute sovereignty: as the owner of all creation, He may dispose of His property as He wishes. This rebuttal hinges on the concept of divine ownership, which undercuts the very notion of injustice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how concise the argument and refutation are.
This is a single sentence containing both the objection and the response.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili claim
Identify the claim: punishing what God wills would be unjust. Ask yourself why the Mu'tazila think this is a problem.
3
Grasp the refutation
Taftazani refutes by 'denial' (al-man‘), arguing that God's disposal of His own property cannot be unjust. Understand the logic: an owner has full rights over what they own.
The key term is 'taṣarruf fī milkihi' (disposal of His property).
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation upholds divine sovereignty. Does it fully answer the Mu'tazili concern? Reflect on the broader debate about divine justice.
This connects to the larger topic of qadar and divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن العقاب على ما أراده ظلم ورد بالمنع فإنه تصرف في ملكه
Second: that punishing what He wills is injustice. It was refuted by denial, for it is disposal of His property.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This is the second argument: if God wills something and then punishes it, that would be unjust because punishment is deserved for what He does not will. The response is a simple denial: it is not unjust because God is the absolute owner of all creation, and He can dispose of His property as He wishes. There is no injustice when an owner does with His property as He wills.

Key Terms
عقاب
ظلم
منع
تصرف في ملكه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that punishing someone for an action God willed is morally problematic because it seems like punishing an innocent agent.
Correction: The refutation asserts that the concept of injustice does not apply to God, as He is the absolute owner of all creation. An owner can do as they please with their property, so there is no injustice.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the claim that punishing what God wills would be unjust?
A) Punishment is justice, not injustice.
B) God is the owner of all and can dispose as He wishes.
C) God does not punish what He wills; He punishes only what He does not will.
D) The Mu'tazila misunderstand the nature of justice.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership means His actions, including punishment of what He wills, cannot be considered unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship, do you ever accuse God of being unfair? How does the truth that He is the absolute Owner of all things challenge that thought? Are you willing to surrender your sense of entitlement to His sovereignty?
?
Reflect on a time you felt that God's decree was unjust. Now consider Taftazani's simple refutation: 'It is disposal of His property.' Does that change your perspective? What would it cost you to fully accept that God owes you nothing?
?
If God's punishment cannot be unjust because He owns all, how does that affect your view of your own sins? Do you truly believe that any punishment He gives is right, even if you don't understand it? Can you say from your heart, 'You are the Owner, I am the owned'?

Third Mu'tazili Argument: Command Without Will Is Not Foolish
Taftazani presents and refutes the third Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that commanding what is not willed is foolish and Taftazani's refutation using real-world examples.

Core Idea
Commands do not always imply the commander's will; they may serve other purposes.

The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot command something He does not will, as that would be foolish. Taftazani counters by showing real-world cases where commands are issued without the commander intending the commanded action. This lesson examines that argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp the overall argument and examples.
Notice the examples of the master and the coerced person.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili argument
Identify the key claim: commanding what is not willed is foolish (safah). Understand why they consider it problematic for God.
This sets up the need for a refutation.
3
Understand Taftazani's denial and examples
Taftazani refutes by denial (man‘) and gives examples showing commands can have purposes other than execution.
The master’s test and the coerced person demonstrate this.
4
Reflect on the sultan objection and response
The objection that subjects act because of the sultan's will is answered: they act on the command itself, not necessarily on will.
This reinforces that commands are independent indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن الأمر بما لا يراد والنهي عما يراد سفه ورد بالمنع إذ ربما لا يكون غرض الآمر الإتيان بالمأمور .. كالسيد إذا أمر العبد امتحاناً له هل يطيعه أم لا؟ فإنه لا يريد شيئاً من الطاعة والعصيان، أو اعتذاراً عن ضربه بأنه لا يطيعه فإنه يريد منه العصيان، وكالمكره على الأمر بنهب أمواله، وكذا النهي. فإن قيل : مأمور السلطان يبادر إلى المأمور به معللاً بأنه مراد السلطان قلنا : لا مطلقاً. بل إذا ظهر أمارة الإرادة ، وإنما يعلل مطلقاً بالأمر والإشارة والحكم.
Third: that commanding what is not willed and forbidding what is willed is foolishness. It was refuted by denial, for sometimes the commander does not intend the commanded action to be performed, like when a master commands his servant to test him whether he will obey him or not, for he does not will any obedience or disobedience; or as an excuse to hit him if he disobeys, for he wills disobedience from him; and like one who is compelled to command the plundering of his wealth, and similarly for forbidding. If it is said: The subject of the sultan hastens to the commanded action because it is the sultan's will, we say: not absolutely. Rather, (only) when a sign of will appears; and it is only explained absolutely by the command, the sign, and the judgment.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument: commanding what is not willed is foolish, so God cannot command faith while not willing it. The response is a denial, illustrated with examples. A master may command his servant to test obedience, without willing any particular outcome. Similarly, one may order the plunder of his own property under duress. The objection about the sultan's command is also addressed: people act on the command, not necessarily on the knowledge of will. Thus, there is no foolishness in commanding without willing.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
سفه
امتحان
إكراه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A command always indicates that the commander wills the commanded action.
Correction: Commands can be issued for other reasons, such as testing or coercion, without the commander willing the act itself.

Checkpoint
Which example does Taftazani use to demonstrate that a command can be issued without the commander willing the commanded action?
A) A master commanding his servant to test him.
B) A king commanding his army to war.
C) A teacher commanding a student to study.
D) A father commanding his son to work.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili argument fails because commands can serve purposes other than actual compliance, such as testing or coercion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah's commands, do you assume He wills the specific outcome for you? How does this lesson change your understanding of why He commands even when He knows you will struggle? What does it mean for your sincerity?
?
Think of a time you commanded someone but did not will their compliance. What was your true purpose? How does that relate to Allah's commands to humanity? Are you comfortable with the idea that Allah may command something He does not will for you individually? How should that affect your relationship with His commands?
?
If you were tested by a command you found impossible to fulfill, would you still obey because of the command itself, or only if you felt God willed it for you? What does your answer reveal about your trust in His wisdom beyond your understanding?

Disbelief as Obedience: Refuting the Fourth Argument
Taftazani presents and refutes the fourth Mu'tazili argument about obedience and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that if disbelief is willed by God it would be obedience, and why Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between divine will and divine command.

Core Idea
Obedience is following God's command, not fulfilling God's will.

The fourth Mu'tazili argument attempts to force a contradiction: if disbelief is willed by God, then it must be obedience. This would make disbelief an act of obedience, which is absurd. Taftazani's refutation draws a crucial distinction between divine will and divine command. Read the passage to see how this distinction resolves the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then read the translation. Notice the structure: the claim followed by the refutation.
The argument is very concise.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'طاعة' (obedience), 'مراد' (willed), and 'موافقة الأمر' (compliance with command). Understand how the author redefines obedience.
The refutation hinges on these definitions.
3
Analyze the refutation
Why does the refutation succeed? Because it denies the definitional premise. Practice explaining the difference between acting according to God's will and acting according to God's command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الكفر مراداً الله تعالى لكان طاعة لأن معناها تحصيل مراد المطاع لدورانه معه وجوداً وعدماً. ورد بالمنع بل هي موافقة الأمر وإنما تدور معه علمت الإرادة أو لم تعلم .
Fourth: If disbelief were willed by God Almighty, it would be obedience, because its meaning is the attainment of the will of the one obeyed, as it revolves with it in existence and non-existence. It was refuted by denial; rather, it is compliance with the command, and it revolves with it whether the will is known or not.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
The fourth argument claims that if God wills disbelief, then disbelief would be obedience, since obedience is defined as doing what the obeyed wills. The response is a denial of that definition. Obedience is not about fulfilling the will but about complying with the command. One can obey a command without knowing the will behind it. Thus, even if God wills disbelief, committing disbelief would not be obedience because it contradicts His command.

Key Terms
طاعة
مراد
موافقة الأمر
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that obedience means doing whatever God wills, so if God wills something, doing it is obedience.
Correction: Obedience is defined by compliance with divine command, not by alignment with divine will. God may will something but command its opposite, so obedience is acting on the command.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the fourth argument, what is obedience based on?
A) Compliance with God's will.
B) Compliance with God's command.
C) Attaining God's pleasure.
D) Avoiding God's wrath.

Key Takeaway
Obedience is defined by compliance with divine command, not by alignment with divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself committing a sin, do you ever excuse it by thinking 'it must have been Allah's will'? How does this lesson challenge that excuse and call you to take full responsibility for your actions?
?
Obedience to Allah is defined as compliance with His command, not as accepting whatever happens. Is your life marked more by actively obeying what Allah commanded, or by passively accepting whatever fate brings? Which one will you prioritize starting today?
?
If you truly believe that disbelief is willed by Allah yet still a sin, how does that shape your view of other people's disbelief? Does it lead you to humility and compassion, or to judgment? What change does this lesson demand from your heart?
