Fifth Mu'tazili Argument: Decree and Pleasure Refuted
Taftazani presents and refutes the fifth argument about decree and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that God's will necessitates a decree requiring pleasure, and the Ash'ari refutation distinguishing between the decree (qada') and the decreed thing (maqdi).

Core Idea
Obligation to be pleased applies to God's decree, not to the decreed events.

This lesson presents the fifth Mu'tazili argument against determinism: if God wills disbelief, it becomes His decree, and we must be pleased with it, yet we cannot be pleased with disbelief. Taftazani refutes this by distinguishing between God's attribute of decree (qada') and the decreed events (maqdi). The key is that we are obliged to be pleased only with the decree itself, not with every particular event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argumentative flow come through.
Notice the compressed style; the argument and refutation are tightly packed.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili argument
The argument states: if disbelief were willed, it would be a decree, so it would be obligatory to be pleased with it. Since we cannot be pleased with disbelief, the premise (that it is willed) is false.
The argument uses a conditional with an impossible consequent.
3
Understand Taftazani's refutation
Taftazani replies that the obligation to be pleased applies only to the decree (qada') as God's attribute, not to the decreed thing (maqdi) which is the event. Disbelief is a decreed thing, not the decree itself.
He also rejects the Mu'tazili redefinition of qada' as events rather than God's creative act.
4
Consider the additional answer
An alternative response is that being pleased with disbelief insofar as it is from God's decree is obedience, but insofar as it is disbelief is kufr. However, Taftazani notes this answer is questionable.
This introduces a dichotomy of aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لو كان مراداً لكان قضاء فوجب الرضاء به والملازمة وبطلات اللازم ورد بأنه مقضى لاقضاء، ووجوب الرضا إنما هو بالقضاء دون المقضى ، ودعوى أن المراد بالقضاء الواجب الرضا به هو المقضى من المحن والبلايا والمصائب والرزايا لا الصفة الذاتية لله تعالى بهت بل هو الخلق والحكم والتقدير. وقد يجاب بأن الرضا بالكفر من حيث انه من قضاء الله تعالى طاعة ، ولا من هذه الحيثية كفر وفيه نظر .
Fifth: If (disbelief) were willed, it would be a decree, and thus it would be obligatory to be pleased with it, and the implication and the falsity of the necessary consequent. It was refuted by saying that it is a decreed thing, not a decree, and the obligation of pleasure is only with the decree, not the decreed thing. And the claim that what is meant by 'decree' that requires pleasure is the decreed things of trials, calamities, afflictions, and misfortunes, not the essential attribute of God Almighty, is false; rather, (qada') is creation, judgment, and predetermination. And it may be answered that being pleased with disbelief in so far as it is from God's decree is obedience, and in so far as it is disbelief it is not, and there is something to consider in this.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
The fifth argument states that if God wills disbelief, it becomes His decree, and we must be pleased with it, but we cannot be pleased with disbelief. The refutation distinguishes between the decree (qada') as God's attribute and the decreed thing (maqdi) as the event. The obligation to be pleased applies only to the decree itself, not to the particular events. The claim that 'qada' in the verse means events is false; qada' is God's act of creation and judgment. An additional answer is that being pleased with disbelief as a divine act is obedience, while being pleased with it as disbelief is kufr, but the author notes that this answer is questionable.

Key Terms
قضاء
مقضى
الرضا
محن
بلايا
خلق
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must be pleased with everything that God wills, including acts of disbelief, because God's will necessitates decree.
Correction: The obligation to be pleased applies only to God's decree (the attribute), not to the specific decreed events. Disbelief as an event is not something we must be pleased with.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani distinguish between qada' and maqdi in refuting the fifth argument?
A) Qada' is God's attribute; maqdi is the created event.
B) Both are the same.
C) Qada' is the event; maqdi is the decree.
D) He does not distinguish.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to be pleased applies only to God's decree, not to the specific events that are decreed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent trial you faced. Did your heart resent the event itself, or did it acknowledge God's wisdom in decreeing it? How can you shift your focus from the maqdi to the qada'?
?
If you see a person committing shirk, can you simultaneously be pleased that God's decree is just and be saddened by their action? What does that look like in your attitude toward them?
?
Are there any calamities in your life that you still hold a grudge against God (even subconsciously)? Will you today repent of that and commit to being pleased with His decree, while still feeling the pain of the situation?

The Seventh Objection: The Verse of Creation for Worship
Introduction of the seventh objection and initial refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection against the Ash'ari position and the initial refutations based on generality and the true purpose of the verse.

Core Idea
The verse states the purpose of creation, not its universal fulfillment.

The Mu'tazila often cite Quran 51:56 to argue that Allah intends obedience from every person. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the early part of the refutation: the verse does not claim that the purpose is always realized, and its real intent is to show Allah's self-sufficiency and human need.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Start by reading the seventh objection as quoted from the text. Notice how the objector uses the 'lam al-gharad' (lam of purpose) to argue that Allah intended worship from all.
The verse is Quran 51:56, a key proof text in the debate.
2
Understand the first refutation: denying generality
The author concedes that the lam indicates purpose, but then denies that the verse is general. He points out exceptions like children and the insane. Reflect on why this undermines the objection.
This is a logical move: if the verse is not universal, it cannot prove that Allah wills obedience from every individual.
3
Explore the self-sufficiency interpretation
The author then argues that the verse's real purpose is to state Allah's independence, not to assert that everyone worships. Read the supporting verse (Quran 51:57) and see how it shifts the meaning.
This reframes the entire discussion from obligation to divine independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : قوله تعالى وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون) (۱) دل على أنه أراد من الكل الطاعة والعبادة لا المعصية . ورد بعد تسليم دلالة لام الفرض على كون ما بعدها، مراداً بمنع العموم للقطع بخروج من مات على الصبا أو الجنون، فليخرج من مات على الكفر. عنهم ولو سلم فليس المقصود بيان خلقهم لهذا الغرض بل بيان استغنائه وافتقارهم إليه بدليل قوله تعالى ما أريد منهم من رزق وما أريد أن يطعمون ) (۲) فكأنه قال وما خلقتهم لينفعوني بل لأمرهم بالعبادة أو ليتذللوا إلى أما بالنسبة إلى المطيع فظاهر وأما بالنسبة إلى العاصي فبشهادة الفطرة على تذلله، وإن تخرص وافترى كذا في الارشاد لإمام الحرمين . وذهب كثير من أهل التأويل إلى أن المعنى ليكونوا عباداً لي فتكون الآية على عمومها على أنها يعارضها قوله تعالى ولقد ذرأنا لجهنم كثيراً من الجن والإنس ) (۳) وقوله تعالى إنما نملي لهم ليزدادوا إثماً ) (٤) وجعل اللام للعاقبة كما في قوله تعالى فالتقطه آل فرعون ليكون لهم عدواً وحزناً ) (٥) إنما يصح في فعل من يجهل العواقب فيفعل لغرض فلا يحصل ذلك بل ضده فيجعل كأنه فعل الفاعل لهذا الغرض الفاسد تنبيهاً على أخطائه، وكيف يتصور في علام الغيوب أن يفعل فعلاً لغرض يعلم قطعاً أنه لا يحصل البتة بل يحصل ضده. والعجب من المعتزلة كيف لا يعدون ذلك سفهاً وعبثاً .؟
The seventh: His statement, the Exalted, 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Quran 51:56) indicates that He intended from all obedience and worship, not disobedience. It is refuted after conceding that the 'lam of purpose' indicates that what follows is intended, by denying generality because it is certain that one who dies in childhood or insanity is excluded, so let one who dies in disbelief be excluded. And if it is conceded, then the intended meaning is not to state that He created them for this purpose, but rather to state His self-sufficiency and their need for Him, as evidence by His statement, the Exalted, 'I do not want from them any provision, nor do I want them to feed Me' (Quran 51:57). So it is as if He said, 'I did not create them to benefit Me, but rather to command them to worship or to humble themselves before Me.' As for the obedient, it is apparent; as for the disobedient, the innate disposition testifies to his humility, even if he fabricates and lies. This is from al-Irshad by Imām al-Haramayn. Many of the people of interpretation went to the meaning that they should be servants to Me, so the verse is general, but it is contradicted by His statement, the Exalted, 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (Quran 7:179) and His statement, the Exalted, 'We only give them respite so that they may increase in sin' (Quran 3:178). Making the 'lam' for consequence, as in His statement, the Exalted, 'So the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be an enemy and a sorrow for them' (Quran 28:8), is only valid in the action of one ignorant of consequences who acts for a purpose and that purpose does not occur but rather its opposite, so it is made as if the agent acted for that faulty purpose by way of alerting to his mistakes. How can it be imagined of the Knower of the Unseen that He does an act for a purpose which He knows for certain will not occur at all, but rather its opposite will occur? And amazing is it of the Mu'tazila how they do not consider that foolishness and absurdity.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection against the Ash'ari position that God does not will disobedience. The objector cites the verse 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me' to argue that God intended obedience from all, not disobedience. The author provides multiple refutations. First, even if the 'lam' indicates purpose, it does not imply that the purpose is actually realized in every individual; the verse only states the purpose of creation, not that it always occurs. Second, the verse is not general because it excludes those who die in childhood or insanity. Third, the true intent of the verse is to show God's independence and human need, not to state that everyone will worship. Fourth, an alternative interpretation by many scholars holds that the meaning is 'to be servants', making the verse general, but this is contradicted by other verses clearly stating that many are destined for Hell. The author then refutes the attempt to reconcile these verses by taking the 'lam' as indicating consequence rather than purpose, arguing that this only applies to beings ignorant of outcomes, not to God, who knows that His purpose will not be achieved. The author ends by expressing amazement at the Mu'tazila for not recognizing the absurdity of their position.

Key Terms
لام الفرض
العموم
المشيئة
العبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that God wills obedience from every individual without exception.
Correction: The verse only states the purpose of creation, not its universal fulfillment, and it is not general because of exceptions like children and the insane.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument the author uses to refute the seventh objection?
A) The verse is abrogated by other verses.
B) The 'lam' indicates purpose but the verse is not general; exceptions exist.
C) The verse only applies to Muslims.
D) The verse refers to the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'except to worship Me' states the purpose of creation, not a guarantee that all will worship, and its true intent is to highlight God's self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me,' do you assume that your existence guarantees that you worship properly? How does the lesson's deeper interpretation—that the verse highlights God's self-sufficiency, not your worship—expose any complacency you may have about your actual devotion? Will you examine today whether your worship is truly humble recognition or mere routine?
?
The passage says that even the disobedient person shows humility by their innate disposition, though they fabricate and lie. In what areas of your life are you fabricating reasons for your independence from God while your very existence and needs testify to your dependence? What one concrete admission of need will you make to God today?
?
If the purpose of your creation is to worship, but God does not need your worship, why does He still command it? Does your heart seek to worship out of love and humility, or are you merely checking a duty? What would it mean for you to truly live as one who acknowledges God's self-sufficiency and your own poverty before Him?

The Deeper Refutation: The 'Lam' of Consequence and the Absurdity of the Mu'tazila Position
Refutation of the alternative interpretation and 'lam of consequence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper refutation concerning the 'lam of consequence' and why the Mu'tazila position leads to absurdity when applied to God.

Core Idea
Using the 'lam' for consequence implies ignorance, which is impossible for God.

Some interpreters try to reconcile the verse with predestination by taking the 'lam' as indicating consequence rather than purpose. In this lesson, we explore why this move fails: it only works for agents unaware of outcomes. Since God knows the future, acting for a purpose that will not be realized would be futile. The author concludes by expressing amazement at the Mu'tazila for not seeing this absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Review the alternative interpretation
Read the part where many scholars interpret 'to worship Me' as 'to be servants', making the verse general. Then see how other verses (7:179, 3:178) contradict this general reading.
The contradiction shows that the verse cannot be understood as universally applicable.
2
Examine the 'lam of consequence' proposal
Some try to resolve the contradiction by claiming the 'lam' indicates consequence, not purpose, like in Quran 28:8. Understand the example.
This move attempts to preserve God's will while acknowledging that many do not worship.
3
See why it fails: God knows the outcome
The author argues that the 'lam of consequence' is only valid when the agent is ignorant of the outcome. Since God knows everything, He cannot act for a purpose He knows will not happen. This would be absurd.
This refutes the reconciliation attempt completely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does the author reject the interpretation that the 'lam' in 'to worship Me' indicates consequence?

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials that seem pointless, do you ever catch yourself doubting that God has a real purpose? How does this lesson's insistence that God never acts for a purpose He knows will not occur change the way you should view your struggles?
?
The author marvels that the Mu'tazila do not see their own foolishness. Where in your own life have you been stubbornly holding onto a belief or interpretation about God that, upon honest reflection, contradicts His perfect knowledge? What would repentance look like in that area?
?
If God's actions are never absurd or futile, then what does it mean about the times you feel your worship or efforts are going nowhere? Are you willing to trust that He is accomplishing something, even when you cannot see it, and to serve Him without demanding visible results?

Refuting the Eighth Objection: The Verse of Disliked Things
The author presents the eighth objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that prohibitions cannot be willed by Allah because they are called 'disliked' in the Quran.

Core Idea
The verse's 'disliked' refers to human custom, not Allah's own dislike.

The eighth objection uses the phrase 'disliked by your Lord' from Quran 17:38 to argue that prohibitions are not willed by Allah. The author provides a crisp refutation, showing that the term refers to human perspective and that metaphorical interpretation is unnecessary. Let's engage with this passage to see how the author dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection followed by refutation.
The objection is based on the apparent contradiction between will and dislike.
2
Identify the two refutation strategies
The author gives two refutations. First, he concedes the verse refers to prohibitions but reinterprets 'disliked' as human custom. Second, he dismisses the metaphor approach as frivolous.
The first refutation uses a 'even if' concession.
3
Reflect on the 'frivolous speech' critique
Consider why the author considers the metaphor interpretation unnecessary. The phrase already indicates prohibition, so calling it a metaphor adds nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن: قوله تعالى: كل ذلك كان سيئه عند ربك مَكْرُوهًا (۱) جعل المنهيات مكروهة فلا تكون مرادة، لأن الإرادة والكراهة ضدان، ورد بعد تسليم كونه إشارة إلى المنهيات الواقعة ليلزم كونها مرادة بأن المعنى أنها مكروهة عند الناس وفي مجال العادات لا عند الله فيلزم المحال . وأما جعل المكروه مجازاً عن المنهي فلغو من الكلام لكون ذلك إشارة إلى المنهي .
The eighth: His statement, the Exalted, 'All of that – its evil is disliked by your Lord' (Quran 17:38) made the prohibitions disliked, so they are not willed, because will and dislike are opposites. It is refuted after conceding that it is an indication of actual prohibitions so that they are necessarily willed, by [saying] that the meaning is that they are disliked by people and in the domain of customs, not by Allah, so that absurdity follows. As for making 'disliked' a metaphor for 'prohibited', it is frivolous speech since that [i.e., the phrase] is an indication of the prohibition.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection: the verse 'All of that – its evil is disliked by your Lord' lists prohibitions and calls them 'disliked' (makrūh), so they cannot be willed by Allah because will and dislike are opposites. The author provides two refutations. First, even if we concede that the verse refers to actual prohibitions (so that they are willed by Allah in reality), the meaning of 'disliked' here is that they are considered disliked by people and according to customary norms, not that Allah Himself dislikes them. If it were otherwise, an absurdity would follow (i.e., that Allah wills what He dislikes). Second, the attempt to interpret 'disliked' metaphorically as 'prohibited' is futile because the phrase already indicates prohibition directly, making the metaphor redundant.

Key Terms
المنهيات
المكروه
الإرادة
الكراهة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'makruh' in the verse means that Allah actually dislikes these actions.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'makruh' refers to what is disliked by people and according to custom, not Allah's own dislike.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the eighth objection based on the verse 'كل ذلك كان سيئه عند ربك مَكْرُوهًا'?
A) By arguing that 'makruh' means 'prohibited' literally
B) By saying that the verse is about what is disliked by people, not by Allah
C) By claiming the verse is abrogated
D) By denying that the verse refers to prohibitions

Key Takeaway
The verse's description of prohibitions as 'disliked' does not imply that Allah dislikes them; it refers to human custom and perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'All of that – its evil is disliked by your Lord', what was your immediate reaction? Did you assume that Allah feels dislike the way you do? How does separating divine attributes from human emotions change your understanding of Allah's will?
?
This lesson teaches that some things we call 'disliked' are only according to human custom. Is there a sin or action you personally dislike so much that you project that disgust onto Allah? How can you ensure your feelings do not distort your perception of His commands?
?
The author calls the metaphorical interpretation 'frivolous speech.' Are there times when you have rationalized away the plain meaning of Quranic verses to avoid a challenging truth? What would it cost you to accept the literal meaning as it stands?

Defining the Disputed Matter in Taklif al-Muhal
The author defines the disputed matter and lists three types of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the disputed matter is in the obligation of the impossible and the three initial types of impossibility distinguished by the scholars.

Core Idea
The core dispute is about permissibility, not occurrence, and concerns what is possible but not under power.

The author begins by clarifying exactly what is being disputed when scholars discuss the obligation of the impossible. He distinguishes between different types of impossibility: what is possible in itself but not subject to the slave's power, what is impossible by itself, and what is impossible due to divine foreknowledge. This lesson lays the groundwork for the more detailed three-level classification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the disputed matter
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the author sets the boundaries of the dispute.
The disputed matter is specified as what is possible in itself but not under the slave's power.
2
Identify the three types of impossibility
The passage mentions three types: what is impossible by itself, what is impossible by prior knowledge, and what is possible but not under power. Find each one.
The first type (impossible by itself) is excluded from the dispute.
3
Understand the majority view and the clarification
Note that the majority hold the first two types as impossible to obligate, but the third type (prior knowledge) is agreed upon. Also, the dispute is about permissibility (jawāz), not occurrence.
The verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not about this disputed matter.
4
Consider the example of Abu Lahab
The author presents an example from some verifiers who permit obligating what is impossible by itself, using Abu Lahab's case. Reflect on the response given.
The response is considered weak by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم المتنازع) ( في الأول ما أمكن في نفسه ولم يقع متعلقاً لقدرة العبد أصلاً كخلق الأجسام أو عادة كالصعود إلى السماء لا ما امتنع لذاته كجمع النقيضين. فإن الجمهور على امتناع التكليف به بناء على أنه يستدعي تصور المكلف به واقعاً، والمستحيل لا يتصور إلا على سبيل التشبيه والنفي ولا ما امتنع لسابق علم أو إخبار من الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه، فإن التكليف به واقع وفاقاً . ثم النزاع في الجواز، وإلا فالوقوع منفي بحكم النص والاستقراء، وفي التكليف بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل والإتيان به واستحقاق العذاب على الترك، وإلا فعل قصد التعجيز واقع وفاقاً، وبهذا يظهر أن تمسك المانعين بمثل ( لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها (١) ليس على المتنازع، وكذا تمسك المجوزين بمثل فأتوا بسورة ) (۲) وبأن فعل العبد ليس بقدرته وبأن التكليف قبل الفعل والقدرة معه وبأن من علم الله أنه لا يؤمن مكلف بالإيمان مع استحالته منه لاستحالة الجهل على الله تعالى. وفي كلام كثير من المحققين التكليف بالممتنع لذاته كجمع النقيضين جائز بل واقع . لأن مثل أبي لهب (۳) مكلف بأن يصدق في جميع ما جاء به، ومن جملته أنه لا يصدقه أصلاً، فقد كلف بأن يصدقه فى أنه لا يصدقه، وهو جمع للنقيضين . والجواب بأن المكلف به ليس إلا تحصيل الإيمان وهو ممكن في نفسه ممتنع لسابق العلم والإخبار، أو بأنه إنما كلف التصديق بما عدا هذا الإخبار ضعيف. يشير إلى تحرير محل النزاع على ما هو رأي المحققين من أصحابنا، فإنه حكى عن بعضهم تجويز تكليف المحال حتى الممتنع لذاته، كجعل القديم محدثاً وبالعكس، وعن بعضهم أن تكليف ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه أو أراد ذلك أو أخبر به كلها تكليف ما لا يطاق . فنقول مراتب ما لا يطاق ثلاثة أدناها ما يمتنع بعلم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه، أو لإرادته ذلك أو لإخباره بذلك ، ولا نزاع في وقوع التكليف به فضلاً عن الجواز فإن من مات على كفره، ومن أخبر الله تعالى بعدم إيمانه يعد عاصياً إجماعاً وأقصاها ما يمتنع لذاته كقلب الحقائق ، وجمع الضدين أو النقيضين. وفي جواز التكليف به تردد بناء على أنه يستدعي تصور المكلف به ،واقعاً والممتنع هل يتصور واقعاً، ؟ فيه تردد . فقيل : لو لم يتصور لم يصح الحكم بامتناع تصوره وقبل تصوره. إنما يكون على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع . ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض كذا في الشفاء (١) وله زيادة تحقيق وتفصيل أوردناها في شرح الأصول. والمرتبة الوسطى ما أمكن في نفسه لكن لم يقع متعلقاً لقدرة العبد أصلاً كخلق الجسم أو عادة كالصعود إلى السماء، وهذا هو الذي وقع النزاع في جواز التكليف به بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل والإتيان به واستحقاق العقاب على تركه لا إلى قصد التعجيز وإظهار عدم الاقتدار على الفعل كما في التحدي بمعارضة القرآن، فإنه لا خفاء في وجوب كونه مما لا يطاق . فإن قيل : تكليف الجماد ليس بأبعد من هذا الجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الحياة والعلم والقدرة . فكيف لم يقع النزاع في امتناعه حتى للقائلين بجواز تكليف الممتنع لذاته .
He said: 'Then the disputed matter is, in the first, what is possible in itself and is not subject to the slave's power at all, like creating bodies, or habitually, like ascending to the sky, not what is impossible by itself like combining opposites. For the majority hold the obligation of it impossible, based on the fact that it requires conceiving the obligated thing as real, and the impossible is only conceived via analogy (tashbīh) and negation (nafy). Nor what is impossible due to prior knowledge or information from God that it will not occur, for obligation with it occurs by agreement. Then the dispute is about permissibility (jawāz); otherwise, occurrence is negated by textual evidence and induction. And in obligation meaning the demand to realize the action and perform it and deserve punishment for omission; otherwise, the action intending incapacitation occurs by agreement. And by this it appears that the adherence of the prohibiters to something like 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not about the disputed matter, and likewise the adherence of the permiters to something like 'then bring a sūrah' and that the slave's action is not within his power, and that obligation precedes action and power accompanies it, and that whom God knows will not believe is obliged to believe despite its impossibility from him due to the impossibility of ignorance upon God. And in the speech of many verifiers, obligation of what is impossible by itself like combining opposites is permissible, indeed occurs. For example, Abū Lahab is obliged to believe in all that he brought, and among it is that he does not believe him at all, so he was obliged to believe in him that he does not believe him, which is combining opposites. And the answer that the obligated thing is only the acquisition of faith, which is possible in itself but impossible due to prior knowledge and information, or that he was only obliged to believe in other than this information, is weak. He points to refining the point of dispute according to the opinion of the verifiers among our companions, for it is narrated from some of them the permissibility of obligating the impossible, even what is impossible by itself, like making the eternal originated and vice versa, and from some that obligating what God knows will not occur, or wills that, or informs about it, is all obligation of what is not within capacity. So we say: The levels of what is not within capacity are three. The lowest is what is impossible by God's knowledge that it will not occur, or by His will for that, or by His information about that; and there is no dispute about the occurrence of obligation with it, let alone permissibility, for whoever dies upon his disbelief and whom God informed of his disbelief is considered disobedient by consensus. The highest is what is impossible by itself like reversing realities, and combining opposites or contradictions; and there is hesitation about the permissibility of obligation with it, based on the fact that it requires conceiving the obligated thing as real, and the impossible: is it conceived as real? There is hesitation about it. So it was said: If it were not conceived, then the judgment of the impossibility of its conception would not be valid, and before its conception it is only via analogy (tashbīh) by intellecting between blackness and sweetness something which is combination, then it is said such a thing cannot be between blackness and whiteness; or via negation (nafy) by the intellect judging that there cannot exist a concept which is the combination of blackness and whiteness. Thus in al-Shifā', and it has additional verification and detail which we have presented in Sharḥ al-Uṣūl. And the middle level is what is possible in itself but is not subject to the slave's power at all, like creating a body, or habitually like ascending to the sky, and this is what the dispute occurred about the permissibility of obligation with it in the sense of demanding the realization of the action and performance and deservingness of punishment for omission, not intending incapacitation and showing inability to do the action as in the challenge (taḥaddī) by opposing the Qur'an, for there is no hiddenness in that it must be something not within capacity. Then if it is said: Obligating an inanimate object is not farther than this, for God can create life, knowledge and power in it. So why did the dispute not occur about its impossibility, even for those who say the permissibility of obligating what is impossible by itself.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the disputed matter in taklīf of the impossible. The author first defines the types of impossibility: (1) what is possible in itself but not subject to the slave's power (like creating bodies), or habitually impossible (like ascending to sky); (2) what is impossible by itself (like combining opposites); (3) what is impossible due to God's prior knowledge or information that it will not occur. He notes that the majority hold the first two as impossible to obligate, while the third is agreed upon as occurring. Then he clarifies that the dispute is about permissibility (jawāz), not occurrence, and that the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not about this disputed matter. He then discusses the view of some verifiers who permit obligating what is impossible by itself, using the example of Abū Lahab. He then refines the point of dispute according to the verifiers, stating that the levels of 'what is not within capacity' (mā lā yuṭāq) are three: the lowest (impossible by God's knowledge, will, or information), the highest (impossible by itself), and the middle (possible in itself but not subject to the slave's power). He discusses the hesitation about the highest level due to the need to conceive the impossible, and explains the two ways of conceiving it: via analogy (tashbīh) and via negation (nafy). He then describes the middle level as the actual point of dispute, and ends with an objection about obligating an inanimate object.

Key Terms
المتنازع
الممتنع لذاته
الممتنع لسابق العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All impositions of the impossible are equally contested.
Correction: The lowest level—impossibility by divine knowledge—is agreed upon by all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the disputed matter in obligations of the impossible?
A) What is impossible by itself, like combining opposites.
B) What is possible in itself but not under the slave's power, like creating bodies.
C) What is impossible by prior knowledge, like God knowing it will not occur.
D) What is habitually impossible, like ascending to the sky.

Key Takeaway
The disputed matter in taklif al-muhal is what is possible in itself but not under the slave's power, not what is impossible by itself or by divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may sometimes feel that Allah's commands are too difficult. But after studying this distinction, ask yourself: have you ever mistaken something that is actually within your power (even if hard) for something impossible? What specific command do you need to stop labeling as 'impossible' and start tackling as a challenge?
?
The example of Abu Lahab shows that some are obligated even knowing they will not comply. Think about an area of your life where you have failed repeatedly. Does your past failure give you a license to stop trying, or does it make the command more urgent? What would it mean for you to obey despite knowing your own weakness?
?
When you hear the verse 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها', do you use it more to reassure yourself or to lower your expectations? This lesson shows that the verse is not about the disputed matter. Are there obligations you have been avoiding because you assume they are beyond you, when in reality they are just difficult but possible? What is one such obligation you will commit to fulfilling today?

The Three Levels of What is Beyond Capacity
The author enumerates the three levels of mā lā yuṭāq and discusses their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of mā lā yuṭāq (what is not within capacity) and the specific dispute about the middle level.

Core Idea
The three levels form a spectrum from agreed impossibility (by divine knowledge) to disputed (by power) to debated (by essence).

Building on the definition of the disputed matter, the author now refines the discussion by presenting a three-tiered classification of impossibility. He explains which level is agreed upon, which is debated, and which is uncertain, and concludes with an objection about obligating inanimate objects.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three levels
Read the Arabic passage and notice the three levels: adna (lowest), aqsa (highest), wusta (middle). Understand each level's definition.
The lowest level is impossibility by divine knowledge, will, or information; the highest is impossibility by essence; the middle is possibility in itself but not under power.
2
Understand the rulings on each level
The lowest level is agreed to occur. The highest level has hesitation about its permissibility. The middle level is the actual point of dispute.
The author notes that the middle level is the one where the dispute about permissibility occurs.
3
Examine the conceptualization of the impossible
For the highest level, the author discusses whether the impossible can be conceived. He presents two methods: via analogy (tashbīh) and via negation (nafy).
This connects to the earlier point about the impossible being conceived only through these two ways.
4
Consider the objection about inanimate objects
The passage ends with an objection: if obligating what is possible but not under power is allowed, then obligating an inanimate object (which is even further from capacity) should also be allowed, but it is not disputed. Reflect on this.
This objection highlights the complexity of the issue.

على ما هو رأي المحققين من أصحابنا، فإنه حكى عن بعضهم تجويز تكليف المحال حتى الممتنع لذاته، كجعل القديم محدثاً وبالعكس، وعن بعضهم أن تكليف ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه أو أراد ذلك أو أخبر به كلها تكليف ما لا يطاق . فنقول مراتب ما لا يطاق ثلاثة أدناها ما يمتنع بعلم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه، أو لإرادته ذلك أو لإخباره بذلك ، ولا نزاع في وقوع التكليف به فضلاً عن الجواز فإن من مات على كفره، ومن أخبر الله تعالى بعدم إيمانه يعد عاصياً إجماعاً وأقصاها ما يمتنع لذاته كقلب الحقائق ، وجمع الضدين أو النقيضين. وفي جواز التكليف به تردد بناء على أنه يستدعي تصور المكلف به ،واقعاً والممتنع هل يتصور واقعاً، ؟ فيه النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض كذا في الشفاء (١) وله زيادة تحقيق وتفصيل أوردناها في شرح الأصول. والمرتبة الوسطى ما أمكن في نفسه لكن لم يقع متعلقاً لقدرة العبد أصلاً كخلق الجسم أو عادة كالصعود إلى السماء، وهذا هو الذي وقع النزاع في جواز التكليف به بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل والإتيان به واستحقاق العقاب على تركه لا إلى قصد التعجيز وإظهار عدم الاقتدار على الفعل كما في التحدي بمعارضة القرآن، فإنه لا خفاء في وجوب كونه مما لا يطاق . فإن قيل : تكليف الجماد ليس بأبعد من هذا الجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الحياة والعلم والقدرة . فكيف لم يقع النزاع في امتناعه حتى للقائلين بجواز تكليف الممتنع لذاته .
He said: 'Then the disputed matter is, in the first, what is possible in itself and is not subject to the slave's power at all, like creating bodies, or habitually, like ascending to the sky, not what is impossible by itself like combining opposites. For the majority hold the obligation of it impossible, based on the fact that it requires conceiving the obligated thing as real, and the impossible is only conceived via analogy (tashbīh) and negation (nafy). Nor what is impossible due to prior knowledge or information from God that it will not occur, for obligation with it occurs by agreement. Then the dispute is about permissibility (jawāz); otherwise, occurrence is negated by textual evidence and induction. And in obligation meaning the demand to realize the action and perform it and deserve punishment for omission; otherwise, the action intending incapacitation occurs by agreement. And by this it appears that the adherence of the prohibiters to something like 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not about the disputed matter, and likewise the adherence of the permiters to something like 'then bring a sūrah' and that the slave's action is not within his power, and that obligation precedes action and power accompanies it, and that whom God knows will not believe is obliged to believe despite its impossibility from him due to the impossibility of ignorance upon God. And in the speech of many verifiers, obligation of what is impossible by itself like combining opposites is permissible, indeed occurs. For example, Abū Lahab is obliged to believe in all that he brought, and among it is that he does not believe him at all, so he was obliged to believe in him that he does not believe him, which is combining opposites. And the answer that the obligated thing is only the acquisition of faith, which is possible in itself but impossible due to prior knowledge and information, or that he was only obliged to believe in other than this information, is weak. He points to refining the point of dispute according to the opinion of the verifiers among our companions, for it is narrated from some of them the permissibility of obligating the impossible, even what is impossible by itself, like making the eternal originated and vice versa, and from some that obligating what God knows will not occur, or wills that, or informs about it, is all obligation of what is not within capacity. So we say: The levels of what is not within capacity are three. The lowest is what is impossible by God's knowledge that it will not occur, or by His will for that, or by His information about that; and there is no dispute about the occurrence of obligation with it, let alone permissibility, for whoever dies upon his disbelief and whom God informed of his disbelief is considered disobedient by consensus. The highest is what is impossible by itself like reversing realities, and combining opposites or contradictions; and there is hesitation about the permissibility of obligation with it, based on the fact that it requires conceiving the obligated thing as real, and the impossible: is it conceived as real? There is hesitation about it. So it was said: If it were not conceived, then the judgment of the impossibility of its conception would not be valid, and before its conception it is only via analogy (tashbīh) by intellecting between blackness and sweetness something which is combination, then it is said such a thing cannot be between blackness and whiteness; or via negation (nafy) by the intellect judging that there cannot exist a concept which is the combination of blackness and whiteness. Thus in al-Shifā', and it has additional verification and detail which we have presented in Sharḥ al-Uṣūl. And the middle level is what is possible in itself but is not subject to the slave's power at all, like creating a body, or habitually like ascending to the sky, and this is what the dispute occurred about the permissibility of obligation with it in the sense of demanding the realization of the action and performance and deservingness of punishment for omission, not intending incapacitation and showing inability to do the action as in the challenge (taḥaddī) by opposing the Qur'an, for there is no hiddenness in that it must be something not within capacity. Then if it is said: Obligating an inanimate object is not farther than this, for God can create life, knowledge and power in it. So why did the dispute not occur about its impossibility, even for those who say the permissibility of obligating what is impossible by itself.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the disputed matter in taklīf of the impossible. The author first defines the types of impossibility: (1) what is possible in itself but not subject to the slave's power (like creating bodies), or habitually impossible (like ascending to sky); (2) what is impossible by itself (like combining opposites); (3) what is impossible due to God's prior knowledge or information that it will not occur. He notes that the majority hold the first two as impossible to obligate, while the third is agreed upon as occurring. Then he clarifies that the dispute is about permissibility (jawāz), not occurrence, and that the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not about this disputed matter. He then discusses the view of some verifiers who permit obligating what is impossible by itself, using the example of Abū Lahab. He then refines the point of dispute according to the verifiers, stating that the levels of 'what is not within capacity' (mā lā yuṭāq) are three: the lowest (impossible by God's knowledge, will, or information), the highest (impossible by itself), and the middle (possible in itself but not subject to the slave's power). He discusses the hesitation about the highest level due to the need to conceive the impossible, and explains the two ways of conceiving it: via analogy (tashbīh) and via negation (nafy). He then describes the middle level as the actual point of dispute, and ends with an objection about obligating an inanimate object.

Key Terms
ما لا يطاق
المرتبة الوسطى
التشبيه
النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three levels of impossibility are similarly debated.
Correction: Only the middle level is the actual point of dispute; the lowest is agreed, the highest is uncertain.

Checkpoint
What is the middle level of mā lā yuṭāq?
A) Impossible by divine knowledge, will, or information.
B) Impossible by essence, like reversing realities.
C) Possible in itself but not under the slave's power, like creating a body.
D) Habitually impossible, like ascending to the sky.

Key Takeaway
The dispute in taklif al-muhal centers on the middle level: what is possible in itself but not under the slave's power, while the highest level remains uncertain due to the difficulty of conceptualizing the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the lowest level—that someone can die in disbelief despite God's knowledge and will—does it make you examine your own certainty of faith? How does that possibility affect the way you cling to belief today?
?
The author mentions hesitation about obligating the impossible by itself because it may be inconceivable. Are there any commands of Allah that you find conceptually impossible to reconcile with your reason? How do you handle that tension without falling into despair or denial?
?
The middle level involves what is possible but beyond your power, like creating a body. Are there obligations in your life that you have avoided, claiming they are beyond your capacity? How does the distinction between demanding the act and showing inability (ta'jīz) challenge your excuses?

Mu'tazilite View and Three Proofs on Purpose of Taklif
Presents the Mu'tazilite position and three proofs on the purpose of taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that the purpose of taklif is exposure to reward, and the three proofs they offer.

Core Idea
Taklif's purpose is exposure to reward, not arbitrary command.

This passage presents the Mu'tazilite perspective on why God imposes obligations (taklif). They argue that it must be for a rational purpose: to expose humans to reward through merit earned by enduring hardships. They support this with three proofs: scripture, consensus, and logical reasoning. Let's examine their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the Mu'tazilite argument come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the main claim and three proofs
The passage states the Mu'tazilite claim and then lists three proofs. Find each proof and note how it supports the claim.
The third proof is a logical argument that uses the concept of justice.
3
Examine the logical proof in detail
Focus on the third proof: it argues that imposing hardships without merit would be unjust, so the only good-making aspect is exposure to benefit (reward). Trace the reasoning step by step.
4
Reflect on the role of merit (istihqaq)
Notice how merit earned through hardships is central: it makes reward deserved and thus the whole taklif process just.
This contrasts with other views that might see reward as pure grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال [خاتمة] ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الغرض من التكليف هو التعريض للثواب فإنه لا يحسن بدون الاستحقاق الحاصل بالمشاق ويدل عليه وجوه : فتعين . الأول : مثل قوله تعالى ومن يطع الله ورسوله يدخله جنات الآية . الثاني : أنه لا غرض سواه إجماعاً لأنهم لا يثبتون الغرض ونحن ننفي غيره . الثالث : أن التكليف بالمشاق إضرار وهو بدون استحقاق، ولا منفعة ظلم فيكون التعريض للمنفعة هو الجهة المحسنة
He said [Conclusion]: The Mu'tazila held that the purpose of responsibility (taklif) is the exposure to reward, for it is not good without the merit (istihqaq) obtained through hardships. And there are proofs for it: thus it is established. First: like His statement, Exalted is He, 'And whoever obeys Allah and His Messenger, He will admit him to gardens' [verse]. Second: that there is no purpose other than it by consensus, because they do not affirm any purpose and we deny any other. Third: that imposing hardships as responsibility is harmful, and it without merit, and no benefit, is injustice, so the exposure to benefit is the good-making aspect.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite claim regarding the purpose of divine obligation (taklif). They argue that the sole purpose is to expose humans to reward (al-ta'rid li al-thawab), because reward is not good without prior merit (istihqaq) that comes from enduring hardships. They then support this with three proofs: (1) Scriptural evidence: the Quran promises paradise to those who obey, implying that obedience merits reward. (2) Consensus: there is no other purpose recognized by opponents, as they either deny purpose altogether or affirm only this one. (3) Logical reasoning: imposing hardship without merit and without benefit would be unjust (zulm), so the only way to make it good is to view it as exposure to benefit. This sets up the foundation for the subsequent refutation.

Key Terms
الغرض
التكليف
التعريض للثواب
الاستحقاق
المشاق
الجهة المحسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite view denies God's grace; they instead base justice on rational merit.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites do not deny grace; they argue that taklif must be rationally justifiable, and the exposure to reward through earned merit is that justification.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite view presented, what makes the imposition of hardships (taklif) good?
A) The hardship itself is good.
B) The exposure to reward that it enables.
C) It is good by divine command regardless.
D) It is good because it leads to punishment for sinners.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that taklif's purpose is exposure to reward, supported by scripture, consensus, and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship in obeying Allah, do you see it as pointless suffering or as a chance to earn reward? How does this lesson challenge you to change your perspective on your daily struggles in worship?
?
If you truly believed that every act of obedience is an exposure to reward, would your intention (niyya) in praying, fasting, or giving charity change? What specific intention would you cultivate starting today?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue that without merit, hardship would be unjust. When you feel that Allah's commands are burdensome, are you accepting that He is unjust? How can you use this lesson to silence that whisper and renew your trust in His wisdom?

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Proofs: Reward as Favor, Not Necessity
Refutes three Mu'tazilite proofs by challenging premises and conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni theologians refuted Mu'tazilite arguments that actions necessarily merit reward and that divine actions must have a knowable purpose.

Core Idea
Reward is Allah's favor, not a necessary effect of deeds.

The Mu'tazilites argued that human actions must result in reward or punishment and that God's actions must have a purpose known to reason. This passage presents a threefold refutation: reward is a gratuitous favor, the consensus on purpose is denied, and the necessity of purpose itself is unproven. Let's examine each point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the refutational tone and the three distinct counterarguments.
The passage begins with 'Wa rudda bi-anna...' (It was refuted by saying...).
2
Identify the three refutation points
List the three ways the author challenges the Mu'tazilite proofs. Look for: (1) reward as favor, (2) denial of consensus on purpose, (3) challenge to the principle of necessity of purpose.
Each point begins with a key phrase: 'قد يكون فضلاً', 'ولا نسلم', 'ولو سلم'.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the Mu'tazilite position is problematic from the Sunni perspective. How does the refutation undermine their claim that reward is a necessary effect?
Think about the implications for divine justice and human responsibility.
4
Explore alternative purposes
The author lists several alternative purposes for taklif: trial (ibtilā’), gratitude (shukr), preservation of order (hifẓ al-niẓām), and something beyond reason. Discuss why these are significant.
These alternatives show that the Mu'tazilite assumption of a single purpose is not universally accepted.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasped the key point of the refutation.
The checkpoint focuses on the alternative purposes mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بأن المترتب قد يكون فضلاً من الله تعالى لا أثراً لما ترتب عليه، وكيف يعقل استحقاق النعيم الدائم بمجرد كلمة وتصديق فيمن آمن فمات. ولا نسلم الإجماع على أنه لا غرض سواه، فقيل الابتلاء، وقيل الشكر، وقيل حفظ النظام، وقيل أمر لا طريق إليه للعقل . ولو سلم فلا يفيد كونه الغرض إلا بعد ثبوت لزوم الغرض ولم يثبت .
It was refuted by [saying] that the resultant may be a favor (fadl) from Allah, not an effect (athar) of what it is based on, and how is it conceivable that eternal bliss is merited by just a word and affirmation from one who believed and died? And we do not accept the consensus that there is no purpose other than it; for it is said: [it is] trial (ibtilā’), and it is said: gratitude (shukr), and it is said: preservation of order (hifẓ al-niẓām), and it is said: an affair to which reason has no path. And even if it is accepted, it does not establish it as the purpose except after proving the necessity of a purpose, which has not been proven.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (rad) of the three Mu'tazilite proofs presented earlier. The author makes three key points: (1) The reward that results from obedience may be a gratuitous favor (fadl) from Allah, not a necessary effect (athar) of the actions; it is inconceivable that eternal bliss is earned merely by uttering a word of faith. (2) The claimed consensus that there is no other purpose for taklif is not accepted; other purposes have been proposed, such as trial (ibtilā’), gratitude (shukr), preservation of social order (hifẓ al-niẓām), or something beyond reason. (3) Even if the consensus were granted, it would not prove that the purpose is specifically exposure to reward unless the principle that actions must have a purpose (luzūm al-gharaḍ) is established—and that principle itself is unproven. Thus the Mu'tazilite conclusion is undermined.

Key Terms
الفضل
الأثر
الابتلاء
الشكر
حفظ النظام
لزوم الغرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies any divine purpose for taklif.
Correction: The refutation merely challenges the Mu'tazilite reasoning and the necessity of a known purpose. It does not deny that there is a purpose, but rather argues that the purpose is not necessarily known or limited to reward.

Checkpoint
What is one of the alternative purposes for taklif mentioned in the refutation?
A) Attaining worldly power.
B) Trial (ibtilā’).
C) Punishment for sin.
D) Increasing the number of believers.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that reward is a divine favor, not a necessary consequence, and that the necessity of a purpose for divine actions is not rationally provable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your good deeds 'earn' you a place in Paradise, as if Allah owes you? How does recognizing that reward is pure favor (fadl) shatter that pride? What can you do today to live in gratitude rather than entitlement?
?
If reward is not a necessary consequence of obedience, then why do you still obey? Are you serving Allah out of love and hope in His generosity, or out of a transactional mindset? Which of your actions this week was most motivated by a sense of entitlement?
?
The refutation challenges the idea that we can rationally prove Allah must have a purpose for His actions. How comfortable are you with not fully understanding why Allah does what He does? What does this teach you about submission (taslīm) to His will beyond your intellect? Will you surrender a specific worry today that you've been trying to rationalize?

Reward as Purpose for All, Including Sinners
Defines reward and establishes merit from actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even for those who die in disbelief or sin, the purpose of divine obligation is exposure to reward, defined as eternal pleasures and exaltation, and that difficult actions establish merit.

Core Idea
Even for sinners, the purpose is reward—pure eternal pleasure.

This lesson examines the author's reaffirmation that the purpose of obligation remains exposure to reward, even for those who forfeit it. He defines reward as eternal, pure pleasures and exaltation, and argues that difficult acts and abstentions establish merit, citing Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own words introduce the concept.
Notice the definition of reward: 'many eternal pleasures pure of sorrow and exaltation'.
2
Understand the definition of reward
The author defines 'exposure to reward' as eternal benefits without sorrow and exaltation. Note that this reward requires merit (istihqaq).
3
Identify the role of difficult actions
The passage asserts that difficult actions (af'al) and abstentions (tark) have a real effect in establishing merit. This is supported by Quranic verses.
Look at the verses cited: Q48:29 and Q16:97.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بالنسبة إلى من مات على الكفر أو الفسق هو التعريض للثواب أعني منافع كثيرة دائمة خالصة السرور والتعظيم، فإن ذلك لا يحسن بدون الاستحقاق مع ولاخفاء في أن للأفعال والتروك الشاقة تأثيراً في إثبات الاستحقاق بشهادة الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على ترتب الثواب . استحقاق التعظيم على تلك الأفعال والتروك ، ومن يطع الله ورسوله يدخله جنات تجري من تحتها الأنهار ثواباً من عند الله ) ومن عمل صالحاً من ذكر أو أنثى وهو مؤمن فلنحيينه حياة طيبة ولنجزينهم أجرهم بأحسن ما كانوا يعملون إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى وبدلالة العقول. أما أولاً: فلأن الخالي عن الغرض عبث لا يصدر عن الحكيم أفحسبتم أنما خلقناكم عبثاً ولا غرض سوى ذلك إجماعاً لأنا لا نثبت غيره، والمخالف لا يثبت الغرض أصلاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن العبث على أمر شاق بطريق الاستعلاء بحيث لو خولف ترتب عليه العقاب إضرار وإضرار غير المستحق لا لمنفعة ظلم يستحيل على الله تعالى، فالتعريض لتلك المنافع ، والتمكين من اكتساب السعادة الأبدية هي الجهة المحسنة للتكليف ولا يبطل حسنه بتفويت الكافر والفاسق ذلك على نفسه بسوء اختياره .
And as for one who dies upon disbelief or sin, it is the exposure to reward, meaning many eternal pleasures pure of sorrow and exaltation; for that is not good without merit, and there is no hiddenness in the fact that difficult actions and abstentions have an effect in establishing merit, as testified by the verses and hadiths indicating the consequent reward, merit of exaltation upon those actions and abstentions. 'And whoever obeys Allah and His Messenger, He will admit him to gardens beneath which rivers flow, as a reward from Allah.' 'Whoever does righteousness, male or female, while being a believer, We will surely grant him a good life and We will surely reward them according to the best of what they used to do.' And other things without number, and by the indication of intellects. As for first: because that which is devoid of purpose is frivolous ('abath) and does not issue from the Wise. 'Did you think that We created you in vain?' And there is no purpose other than that by consensus, for we do not affirm any other, and the opponent does not affirm any purpose at all. As for second: because frivolousness in a difficult matter in a way of domination such that if disobeyed, punishment follows, is harmful, and harming one who does not deserve it without benefit is injustice, which is impossible for Allah. So the exposure to those benefits and the enabling to acquire eternal happiness is the good-making aspect of obligation (taklif), and its goodness is not nullified by the disbeliever and sinner forfeiting that upon themselves by their bad choice.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by focusing on the case of those who die in disbelief or sin. The author reaffirms that the purpose remains exposure to reward (al-ta'rid li al-thawab), defined as eternal pure pleasures and exaltation. He emphasizes that difficult actions (af'al) and abstentions (tark) have a real effect in establishing merit (istihqaq), citing Quranic verses and rational proof. Then he presents two independent arguments: (1) An act without purpose is frivolous ('abath) and cannot be attributed to the Wise; the Quran says 'Did you think We created you in vain?', and there is consensus that there is no other purpose. (2) Imposing a difficult command with threats of punishment for disobedience would be harmful and unjust if no benefit is intended; but since God is just, the only good-making aspect is the exposure to benefits and the opportunity to acquire eternal happiness. The forfeiture of this by sinners through their own choice does not nullify the goodness of the obligation itself.

Correction: He is presenting his own positive argument for a similar conclusion via different premises, not contradicting.

A) Great effort
B) Merit (istihqaq)
C) Faith only
D) God's arbitrary will

Key Takeaway
Reward is eternal pure pleasure that requires merit, and difficult actions are a means to establish that merit.

?

Two Rational Proofs for the Necessity of Purpose
Presents two rational arguments for purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments that prove the purpose of obligation cannot be frivolous or unjust, but must be exposure to reward and enabling eternal happiness.

Core Idea
Without purpose, action is frivolous; with threat of harm, it is unjust—so purpose must be reward.

Here the author presents two independent rational proofs to establish that the purpose of obligation is exposure to reward. The first argument uses the principle that an act without purpose is frivolous, which cannot be attributed to the Wise. The second argues that imposing difficult commands with threat of punishment without benefit would be unjust, which is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for each proof
Read the Arabic passage, noting the two proofs introduced by 'Awwalan' and 'Thaniyan'. Then read the translation.
The first proof is about frivolousness; the second about injustice.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first proof: An act devoid of purpose is frivolous ('abath). The Wise does not do frivolous acts. The Quran says 'Did you think We created you in vain?' So obligation must have a purpose.
The author claims consensus that no other purpose is affirmed.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof: Imposing a difficult command with threat of punishment for disobedience would be harmful. Harming someone undeserving without benefit is injustice. Since God is just, the only good-making aspect is the exposure to benefits and enabling eternal happiness.
The siner's bad choice does not nullify the goodness of the obligation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بالنسبة إلى من مات على الكفر أو الفسق هو التعريض للثواب أعني منافع كثيرة دائمة خالصة السرور والتعظيم، فإن ذلك لا يحسن بدون الاستحقاق مع ولاخفاء في أن للأفعال والتروك الشاقة تأثيراً في إثبات الاستحقاق بشهادة الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على ترتب الثواب . استحقاق التعظيم على تلك الأفعال والتروك ، ومن يطع الله ورسوله يدخله جنات تجري من تحتها الأنهار ثواباً من عند الله ) ومن عمل صالحاً من ذكر أو أنثى وهو مؤمن فلنحيينه حياة طيبة ولنجزينهم أجرهم بأحسن ما كانوا يعملون إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى وبدلالة العقول. أما أولاً: فلأن الخالي عن الغرض عبث لا يصدر عن الحكيم أفحسبتم أنما خلقناكم عبثاً ولا غرض سوى ذلك إجماعاً لأنا لا نثبت غيره، والمخالف لا يثبت الغرض أصلاً. وأما ثانياً: فلأن العبث على أمر شاق بطريق الاستعلاء بحيث لو خولف ترتب عليه العقاب إضرار وإضرار غير المستحق لا لمنفعة ظلم يستحيل على الله تعالى، فالتعريض لتلك المنافع ، والتمكين من اكتساب السعادة الأبدية هي الجهة المحسنة للتكليف ولا يبطل حسنه بتفويت الكافر والفاسق ذلك على نفسه بسوء اختياره .
And as for one who dies upon disbelief or sin, it is the exposure to reward, meaning many eternal pleasures pure of sorrow and exaltation; for that is not good without merit, and there is no hiddenness in the fact that difficult actions and abstentions have an effect in establishing merit, as testified by the verses and hadiths indicating the consequent reward, merit of exaltation upon those actions and abstentions. 'And whoever obeys Allah and His Messenger, He will admit him to gardens beneath which rivers flow, as a reward from Allah.' 'Whoever does righteousness, male or female, while being a believer, We will surely grant him a good life and We will surely reward them according to the best of what they used to do.' And other things without number, and by the indication of intellects. As for first: because that which is devoid of purpose is frivolous ('abath) and does not issue from the Wise. 'Did you think that We created you in vain?' And there is no purpose other than that by consensus, for we do not affirm any other, and the opponent does not affirm any purpose at all. As for second: because frivolousness in a difficult matter in a way of domination such that if disobeyed, punishment follows, is harmful, and harming one who does not deserve it without benefit is injustice, which is impossible for Allah. So the exposure to those benefits and the enabling to acquire eternal happiness is the good-making aspect of obligation (taklif), and its goodness is not nullified by the disbeliever and sinner forfeiting that upon themselves by their bad choice.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by focusing on the case of those who die in disbelief or sin. The author reaffirms that the purpose remains exposure to reward (al-ta'rid li al-thawab), defined as eternal pure pleasures and exaltation. He emphasizes that difficult actions (af'al) and abstentions (tark) have a real effect in establishing merit (istihqaq), citing Quranic verses and rational proof. Then he presents two independent arguments: (1) An act without purpose is frivolous ('abath) and cannot be attributed to the Wise; the Quran says 'Did you think We created you in vain?', and there is consensus that there is no other purpose. (2) Imposing a difficult command with threats of punishment for disobedience would be harmful and unjust if no benefit is intended; but since God is just, the only good-making aspect is the exposure to benefits and the opportunity to acquire eternal happiness. The forfeiture of this by sinners through their own choice does not nullify the goodness of the obligation itself.

العبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the purpose of obligation is only for the obedient and not for sinners.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that even for those who die in disbelief or sin, the purpose remains exposure to reward.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would imposing a difficult command with threat of punishment without benefit be?
A) A test of faith
B) Frivolousness
C) Injustice (zulm)
D) Mercy

Key Takeaway
Two rational arguments prove obligation must have a purpose: avoidance of frivolousness and avoidance of injustice, leading to exposure to reward as the good-making aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every command from Allah is actually an opportunity for you to gain eternal happiness, why do you often feel burdened by obligations like prayer or fasting? Will you today consciously treat a difficult act of worship as a chance to acquire eternal happiness?
?
The passage says that the harm of punishment comes only from your own bad choice (سوء اختياره). In what area of your life have you been blaming Allah or others for consequences that are actually the result of your own decisions? What will you do to take responsibility starting now?
?
Knowing that Allah cannot be unjust, and that every command has a purpose for your ultimate good, how does this change your reaction when you face a ruling you find difficult or inconvenient? Will you commit to trust His wisdom even when you do not see the benefit immediately?
